The Objection from Dimensions: A Strong Challenge
The strong objection from dimension interpenetration.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the dilemma of boundaries leads to a powerful objection concerning the interpenetration of dimensions.

Core Idea
If two lines can interpenetrate without sum increase, why not two dimensions?

The previous lesson left one sub-case: if one boundary completely vanishes into the other without increasing total quantity, then we have two quantities coinciding. The author now draws a striking analogy: if this is possible for lines, why can't it happen for the dimension of a body and the dimension of place? This forms a strong objection against the Aristotelian framework, though an answer is promised later.

Guided Path
1
Recall the sub-case where quantity does not increase
From the previous lesson, if one line completely penetrates the other without a net increase, we have two distinct lines occupying the same extension.
2
Extend the analogy to dimensions
If two lines can coincide without sum change, then why can't the dimension of a located body and the dimension of its place interpenetrate? This would allow two dimensions in one location.
The author calls this a strong objection.
3
Understand the response pending
The author notes that the Aristotelians have an excuse, to be given later. This shows the depth of the problem.
For now, note that the definition of contact leads to a metaphysical dilemma.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أرسطاطاليس، فإنهم قالوا: "المتماسان هما اللذان طرفاهما معاً في الوضع، أي في الإشارة الحسية." قال الشيخ في الشفاء: "والمتماسان هما اللذان تقع الإشارة الحسية على طرفيهما معاً." فأقول: لنفرض سطحاً ماس سطحاً، فههنا يلزم أن يكون طرفاهما معاً، يعني: الخطان اللذان هما نهايتهما يكونان معاً في الوضع. فنقول: هذان الخطان إما أن يكونا باقيين حال حصول هذه المماسة أو لا يكونان باقيين. فإن لم يبقيا بل حدث خط واحد يكون هو بعينه مشتركاً فيه بين ذينك السطحين، فهذا يكون اتصالاً لا مماسة. وقد فرضنا أن الحاصل هو المماسة لا الاتصال. وأيضاً فقول الشيخ: "المتماسان هما اللذان طرفاهما معاً في الوضع"، يقتضي أن يكون طرفاهما باقيين حال التماس، وذلك يمنع من القول بأنهما عدماً وحصل طرف واحد مشترك فيه بين السطحين. وأما القسم الثاني: وهو أن يقال: الطرفان باقيان. فإما أن يقال: نفد أحدهما بالكلية في الآخر أو لم ينفد. فإن لم ينفد أحدهما في الآخر كان أحدهما مبايناً عن الآخر، فيلزم أن لا تكون الإشارة إلى أحدهما عين الإشارة إلى الآخر، مع أنا قد فرضناهما متماسين، هذا خلف. وأما إن قلنا: إن أحد هذين الخطين قد نفد بكليته في كلية الخط الآخر، فإما أن يكون مقدار هذا المجموع أزيد من مقدار الواحد أو لا يكون. فإن كان الأول فحينئذ لا تكون كلية أحد هذين الخطين نافدة في كلية الخط الآخر، وقد فرضنا الأمر كذلك، وهذا خلف. وإن كان الثاني فقد حصل ههنا مقداران ونفد أحدهما في كلية الآخر، مع أنه لم يصر مقدار المجموع أزيد من مقدار الواحد، وإذا عقلنا ذلك في هذه الصورة، فلم لا يجوز مثله في بعد المتمكن وبعد المكان؟ وهذا سؤال قوي على قانون قولهم. ولهم عنه عذر سيأتي ذكره بعد ذلك مع الجواب، إن شاء الله تعالى.
Aristotle, for they said: "The two things in contact are those whose two ends are together in position, i.e., in sensible indication." The Shaykh said in al-Shifa: "And the two things in contact are those upon whose two ends the sensible indication falls together." So I say: let us suppose a surface touching a surface. Here it is necessary that their two ends be together, meaning: the two lines that are their boundaries are together in position. So we say: these two lines either remain during the occurrence of this contact or they do not remain. If they do not remain but a single line occurs that is itself shared between those two surfaces, then this would be a union (ittiṣāl), not a contact (tamās). And we have supposed that what occurs is contact not union. Also, the Shaykh's statement: "The two things in contact are those whose two ends are together in position" necessitates that their two ends remain during contact, and that prevents saying that they are annihilated and a single end shared between the two surfaces occurs. As for the second division: that it is said: the two ends remain. Then it is either said: one of them completely vanishes (nafada) in the other or it does not vanish. For if one of them does not vanish in the other, then one of them is separate from the other, and it follows that the indication of one of them is not identical to the indication of the other, although we have supposed them to be in contact. This is a contradiction. And if we say that one of these two lines has completely vanished in the entirety of the other line, then either the quantity of this sum is greater than the quantity of the one or not. If it is the first, then the entirety of one of these two lines is not penetrating (nāfiḍa) in the entirety of the other line, and we have supposed it to be so, and this is a contradiction. And if it is the second, then there have occurred here two quantities and one has vanished in the entirety of the other, while the quantity of the sum has not become greater than the quantity of the one. And if we conceive that in this form, then why is its like not permissible in the dimension of the located (bu'd al-mutamakkin) and the dimension of place (bu'd al-makān)? And this is a strong objection to the rule of their statement. And they have an excuse for it that will be mentioned later with the answer, God willing.
Source pages p. 125

Commentary
This passage is a logical analysis of the definition of contact (tamās). It begins by quoting Aristotle and Ibn Sina's definition: two things in contact have their ends together in position, i.e., they are perceived together by the senses. The author then sets up a thought experiment: suppose one surface touches another. The boundaries (lines) must be together. He then considers two possibilities: either the boundaries remain during contact, or they do not. If they do not remain, a single line shared between both surfaces appears, which would be union (ittiṣāl), not contact. Since we assumed contact, this is rejected. Moreover, the definition requires the ends to remain. The second possibility: the ends remain. Now a further sub-division: either one boundary completely vanishes (nafada) in the other or not. If it does not vanish, the ends remain separate, so the sensible indication of each is distinct, contradicting their being together in sensation. If one does vanish in the other, then either the total quantity becomes greater or not. If greater, then the entirety of one line is not fully penetrating the other, contradicting the assumption of complete vanishing. If not greater, then we have two quantities with one vanished into the other but total quantity unchanged. The author then asks why this same reasoning cannot apply to the dimension of the located body and the dimension of space, implying that two dimensions might interpenetrate without increase. This is a strong objection to the Aristotelian position, though they have an excuse to be given later. The passage proceeds step by step, using conditional reasoning and showing contradictions (khalaf) to narrow down the possibilities.

Key Terms
بعد (bu'd)
بعد المتمكن (bu'd al-mutamakkin)
بعد المكان (bu'd al-makān)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The objection is easily answered; the passage shows it is strong.
Correction: The author explicitly calls it a 'strong objection' and notes that an answer will come later, indicating it is a serious challenge.

Checkpoint
What is the analogy used by the author to challenge the Aristotelian definition of contact?
A) Contact between two surfaces is like union.
B) If two lines can interpenetrate without size increase, why can't two dimensions?
C) Contact requires boundaries to vanish.
D) The definition of contact is from Aristotle.

Key Takeaway
The dilemma of boundaries forces a consideration of interpenetrating dimensions, a powerful challenge to Aristotelian physics.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The author acknowledges the strength of this objection but postpones the answer. When you face a compelling challenge to your beliefs, do you honestly confront it or do you defer it? What would it take for you to truly wrestle with a difficult question instead of setting it aside?
?
The argument relies on an analogy: if it works for lines, why not for dimensions? In your own life, are there analogies you draw that might be flawed? Reflect on a time you assumed that what applied in one situation must apply in another. How can you test the validity of your analogies?
?
This passage challenges the assumption that two things cannot occupy the same space. What assumptions do you hold so deeply that you have never thought to question them? Pick one such assumption today and commit to examining its foundation.

Why Interpenetrating Dimensions Cannot Be Distinguished
The author proves the impossibility of distinguishing two interpenetrating dimensions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical proof that two interpenetrating dimensions cannot be distinguished by essence, accidents, or concomitants, and why the claim of their distinction is false.

Core Idea
Interpenetrating dimensions share the same essence, so nothing can set them apart.

This lesson unpacks a dense philosophical proof about dimensions and distinctness. The author argues that when two dimensions fully interpenetrate, they become indistinguishable because any potential distinguisher—essence, accident, or necessary concomitant—would apply equally to both. We will walk through this reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
The passage is a single continuous proof with two main parts.
2
Understand the first argument: against distinction by accidents
Identify why accidents cannot distinguish two equal-in-essence dimensions. The author argues that if essence is identical, any accident of one must also be an accident of the other, so it cannot be a differentiator.
Focus on the logic: equality in essence forces shared accidents.
3
Understand the second argument: against the vanishing of distinction
Consider the alternative that after interpenetration, no distinction remains. The author shows this leads to the absurdity that the dimensions would not be different, which violates the principle that different things must be distinguished by something.
The threefold classification (essence, concomitant, accident) reappears here.
4
Reflect on the overall conclusion
Combine both arguments to see why the author concludes that the entire notion of interpenetrating dimensions is false and corrupt.
The author calls this the best argument in this topic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإنما قلنا : إنه يمتنع حصول الامتياز بالعوارض ، وذلك لأن هذين البعدين لما نفدت كلية أحدهما في كلية الآخر ، وكان كل واحد منهما مساوياً للآخر في تمام الماهية لماهية الآخر فكل عارض يفرض كونه عارضاً لأحدهما ، فإنه لا بد وأن يكون ممكن العروض للآخر ، لكونها متساويين في تمام الماهية . وأيضاً : فكل عارض يفرض كونه عارضاً لأحدهما فإن نسبته إلى أحدهما كنسبته إلى الآخر ، لكون كل واحد منهما مساوياً للآخر في تمام الماهية ، ولكون كل واحد منهما مساوياً بتمامه في تمام الآخر . وإذا كان كذلك فكل عارض يفترض كونه عارضاً لأحدهما فهو بعينه يكون عارضاً للآخر . وإذا كان كذلك فحينئذ يكون ذلك العارض مشتركاً بينهما ، وكل ما كان مشتركاً بين شيئين ، فإنه يمتنع كونه سبباً لامتياز أحدهما عن الآخر . فيثبت أنه لو امتاز بعد المتمكن عن بعد المكان ، لكان ذلك الامتياز إما بالماهية أو بلوازمها أو بعوارضها . وثبت أن الكل محال ، فبطل القول بحصول الامتياز بين هذين البعدين المتداخلين وأما القسم الثاني وهو أن يقال : المتمكن إذا حصل في المكان ، ونفد بعده في بعد المكان ، فإنه لا يبقى أحد البعدين ممتازاً عن الثاني . فنقول : هذا أيضاً باطل . لان بتقدير أن لا يبقى الامتياز ، وجب أن لا يحصل التغاير ، لأن كل غيرين فلا بد وأن يمتاز كل واحد منهما عن الآخر ، بكونه هو هو ، وذلك الامتياز في أمر ما ، وفي مفهوم ما ، وحينئذ يعود التقسيم المذكور في أن ذلك الأمر إما الماهية أو لوازمها أو عوارضها ، والكل قد أبطلناه . فيثبت : أنه لو ارتفع الامتياز لارتفع التغاير ، وحينئذ يلزم إما القول بعدم أحد ذينك البعدين ، أو القول بحصولهما مع القول باتحادهما ، وكل ذلك مما قد أبطلناه فيثبت أن نفود بعد المتمكن في بعد المكان : قول باطل ، ومذهب فاسد . وهذا الوجه أحسن الوجوه التي يمكن ذكرها في هذا الباب
And we say: it is impossible for distinction to occur through accidents. This is because when the whole of one of these two dimensions pervades the whole of the other, and each one is equal to the other in complete essence, then every accident that is supposed to be an accident of one must be possible of occurrence for the other, because they are equal in complete essence. Also: every accident that is supposed to be an accident of one, its relation to one is like its relation to the other, because each one is equal to the other in complete essence, and because each one is wholly equal in the other. If that is so, then every accident that is supposed to be an accident of one is the very same accident of the other. If that is so, then that accident is common between them, and whatever is common between two things cannot be a cause of distinction of one from the other. Thus it is established that if the dimension of the occupant were distinguished from the dimension of place, that distinction would be either by essence, or its necessary concomitants, or its accidents. And it has been established that all are impossible, so the claim that distinction occurs between these two interpenetrating dimensions is false. As for the second division: that it is said: When the occupant is in place and its dimension pervades the dimension of place, then one of the two dimensions does not remain distinguished from the second. We say: this is also false. Because on the assumption that distinction does not remain, then difference must not occur, because any two different things must each be distinguished from the other by being itself, and that distinction is in some matter and in some concept. Then the mentioned division returns: that matter is either essence, its necessary concomitants, or its accidents, all of which we have refuted. Thus it is established: if distinction were removed, difference would be removed, and then it would necessitate either the non-existence of one of the two dimensions, or their existence with the claim of their unity, all of which we have refuted. Thus it is established that the pervasion of the dimension of the occupant in the dimension of place is a false statement and a corrupt doctrine. And this is the best way that can be mentioned in this chapter.
Source pages p. 129

Commentary
This passage is a proof. The author argues that two interpenetrating dimensions—specifically, the dimension of the occupant (al-mutamakkin) and the dimension of place (al-makān)—cannot be distinguished from one another. The proof proceeds in two parts. First, the author shows that distinction cannot arise from accidents. The reasoning: when two dimensions are equal in complete essence (tamām al-māhiyya), any accident that is supposed to occur to one must be equally possible for the other, and in fact must occur to both because the relation of the accident to each is the same. Therefore the accident is common to both, and a common thing cannot be a cause of distinction. So distinction by accident is impossible. Second, the author considers the possibility that after interpenetration, distinction simply vanishes. He refutes this by noting that if no distinction remains, then the two dimensions would not be different (mutaghāyir), but then we fall back into the same threefold classification (essence, necessary concomitants, accidents) which has already been refuted. Hence the claim that the occupant's dimension pervades place is false. The author concludes that this is the best argument in this topic.

Key Terms
البعد (al-bu'd) – dimension
المتمكن (al-mutamakkin) – the occupant
المكان (al-makān) – place
الماهية (al-māhiyya) – essence
العوارض (al-'awāriḍ) – accidents
اللوازم (al-lawāzim) – necessary concomitants
الامتياز (al-imtiyāz) – distinction
التغاير (al-taghāyur) – difference

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the argument assumes dimensions are numerically one, but it only assumes equality in essence, not identity.
Correction: The author's premise is equality in complete essence, not numerical identity. The argument works because even if they are distinct in number, their essential properties are identical.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is it impossible for two interpenetrating dimensions to be distinguished by an accident?
A) Because accidents cannot occur to dimensions.
B) Because any accident occurring to one must equally occur to the other due to their equal essence, making it common and thus not a distinguisher.
C) Because dimensions have no accidents.
D) Because the accident would be unique to each dimension.

Key Takeaway
Two interpenetrating dimensions cannot be distinguished because any potential distinguisher—essence, necessary concomitant, or accident—applies equally to both, making the distinction impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your relationships, do you ever treat people as if they are fundamentally different from you in essence, when in reality you share the same humanity? How might this false distinction prevent you from true connection? What would it take to see the essence you share rather than the accidental differences?
?
The author argues that if two things are equal in essence, nothing can distinguish them. Is there a belief or habit you hold onto that you treat as separate from your core identity, yet it truly defines you? Can you identify a false distinction between your 'essence' and your 'accidents'? What would change if you removed that distinction?

Sharing in Essence Does Not Entail Sharing in Accidents
Author defends essential identity of dimensions using black/white analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that two things sharing a common essence does not require them to share all accidental properties, as illustrated by the black-and-white analogy.

Core Idea
Sharing essence is not sharing all accidents.

The author defends his claim that dimensions share the same essence despite having different concepts. He uses the analogy of black and white sharing in 'lowniyya' to argue that a common genus does not imply common accidental properties. This lesson unpacks that argument and clarifies the logical point.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the complete Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the English translation to grasp the flow of the argument.
The passage is dense; let the author's own reasoning come through first.
2
Identify the analogy and its logic
Find the example of blackness and whiteness. Notice that they share 'lowniyya' (color-ness) but differ in specific color. Ask: what does this show about dimensions?
The analogy is used to argue that sharing an essence does not force all properties to be shared.
3
Understand the defensive nature
Recognize that the author is answering an objection: the different 'concept' of dimension does not invalidate sharing essence. The analogy reinforces that conceptual difference is compatible with essential identity.
This passage is part of a larger proof; it anticipates a counterargument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولما كان ذلك المفهوم شيئاً مغايراً لطبيعة البعد والامتداد ، لم يقدح ذلك في قولنا : إن الأبعاد من حيث إنها هي أبعاد متشاركة في تمام الماهية ، وحينئذ يتم الدليل الذي ذكرناه لأنا نقول : السواد والبياض يتشاركان في اللونية ، ويتباينان بخصوص كونه سواداً أو بياضاً ، ثم إن هذا لا يوجب أن يصح على لونية السواد ، ما يصح على لونية البياض حتى يلزم صحة أن ينقلب السواد بياضاً وبالعكس فكذا ههنا
And since that concept is something different from the nature of dimension and extension, that does not invalidate our statement: that dimensions, insofar as they are dimensions, share in the complete essence, and then the proof we mentioned is established. For we say: blackness and whiteness share in color-ness, and they differ by being specifically blackness or whiteness, then indeed this does not necessitate that what applies to the color-ness of blackness applies to the color-ness of whiteness such that the correctness of blackness turning into whiteness and vice versa becomes necessary. Thus here as well.
Source pages p. 131

Commentary
This passage is a defensive argument. The author anticipates an objection: the concept of dimension is different from the nature of dimension, so sharing the concept does not prove sharing the essence. He replies that this does not affect his claim. He illustrates with the example of blackness and whiteness: they share in the genus of color (lowniyya), but differ in their specific properties. Despite sharing color-ness, what applies to the color-ness of blackness (e.g., being black) does not apply to that of whiteness, and it does not follow that blackness can transform into whiteness. Similarly, dimensions share in the essence of dimension, but that does not imply that all accidental properties are common. The analogy reinforces his earlier proof that dimensions are identical in essence.

Key Terms
مفهوم
ماهية
لونية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If two things share a concept, they must share the same essence and all properties.
Correction: Sharing a concept (like dimension) does not prove essential identity, and even if essence is shared, it does not force all accidents to be common, as shown by black and white sharing color-ness but not transforming.

Checkpoint
What example does the author use to show that sharing in a general category does not imply sharing in all specific properties?
A) Dimensions and extension
B) Blackness and whiteness sharing in color-ness
C) Bodies and their accidents
D) Souls and bodies

Key Takeaway
The black/white analogy demonstrates that sharing a genus does not force sharing of specific attributes, thus defending the essential identity of dimensions despite conceptual differences.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a relationship (with a spouse, friend, or fellow Muslim) where you have assumed that because you share a fundamental bond—like being Muslim, family, or a shared belief—you must agree on every specific issue. How does the author's analogy of blackness and whiteness challenge that assumption? What would it cost you to accept legitimate differences while still preserving the essential unity? Commit to one practical change this week.
?
Consider your own understanding of a key Islamic concept like 'faith' (imān). Do you sometimes act as if the specific differences between schools or individuals undermine the shared essence? After reading this passage, how will you guard against that error in your interactions with others? Write a concrete resolution.
?
The author uses the example of blackness and whiteness to show that sharing in a genus does not mean one can transform into the other. Reflect on a personal struggle where you have tried to change someone's specific attributes (or your own) while ignoring the shared essence. What would it look like to honor the shared essence while respecting the distinct accidents? What step will you take today?

Six Counterexamples to the Principle of Identical Essences
Author refutes a premise by giving six cases where identical essences have different properties.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand six counterexamples that refute the claim that identical essences always have identical properties.

Core Idea
Identical essences can differ in their necessary attributes.

This passage presents six powerful counterexamples against a key premise in the debate about dimensions. The author aims to show that sameness of essence does not guarantee sameness of all properties, especially when external factors or causation intervene. These examples range from metaphysics (existence of God vs. contingents) to physics (corporeality of elements vs. celestial bodies) to psychology (souls) and logic (genus and species).

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then the translation. Notice the author's systematic listing of six counterexamples.
The passage is an objection-response unit.
2
Identify the first counterexample
Find the first counterexample: the term 'existent' applies to God and contingents, but properties like being created do not apply to God.
3
Identify the second counterexample
Corporeality is a single nature, but properties of elemental bodies differ from celestial bodies according to philosophers.
4
Identify the third and fourth counterexamples
Human souls are identical in essence but cannot migrate between bodies; similarly, two heads are identical but cannot be swapped.
Both rely on external accidents.
5
Identify the fifth and sixth counterexamples
Animality in humans and horses differs in receptivity due to different causes; body and its accidents show multiple existences differentiated by individuation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال الثاني : سلمنا أن الأبعاد متساوية في تمام الماهية. لكن لم لا يجوز أن يقال : إن أعدادها تكون مختلفة في اللوازم ؟ أما قوله : « الأشياء المتساوية في الماهية ، يصح على كل واحد منها ما يصح على الآخر ، قلنا : هذه المقدمة منقوصة بصور كثيرة . إحداها : إنه قد ثبت بالبرهان أن قول الموجود على لذاته ، وعلى الممكن لذاته بحسب مفهوم واحد . فإن الوجود من حيث إنه وجود لا تختلف حقيقته في الواجب والممكن وإذا ثبت هذا فنقول : وجود الله تعالى يكون مساوياً لوجود الممكن ، في مجرد كونه وجوداً ، ثم لم يلزم من هذا أن يصح على وجود الله تعالى كل ما يصح على وجود الممكنات ، وذلك يوجب القدح في قولكم : كل ما صح على الشيء صح على مثله وثانيها : إن الجسمية على ما قررتموه طبيعة واحدة . ثم الأشياء التي يصح كونها مقارنة الجسمية العناصر ، لا يلزم صحة كونها مقارنة الجسمية الأفلاك ، على أصول الفلاسفة . فكذا ههنا وثالثها : إن النفوس الإنسانية متساوية في تمام الماهية ، ثم لم يلزم من هذا أن يقال : النفس المتعلقة بهذا البدن يصح عليها أن ينقطع تعلقها عن هذا البدن ، وتصير متعلقة بالبدن الآخر، وكذلك القول في النفس المتعلقة بتدبير البدن الآخر . فكذا ههنا ورابعها : إن الرأس المتصل بهذا البدن الإنساني مساو للرأس المتصل بالبدن الإنساني الثاني في تمام الماهية. إنما المخالفة لو حصلت ، فإنما حصلت بسبب العوارض الخارجية، ثم لم يلزم أن يصح على كل واحد من الرأسين ما يصح على الرأس الثاني ، حتى يقال : إنه يصح أن ينفصل كل رأس عن البدن المخصوص ، ويتصل بالبدن الثاني. فيثبت : أنه لا يلزم أن يصح على الشيء ما يصح على مثله وخامسها : إن طبيعة الجنس واحدة في الأنواع، ثم إنه لم يلزم من كون الحيوانية التي في الإنسان قابلة للناطقية ، كون الحيوانية التي في الفرس قابلة للناطقية. لا يقال : لم لا يجوز أن يقال : الحيوانية من حيث هي حيوانية قابلة لجميع الفصول ، إلا أن الفصل المعين وهو الناطق ، لما أوجب تلك الحيوانية صارت تلك الحيوانية من لوازم الناطق ، لا لأجل أن منشأ اللزوم، جاء من جانب المعلول، بل إنما جاء من جانب العلة ، فبهذا الطريق يعقل أن الأشياء المتماثلة في تمام الماهية مختلفة في اللوازم . لأنا نقول : إذا عقلتم هذا المعنى ، بالطريق الذي ذكرتم ، فلم لا يجوز مثله في مسألتنا هذه ؟ وسادسها : إن الجسم له وجود ، فإذا حل فيه السواد والحلاوة والحركة فلكل واحد من الجسم، وهذه الأعراض الثلاثة وجود على حدة . فإذا حلت هذه الأعراض في الجسم ، فقد حلت هذه الوجودات الكثيرة في ذات ذلك الجسم ، فيلزمكم هناك اجتماع المتماثلات . فإن قلتم : إن تلك الوجودات وإن كانت متساوية في كونها وجودات، إلا أنها متباينة بتعيناتها وتشخصاتها ، فلم يلزم حصول الاتحاد وارتفاع الامتياز. فنقول : لم لا يجوز أن يكون الأمر كذلك في هذه المسألة ؟
The second question: We concede that dimensions are equal in the complete essence. But why is it not permissible to say that their numbers differ in the necessary attributes? As for his statement: 'Things equal in essence, what applies to one applies to the other.' We say: This premise is defective in many ways. First: It has been established by proof that the term 'existent' applies to the Necessary in itself and to the contingent in itself according to a single concept. For existence insofar as it is existence does not differ in reality in the Necessary and the contingent. And when this is established, we say: The existence of God Most High is equal to the existence of the contingent merely in being existence, yet it does not follow from this that everything that applies to the existence of the contingent applies to the existence of God Most High, and that necessitates a defect in your statement: 'Everything that applies to a thing applies to its like.' Second: Corporeality, according to your position, is a single nature. Then the things that can be concomitant with the corporeality of the elements do not necessarily have to be concomitant with the corporeality of the celestial spheres, according to the principles of the philosophers. Thus here as well. Third: Human souls are equal in the complete essence, yet it does not follow from this that it should be said: The soul attached to this body can have its attachment to this body cut off and become attached to another body, and likewise for the soul attached to the management of the other body. Thus here as well. Fourth: The head attached to this human body is equal to the head attached to the second human body in the complete essence. If difference occurs, it only occurs due to external accidents, yet it does not follow that what applies to one of the two heads applies to the other, such that it be said: It is possible for each head to separate from its specific body and become attached to the second body. Thus it is established that it is not necessary that what applies to a thing applies to its like. Fifth: The nature of the genus is one in the species, yet it does not follow from the fact that the animality in humanity is receptive of rationality that the animality in the horse is receptive of rationality. Do not say: Why is it not permissible to say that animality insofar as it is animality is receptive of all differentiae, except that the specific differentia, which is rationality, when it necessitates that animality, that animality becomes a necessary concomitant of rationality, not because the source of necessity comes from the side of the effect, but rather it comes from the side of the cause. Thus in this way it is understood that things that are similar in the complete essence differ in necessary attributes. For we say: If you have understood this meaning by the way you mentioned, then why is it not permissible for something similar in this question of ours? Sixth: The body has existence, and when blackness, sweetness, and motion inhere in it, each of the body and these three accidents has a separate existence. So when these accidents inhere in the body, these multiple existences have inhered in the essence of that body, and then you would be required to have the aggregation of similars. If you say: These existences, even though they are equal in being existences, are differentiated by their determinations and individuations, so union and elimination of distinction do not occur. We say: Why is it not permissible for the matter to be likewise in this question?
Source pages p. 131

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer unit. The objector (sā'il) raises a second question: Even if dimensions share the complete essence, why can they not differ in their necessary attributes (lawāzim)? He cites the principle that things equal in essence have identical properties. The author (mujīb) replies that this premise is defective (manqūṣa) and provides six counterexamples. First, the term 'existent' applies univocally to God and contingent beings, yet what applies to contingent existence (e.g., being created) does not apply to God's existence. This refutes the universal applicability of the principle. Second, corporeality is a single nature, but properties that accompany elemental bodies (e.g., generation and corruption) do not accompany celestial bodies (according to Aristotelian physics). Third, human souls are identical in essence, but a soul attached to one body cannot migrate to another body. Fourth, two human heads are identical in essence but cannot be swapped between bodies because of external accidents. Fifth, the genus 'animality' in humans is receptive of rationality, but the same animality in horses is not; the difference arises due to the cause (the specific differentia) rather than the essence itself. Sixth, a body and its accidents each have existence, so multiple existences inhere in one subject, which would imply aggregation of similars unless we say existence is differentiated by individuation. The author concludes that if the opponent concedes such differentiation in other cases, it must also be allowed in the case of dimensions.

Key Terms
اللوازم
الماهية
التماثل
المقدمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that identical essences guarantee identical properties in all respects.
Correction: The author shows that properties often differ due to external factors, causation, or individuation, as in the six counterexamples.

Checkpoint
How many counterexamples does the author provide to refute the premise that identical essences have identical properties?
A) Four
B) Five
C) Six
D) Seven

Key Takeaway
The author successfully demonstrates that the premise 'identical essences have identical properties' is false by providing six diverse counterexamples from metaphysics, physics, psychology, and logic.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The first counterexample shows that God's existence and created existences share the same concept of existence yet differ completely in their attributes. When you say 'Allah exists,' do you truly believe His existence is utterly unlike any other existence, or do you unconsciously imagine Him as a powerful being among beings? What specific attribute of God do you need to purify in your understanding today?
?
The example of human souls being identical in essence yet unable to swap bodies reminds you that Allah has placed you in a unique body, time, and circumstance. Are you grateful for your specific role, or do you envy others' situations? How can you embrace your particular responsibilities as a divine appointment?
?
If even in created things identical essences can have vastly different properties, how much more must Allah's essence be beyond all comparison? When you read this lesson, does it humble your intellect? Will you now rely more on revelation and less on analogical reasoning when thinking about Allah? Commit to one specific way you will increase your reliance on transmitted knowledge over personal speculation.

Distinction Through Intrinsic Determination
Objection and answer about distinction of dimensions through accidental determinations

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the philosophers' concept of determination and individuation is used to refute the objection that distinction requires accidental matters in the case of interpenetrating dimensions.

Core Idea
Determination alone distinguishes—no accidents needed.

This lesson examines a key objection in the debate over the interpenetration of dimensions. The opponent argues that distinction could come from accidental matters. The response draws on the philosophers' treatment of rational souls to show that each entity's own determination and individuation suffice for distinction, even without accidents. By the end, you'll see how this refutes the argument that interpenetration leads to indistinguishability.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the passage from 'السؤال الثالث' to the opponent's statement and the author's initial rejection. Notice the claim that accidental matters apply equally to both dimensions.
2
Examine the philosophers' analogy
Focus on the section about rational souls. The philosophers argue that even without accidental differences, souls remain distinct due to their individual determination and individuation acquired upon entering existence.
This is a key move: determination is intrinsic, not accidental.
3
Apply the analogy to space and body
Read the application: space has its own determination, and bodies have theirs. When one penetrates the other, each retains its individual determination.
Thus distinction is preserved even during interpenetration.
4
Understand the collapse of the opponent's argument
The conclusion: because each dimension retains its specific individuation, the opponent's claim that interpenetration leads to indistinguishability is refuted. Their argument collapses.
This shows that accidental matters are not necessary for distinction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال الثالث : لم لا يجوز أن يقال : إنه يحصل الامتياز بسبب الأمور العارضة ؟ قوله : كل عارض يفرض فإن حصوله بالنسبة إلى أحدهما كحصوله بالنسبة إلى الثاني ، قلنا : لا نسلم ، وما الدليل على أن الأمر كذلك ؟ والذي يؤكد هذا السؤال : أن مذهب الحكماء : أن النفوس الناطقة واحدة بالنوعية وبتمام الماهية. ومذهبهم : أنها باقية بعد المفارقة ، ثم إنهم أوردوا على أنفسهم سؤالاً فقالوا : النفوس الهيولانية العارية عن اكتساب المعارف والاخلاق ، إذا فارقت أبدانها ، فهناك لا امتياز بينها وبين غيرها لا في الماهية ولا في لوازمها ، ولا في عوارضها ، وذلك يوجب زوال المغايرة ثم أجابوا عنه ، فقالوا : إنها بعد أن دخلت في الوجود ، وحصل لكل واحد منها تعين وتشخص ، فذلك التعين حصل له لا لغيره ، فهذا القدر كاف في حصوله الامتياز بينها . إذا عرفت هذا فنقول : نحن نذكر ههنا أيضاً الفضاء الذي هو المكان ، لا شك أن له تعيناً وتشخصاً باعتباره كان قابلاً لتوارد الأجسام عليه ، وهذه الأجسام التي هي الأشياء القابلة للحركة والسكون لا شك أنه حصلت له تعينات وتشخصات، باعتبارها ، كانت قابلة للحركة والسكون . وإذا كان كذلك فعند نفود أحدهما في الآخر ، لم لا يجوز أن يقال : إنه بقي مع كل واحد منهما ، تعينه الخاص ، وتشخصه الخاص ؟ ثم من المعلوم أن تشخصه لا يصير هو بعينه تشخص غيره ، فهذا القدر كاف في حصول الامتياز والمغايرة بين البعد الذي هو المكان ، وبين البعد الذي هو المتمكن ، وعلى هذا التقدير فإنه يسقط ما ذكروه من الحجة .
The third question: Why is it not permissible to say that distinction occurs due to accidental matters? [The opponent] says: Every accidental matter that is supposed, its occurrence in relation to one of the two [dimensions] is like its occurrence in relation to the second. We say: We do not accept. And what is the proof that it is so? What confirms this question is that the doctrine of the philosophers is that the rational souls are one in species and complete essence. And their doctrine is that they remain after separation. Then they raised a question against themselves and said: The hylic souls devoid of acquisition of knowledge and character, when they leave their bodies, then there is no distinction between them and others, neither in essence nor in its concomitants nor in accidents, and that necessitates the removal of difference. Then they answered it, saying: After they entered existence, each one of them acquired a determination and individuation, so that determination occurred for it and not for another. This amount is sufficient for the occurrence of distinction between them. If you know this, then we say: We also mention here the space which is place. There is no doubt that it has a determination and individuation by virtue of which it is receptive to the succession of bodies upon it. And these bodies which are things receptive to motion and rest, there is no doubt that they have acquired determinations and individuations by virtue of which they are receptive to motion and rest. If it is so, then when one of them penetrates the other, why is it not permissible to say that each one retains its own determination and its own individuation? Then it is known that its individuation does not become the very individuation of another. So this amount is sufficient for the occurrence of distinction and difference between the dimension which is place and the dimension which is the occupant. On this estimation, their argument collapses.
Source pages p. 133

Commentary
This passage is an objection and answer in a kalam debate about the impossibility of interpenetration of two dimensions. The opponent raises the third question: Why cannot distinction be due to accidental matters? The author (likely Fakhr al-Din al-Razi) presents the opponent's claim that accidental matters apply equally to both, then rejects it, citing the philosophers' example of rational souls to argue that determination and individuation are sufficient for distinction even without accidental differences. He then applies this to space and body: each has its own determination, so upon interpenetration they remain distinct, thus refuting the argument that interpenetration implies indistinguishability.

Key Terms
الامتياز
العارضة
التعين
التشخص
الفضاء
البعد
المتمكن
المغايرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that distinction between two interpenetrating dimensions requires some accidental difference between them.
Correction: The passage shows that each dimension has its own intrinsic determination and individuation, which suffice for distinction without any need for accidental matters.

Checkpoint
According to the philosophers, what ensures distinction between rational souls after separation?
A. Their accidental qualities
B. Their determination and individuation
C. Their essence
D. Their location

Key Takeaway
Intrinsic determination and individuation are sufficient to distinguish two entities, even without accidental differences, thus refuting the objection against interpenetration.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider your own identity, do you rely on external markers (status, accomplishments) to feel distinct from others, or do you recognize the unique determination Allah has given you — a distinct soul that no one else possesses? How would your daily choices change if you lived with that certainty?
?
The philosophers argued that souls remain distinct after death by their intrinsic determination, not by what they acquired. If your soul's distinction is from Allah, not from your deeds, does that humble you or make you complacent? Will you let it drive you to seek nearness to Him, knowing that He has singled you out?
?
The lesson shows that even when two dimensions interpenetrate, each retains its own individuation. Have you ever felt your identity 'penetrated' or overwhelmed by others' expectations? How does this truth — that your unique self cannot be erased — free you from fearing that you will lose yourself in the crowd?

The Objection of Infinite Regress in Determination
Objection and reply: infinite regress from added determination.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical objection that positing determination as an additional attribute leads to an infinite regress.

Core Idea
Adding determination as a separate attribute creates an endless chain of distinctions.

This lesson tackles a key objection in arguments about determination (ta'ayyun). The opponent claims that even without a specific distinguishing factor, two dimensions might still be distinct. The reply shows that if determination were an added attribute, it would require further determinations, leading to an infinite regress—a logical impossibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the first part of the passage, where an opponent questions why lack of specific characterization prevents distinction.
The opponent assumes distinction is possible even without a specific cause.
2
Understand the reply
Then read the explanation: if determination is an extra attribute, all determinations share the same concept, so each needs another attribute to be distinguished, causing infinite regress.
Focus on the logical chain: equal concept → need extra distinction → regress.
3
Identify the key terms
Locate the Arabic terms: التعين (determination), الامتياز (distinction), التسلسل (infinite regress). Notice how they are used in the argument.
4
Reflect on the logical problem
Consider why infinite regress is considered a fatal flaw in kalam reasoning. Ask yourself: Why can't distinctions go on forever?
Infinite regress violates the principle of sufficient reason.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال الرابع : هب أن أحد البعدين لم يختص بشيء لأجله يمتاز عن الآخر ، فلم قلتم : إن ذلك يمنع من حصول المغايرة والامتياز ؟ وبيانه : وهو أنه لو كان تعين الشيء زائداً عليه ، لكانت التعينات متساوية في هذا المفهوم وهو كونها تعينات ، ثم يمتاز كل فرد منها على الفرد الآخر بأمر آخر زائد على تلك الماهية وحينئذ يلزم التسلسل
The fourth question: Suppose that one of the two dimensions is not specifically characterized by something by which it is distinguished from the other. Why do you say that this prevents the occurrence of difference and distinction? Its explanation: If the determination of a thing were additional to it, then determinations would be equal in this concept, namely being determinations. Then each individual would be distinguished from the other individual by another matter additional to that essence, and then an infinite regress would follow.
Source pages p. 133

Commentary
This passage is a concise logical objection. The opponent argues that even if a dimension lacks specific characterization, that does not preclude distinction. The author then points out that if determination were an additional attribute, then each determination would need another to distinguish it, leading to an infinite regress. This is a typical argument against positing extra attributes.

Key Terms
التعين
الامتياز
التسلسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The objection means that determination itself is impossible.
Correction: The objection is not against determination per se, but against the idea that determination is an additional attribute. The author's reply shows that adding it leads to regress, so the correct view is that determination is not additional but inherent.

Checkpoint
What logical consequence does the author derive from the idea that determination is an added attribute?
A. Contradiction
B. Infinite regress
C. Circularity
D. Necessity

Key Takeaway
If determination were an added attribute, it would require an infinite chain of further determinations, which is impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a situation where Allah's decree seems unclear, do you find yourself demanding further explanations or distinctions before you can submit? How does this argument against infinite regress challenge your need for endless justifications? Will you accept that Allah's determination is final and not requiring further added attributes?
?
In your own thinking about faith, are there areas where you add extra conditions or definitions that lead to an infinite chain of reasoning, never reaching a point of certainty? How can this lesson help you cut through that regress and rest in the primacy of Allah's will?
?
Consider a time when you tried to distinguish your own actions as being 'more determined' than others. Did that lead to an endless comparison? How does understanding that determination is not an added attribute free you from needing to constantly differentiate yourself?

Fifth Question: Interpenetration at Points of Contact
Objection using contact of points, lines, surfaces to argue for interpenetration

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the objection of interpenetration at points of contact is raised and why it fails according to the author.

Core Idea
Contact does not cause interpenetration—it would destroy contact itself.

The opponent argues that when two lines meet at a point, the points interpenetrate, proving that interpenetration is possible. The author counters that such a scenario would actually annihilate the points of contact, negating the very concept of contact.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage and translation, noting the structure: objection, examples, then author's response.
2
Identify the key examples
The opponent gives three analogous cases: points at line contact, lines at surface contact, surfaces at body contact.
3
Understand the author's response
The author says that at contact, the two extremities are annihilated and replaced by one common extremity, which would negate contact itself.
4
Reflect on the logical flaw
Consider why the opponent's analogy fails: they assume interpenetration occurs, but the author shows it would destroy the very condition (contact) that they rely on.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال الخامس : إنه ينتقض دليلكم بتداخل النقطتين حال تماس الخطين، وبتداخل الخطين حال تماس السطحين ، ويتداخل السطحين حال تماس الجسمين . فكل ما هو عذر لكم في هذه المواضع ، فهو عذر لنا في هذا المقام . إلا أنا نقول : إن عند حصول التماس يعدم الطرفان ، ويحدث طرف واحد مشترك بينهما ، إلا أن هذا يوجب نفي التماس أصلا . وذلك مما لم يقولوا به أصلاً والله أعلم
The fifth question: Your argument is contradicted by the interpenetration of two points at the contact of two lines, and the interpenetration of two lines at the contact of two surfaces, and the interpenetration of two surfaces at the contact of two bodies. So whatever excuse you have in these cases, is an excuse for us in this situation. Except that we say: Upon contact, the two extremities are annihilated, and a single extremity occurs common between them, except that this necessitates the negation of contact entirely. And that is something they never said at all. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 134

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth objection. The opponent argues that cases like two points at the contact of two lines become one point, which is interpenetration. Therefore, the same should apply to dimensions. The author responds that if contact annihilates the two extremities and creates a single common one, then contact itself is negated, which the opponents do not accept. So the analogy is invalid.

Key Terms
التماس
النقطتان
الخطين
السطحين
الجسمين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Contact between two objects implies that they interpenetrate at the point of contact.
Correction: The author shows that if interpenetration occurred, the two extremities would be annihilated and replaced by a single one, which would negate contact itself. Therefore, contact does not imply interpenetration.

Checkpoint
What does the author claim happens to the two extremities upon contact?
A. They remain separate
B. They are annihilated and replaced by a single common extremity
C. They become identical
D. They are preserved

Key Takeaway
Contact does not cause interpenetration; it would destroy contact itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider your own body touching something else, do you instinctively assume that contact implies a kind of merging? How would the annihilation of your own sense of touch affect your perception of reality?
?
If contact truly annihilated the meeting points, what would that mean for your belief in the distinct creation of each thing? Would you still be able to affirm that God creates all things separate and distinct?
?
The opponent's argument seems logical at first glance. What is it in your own thinking that makes you want to accept analogies without checking their deeper implications? How can you train yourself to spot when an analogy breaks down?

Fourth Argument: Reductio of World in a Mustard Seed
Reductio argument: interpenetration leads to universe in mustard seed

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the reductio argument against interpenetration and the distinction between external and mental permissibility.

Core Idea
If interpenetration were allowed, the entire universe could fit in a mustard seed.

The fourth argument against interpenetration uses a reductio ad absurdum: if bodies could interpenetrate, the entire world could fit into a single mustard seed. But this is obviously false, so interpenetration must be impossible. The argument then examines what kind of permissibility is being claimed — external or mental — and what would need to be proven for the reductio to hold.

Guided Path
1
Read the reductio argument
Read the passage once, focusing on the argument that if interpenetration were permissible, the whole universe could fit into a mustard seed.
2
Identify the key distinction
Notice the question posed: does the opponent require external or mental permissibility? Understand the difference: external permissibility means certainty of non-impossibility, mental means mere lack of certainty.
3
List the required proofs
The passage outlines three things needed to prove the reductio: equality of dimensions, transfer of attributes, and absence of special conditions or obstacles. Note that the first two were already discussed.
4
Connect to previous arguments
Understand how this argument builds on previous objections, especially the second argument. The text references prior discussion.

The Second Objection: Interpenetration at Surfaces
The author objects that surface contact implies interpenetration of lines.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the second objection forces the opponent into self-contradiction regarding interpenetration.

Core Idea
Surface contact implies line penetration, trapping the opponent.

This lesson examines the second objection against the impossibility of interpenetration. The author argues that when two surfaces meet, the edge-line of each penetrates the other, leading to the same absurdity the opponent claimed to reject. This forces the opponent to either accept interpenetration or abandon their own position.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice the conditional structure: if surface contact leads to penetration, and that leads to absurdity, then the opponent cannot escape.
2
Identify the key terms
Focus on 'line' (خط) and 'penetration of dimension into dimension' (نفود بعد في بعد). Understand what is being claimed about the edge-lines.
The objection is about lines, not surfaces.
3
Trace the logical implication
Work through the argument: Premise 1: surfaces meeting cause lines to penetrate. Premise 2: if that penetration leads to absurdity, then the opponent's own view is absurd. Conclusion: The opponent's rejection of interpenetration is self-defeating.
This is a reductio ad absurdum.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال الثاني : أن نقول : قد بينا أنه إذا لقي سطح سطحاً ، فإن الخط الذي هو طرف أحدهما ، يكون نافداً في الخط الذي هو طرف السطح الثاني ، فإن لزم من نفود بعد في بعد ، ما ذكرتم من المحال ، كان ذلك أيضاً لازماً عليكم .
The second objection: we say: we have shown that when a surface meets a surface, then the line that is the edge of one penetrates the line that is the edge of the second surface. So if there follows from the penetration of a dimension into a dimension what you mentioned of absurdity, then that is also necessary upon you.
Source pages p. 135

Commentary
This passage presents the second of three objections. The author argues that if interpenetration is impossible, then the opponent must also be committed to the impossibility in their own view, because when two surfaces meet, the edge-line of one penetrates the other, implying interpenetration of dimensions. Thus the opponent cannot consistently reject interpenetration.

Key Terms
سطح
خط
نفود بعد في بعد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The objection claims that surfaces themselves interpenetrate when they meet.
Correction: The objection specifically says that the lines that are the edges of the surfaces penetrate each other, not the surfaces as a whole.

Checkpoint
According to the second objection, what happens when one surface meets another?
A) The surfaces become one single surface.
B) The line at the edge of one penetrates the line at the edge of the other.
C) The surfaces interpenetrate completely.
D) The surfaces repel each other.

Key Takeaway
The second objection forces the opponent to admit that their own description of surface contact entails the very interpenetration they deny, creating a self-contradiction.

The Third Objection: Rarefaction and Condensation
The author defines rarefaction and condensation and uses them in a reductio argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author uses the concepts of rarefaction and condensation in a reductio ad absurdum argument against the opponent who denies interpenetration.

Core Idea
If you allow rarefaction and condensation, you must allow extreme cases.

The author presents a clever argument: the opponent rejects interpenetration but accepts rarefaction and condensation. By defining these terms precisely, the author shows that they lead to absurd consequences unless the opponent rejects them as well. This exposes inconsistency in the opponent's position.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage carefully
Read the full Arabic passage once, then read the translation. Notice the precise definitions and the logical flow.
The passage includes a bracketed variant; focus on the main text.
2
Understand the definitions
Note how rarefaction and condensation are defined: increase/decrease in quantity without external addition/removal or internal void/compaction.
These definitions are crucial for the argument.
3
Trace the reductio argument
The author says: if you allow these processes in ordinary cases, you must allow extreme cases (mustard seed becoming celestial sphere, etc.). If you reject the extreme, you must reject the ordinary.
This shows the opponent's position is internally inconsistent.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The author concludes by matching the opponent's stance: if they accept the extreme, he accepts their objection; if they reject, he rejects.
The argument is symmetrical and uses the opponent's own principle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال الثالث : أن نقول : إنكم وإن كنتم لا تجوزون نفود بعد في بعد ، لكنكم تجوزون القول بالتخلخل والتكاثف . والمراد من التخلخل هو أن يصير مقدار الجسم أعظم مما كان من غير أن ينضم إليه شيء من خارج ، ومن غير أن يحصل في داخله شيء من الفرج. والمراد من التكاثف هو أن يصير مقدار الشيء أصغر مما كان من غير أن ينفصل عنه شيء من الأجزاء ، أو من غير أن يحصل لشيء من أجزائه المنفوشة اكتناز . إذا عرفت هذا ، فنقول : إذا جوزتم هذا ، فجوزوا أن يتخلخل جرم الخردلة ، حتى يصير في العظم مثل كرة الفلك الأعظم [ أو أعظم ] وجوزوا أيضاً أن يتكاثف الفلك الأعظم حتى يصير في الصغر مثل الخردلة أو أصغر، فإن التزموا تجويز ذلك، فنحن أيضاً نلتزم ما الزموه علينا ، وإن أبوا ذلك أبينا نحن أيضاً ما ألزموه علينا . [ والله أعلم ] .
The third objection: we say: Although you do not allow the penetration of a dimension into a dimension, you do allow the theory of rarefaction and condensation. The meaning of rarefaction is that the quantity of a body becomes greater than it was without anything external being added to it, and without any void occurring inside it. The meaning of condensation is that the quantity of a thing becomes smaller than it was without any part separating from it, or without any compaction occurring in any of its fluffed parts. If you understand this, then we say: If you allow this, then allow that the body of a mustard seed rarefies until it becomes in size like the sphere of the great [or greater] sphere, and also allow that the great sphere condenses until it becomes in smallness like a mustard seed or smaller. If they commit to allowing that, then we also commit to what they required of us, and if they refuse that, we also refuse what they required of us. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 135

Commentary
This passage presents the third objection, which is a classic reductio ad absurdum argument. The opponent allows rarefaction (takhalkhul) and condensation (takathuf) but denies interpenetration. The author defines both terms precisely: rarefaction is increase in quantity without addition of matter or creation of void; condensation is decrease without removal of parts or compaction. If the opponent allows these, then they must allow the extreme cases: a mustard seed rarefying to the size of the celestial sphere, or the sphere condensing to a mustard seed. If they accept this, the author agrees to accept their objection; if they reject, the author also rejects their objection. This shows inconsistency.

Key Terms
تخلخل
تكاثف
مقدار
جرم
خردلة
فلك أعظم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That rarefaction and condensation involve change in substance rather than change in quantity without addition or removal of matter.
Correction: The passage defines rarefaction as increase in quantity without adding matter, and condensation as decrease without removing parts. They do not involve change in substance.

Checkpoint
What is the definition of rarefaction (تخلخل) given in the passage?
A) Increase in quantity by adding matter from outside.
B) Increase in quantity without adding anything external or creating internal void.
C) Decrease in quantity by removing parts.
D) Change in shape without change in quantity.

Key Takeaway
The author uses precise definitions of rarefaction and condensation to construct a reductio ad absurdum that exposes the opponent's inconsistency.

The Fifth Argument: Impossibility of Multiple Dimensions in One Perceived Entity
Fifth argument against interpenetration based on perceptual unity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fifth argument against interpenetration of dimensions, which uses perceptual unity to show its absurdity.

Core Idea
Sensory unity proves dimensional unity; allowing multiplicity leads to absurdity.

This lesson presents a clever reductio ad absurdum argument. The Mutakallimun argue that if interpenetration were possible, then a single perceived object could actually be many objects, which is rationally impossible. Follow the reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic and English side by side to grasp the argument's flow.
The passage is dense; read carefully.
2
Identify the definition of personal dimension
The argument starts with a definition: personal dimension is the distance between two sides of a container.
This is key to the reductio.
3
Understand the reductio
If one dimension could be many, then a perceived single human could be many humans.
This is the absurd conclusion.
4
Note the objection and response
The author notes the objection is same as previous, so no need to repeat.
This shows the argument is not new.
5
Summarize the argument's structure
Review how the argument connects place, dimension, and interpenetration.
This concludes the full argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الخامس من الوجوه التي تمسكوا بها في بيان أن تداخل الأبعاد : ممتنع ، وهو أنهم قالوا : لا معنى للبعد الشخصي إلا البعد الذي بين طرفي هذا الإناء . فلو جاز في العقل أن يقال : البعد الموجود بين طرفي هذا الإناء بعد ان لا بعد واحد ، مع أن المشار إليه بالحس ليس إلا الواحد ، فلم لا يجوز أن يحصل الشك في أن هذا الشخص الذي هو بحسب الإشارة الحسية واحد هل هو في نفسه واحد أم لا ؟ وحينئذ يلزمنا تجويز أن يقال : هذا الإنسان الذي نراه واحداً بحب الحس ، لعله لا يكون [ في نفسه ] واحداً ، بل يكون ألف إنسان أو أكثر ، ومعلوم أن تجويز ذلك دخول في العته والجنون . ولقـائل أن يقول: الاعتراض على هذا الوجه [ مثل الاعتراض على الوجه ] الرابع من غير تفاوت أصلاً ، فلا فائدة في الإعادة . فهذا تمام الكلام في تقرير هذه الحجة نفياً وإثباتاً. وهي قولهم : لو كان المكان بعداً ، لزم من حصول المتمكن في المكان ، تداخل البعدين. وهذا محال ، فذا محال [ والله أعلم ] .
The fifth of the arguments they held to in demonstrating that interpenetration of dimensions is impossible: they said: There is no meaning for personal dimension except the dimension between the two sides of this container. So if it is intellectually permissible to say: The dimension existing between the two sides of this container is not one dimension, despite the fact that the sense-perceptible referent is only one, then why is it not permissible that doubt occurs regarding this individual who, according to sensory indication, is one, whether in itself it is one or not? And then it would be necessary for us to allow the statement: This human whom we see as one by sense, perhaps it is not one in itself, but rather a thousand humans or more. And it is known that allowing that is entering into idiocy and madness. And someone may say: The objection to this argument is like the objection to the fourth, with no difference at all, so there is no benefit in repetition. So this is the complete discussion in presenting this argument negatively and positively. And it is their saying: If place were a dimension, then from the occurrence of the occupant in the place, interpenetration of the two dimensions would follow. And this is impossible, so the premise is impossible. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 136

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth argument for the impossibility of interpenetration. The argument states that a personal dimension (البعد الشخصي) is defined as the distance between two points of a container. If it were possible for one dimension to be actually more than one dimension despite sensory perception indicating only one, then one could doubt whether a single perceived human is really one or thousands, which is absurd. This is a reductio ad absurdum. After presenting the argument, the author notes that a potential objection to this argument is identical to the objection to the fourth argument, so there is no need to repeat. The passage concludes with a summary of the entire first argument: if place is a dimension, then the occupant's being in place would require interpenetration of two dimensions, which is impossible.

Key Terms
البعد الشخصي
الشخص
الإشارة الحسية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: It is possible for one dimension to be multiple dimensions without affecting sensory perception.
Correction: Sensory unity is a reliable indicator of actual unity; allowing multiplicity undermines rational certainty and leads to absurdity.

Checkpoint
What absurdity does the fifth argument claim follows from allowing interpenetration of dimensions?
A) A container would have no dimensions.
B) A single human could be perceived as many.
C) A human could be thousand humans.
D) The universe would collapse.

Key Takeaway
The fifth argument shows that allowing interpenetration leads to absurd consequences like a single human being actually a thousand humans, which is rationally impossible.

The Second Proof against Place Being a Dimension
A syllogism proving that space cannot be a dimension.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the syllogism showing that space cannot be a dimension because it neither inheres in matter nor stands separately.

Core Idea
Space cannot be a dimension; both possible modes fail.

This lesson presents a concise syllogism from the kalam tradition that refutes the idea that place is a dimension. The argument forces us to consider whether the dimension called space inheres in matter or not. Both options lead to contradictions, thus proving the claim false.

Guided Path
1
Read the short passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice the logical structure: a disjunction followed by rejection of both options.
This is a classic reductio ad absurdum.
2
Identify the two divisions
The passage splits the possibility into two: the dimension either resides in matter or does not. Understand what each entails.
3
Grasp why both are false
Although the passage says both are false without elaboration, consider from earlier discussions: inhering would make it an accident (impossible for space), not inhering would make it a separate entity (also impossible).
The author assumes the reader remembers previous proofs.
4
Apply the conclusion
Since both options are false, the original claim that place is the dimension is false. Reflect on the importance of this for the concept of space.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية على إبطال القول بأن المكان هو البعد : أن قالوا : البعد المسمى بالفضاء ، إما أن يكون حالاً في مادة ، أو لا يكون حالاً في مادة . والقسمان باطلان ، فبطل القول بأن المكان هو البعد .
The second proof for invalidating the statement that place is the dimension: they said: The dimension called space is either residing in matter or not residing in matter. Both divisions are false, so the statement that place is the dimension is false.
Source pages p. 136

Commentary
This passage presents a short syllogism. The argument is: The dimension called space (الفضاء) must either inhere in matter or not. Both options are impossible. If it inheres, then it would be an accident in matter; but space is not an accident. If it does not inhere, then it would be a separate entity, but that is also impossible. Therefore, space cannot be a dimension, and thus place is not a dimension. The author does not elaborate on why both divisions are false, but it is implied from earlier discussions.

Key Terms
الحجة الثانية
البعد
الفضاء
حال في مادة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That space is an accident inhering in matter.
Correction: The argument shows that space cannot inhere in matter because that would make it an accident, and space is not an accident. Additionally, it cannot be a separate entity. So space cannot be a dimension at all.

Checkpoint
What are the two divisions of the dimension called space in the second proof?
A) Either it is a substance or an accident.
B) Either it resides in matter or not.
C) Either it is finite or infinite.
D) Either it is created or uncreated.

Key Takeaway
The second proof uses a disjunctive syllogism to show that space is not a dimension, because both possible modes of existence for a dimension are impossible for space.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your everyday experience treats space as an empty container independent of anything. This proof claims that such a concept is logically impossible. Does this shake your confidence in sensory perception? Are you willing to accept a rational proof that contradicts your empirical assumptions?
?
The proof reduces the argument to two possibilities and shows both are false. In your own life, when you hold a belief about God or the afterlife, have you sincerely examined both possible alternatives? Or do you accept one without considering the other?
?
If space is not a dimension, then what is it? The lesson leaves you with a question mark. Does this uncertainty trouble you? Are you comfortable trusting that rational inquiry leads to truth, even when it does not give you a complete picture?

The Fifth Argument Against Dimensional Interpenetration
Opponent's fifth argument against dimensional interpenetration with objection

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fifth argument against the possibility of dimensional interpenetration, including its logical structure and the objection that it is redundant with the fourth argument.

Core Idea
If sensory unity could hide real multiplicity, then we could doubt that a single human is truly one – an absurdity.

The opponents of dimensional interpenetration present a fifth argument that moves from a physical analogy to a psychological absurdity. They argue that if a single sensed distance could be two real distances, then we could not be sure that a person we see as one is not actually many. The author reports this argument and notes that it is essentially the same as the previous one.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the use of the vessel analogy and the reductio ad absurdum.
2
Identify the core analogy
The argument begins with a definition of personal dimension as the distance between the ends of a vessel. It then asks: if it is intellectually permissible to say that this distance is actually two dimensions despite appearing as one, then...
3
Trace the reductio ad absurdum
The argument extends the reasoning: if sensory unity can conceal real multiplicity, then we cannot be certain that a single human seen by the senses is truly one individual. He might be a thousand humans – a clear absurdity.
This shows the logical consequence of allowing two real dimensions in a single sensed distance.
4
Understand the objection and conclusion
An objector points out that this argument is identical to the fourth, so repeating it is pointless. The passage ends by restating the underlying argument: if place were a dimension, then dimensional interpenetration would occur, which is impossible, so the premise is false.
The underlying argument is what really matters – the fifth face is just a way of illustrating it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الخامس من الوجوه التي تمسكوا بها في بيان أن تداخل الأبعاد ممتنع، وهو أنهم قالوا: لا معنى للبعد الشخصي إلا البعد الذي بين طرفي هذا الإناء. فلو جاز في العقل أن يقال: البعد الموجود بين طرفي هذا الإناء بعدان لا بعد واحد، مع أن المشار إليه بالحس ليس إلا الواحد، فلم لا يجوز أن يحصل الشك في أن هذا الشخص الذي هو بحسب الإشارة الحسية واحد هل هو في نفسه واحد أم لا؟ وحينئذ يلزمنا تجويز أن يقال: هذا الإنسان الذي نراه واحداً بالحس، لعله لا يكون [في نفسه] واحداً، بل يكون ألف إنسان أو أكثر، ومعلوم أن تجويز ذلك دخول في العته والجنون. ولقائل أن يقول: الاعتراض على هذا الوجه [مثل الاعتراض على الوجه] الرابع من غير تفاوت أصلاً، فلا فائدة في الإعادة. فهذا تمام الكلام في تقرير هذه الحجة نفياً وإثباتاً. وهي قولهم: لو كان المكان بعداً، لزم من حصول المتمكن في المكان، تداخل البعدين. وهذا محال، فذا محال [والله أعلم].
The fifth face among the faces they clung to in clarifying that the interpenetration of dimensions is impossible: they said: There is no meaning for the personal dimension except the dimension which is between the two ends of this vessel. So if it is permissible in the intellect to say: the existing dimension between the two ends of this vessel is two dimensions, not one dimension, while the sensory referent is only one, then why is it not permissible that doubt occur concerning this individual which is one according to sensory reference – whether it is in itself one or not? And then it would be incumbent upon us to permit saying: this human being whom we see as one by sense, perhaps he is not [in himself] one, rather he may be a thousand humans or more, and it is known that permitting that is entering into imbecility and madness. And one may say: the objection to this face [is like the objection to the] fourth face without any difference at all, so there is no benefit in repetition. So this is the complete speech in presenting this argument negatively and affirmatively. And it is their statement: If place were a dimension, then from the occurrence of the located thing in place, the interpenetration of the two dimensions would follow. And that is impossible, so this is impossible. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 136

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth argument (الوجه الخامس) used by the opponents to prove that the interpenetration of dimensions is impossible. The author recounts the argument: they define the personal dimension (البعد الشخصي) as the distance between two ends of a vessel. Then they propose a hypothetical: if it were intellectually permissible to say that the distance between the two ends is actually two distances despite being sensed as one, then there would be no barrier to doubting whether an individual sensed as one is truly one or many. This leads to the absurdity that a human seen as one might be thousands—a clear absurdity indicating madness. Then an objector notes that this objection is identical to the fourth, so repeating it is pointless. The author then concludes by summarizing the underlying core argument: if place were a dimension, then a located thing existing in place would require two dimensions to interpenetrate, which is impossible; thus the premise is false. The passage thus presents the complete argument, objection, and final conclusion.

الجهة الحسية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument is about actual instances where a single human is really many humans.
Correction: The argument is not about actual multiplicity but about the logical consequences of allowing a distinction between sensory unity and real multiplicity.
