Refutation of Space as Dimension: Interpenetration Impossibility
Author refutes the first division: space as dimension leads to body and interpenetration.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the author argues that if space or void is considered a dimension, it would necessarily be a body, leading to the impossible consequence of interpenetration.

Core Idea
If space is a dimension, it becomes a body, making interpenetration impossible.

In this passage, the author presents a concise proof to refute the first division of the view that place (makan) is a dimension. By defining a body as a dimension subsisting in matter, he shows that space and void would then be bodies, and the claim that an object penetrates space implies interpenetration of two bodies—a logical impossibility. Let us examine this argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic text and its literal translation to grasp the author's compressed argument.
Notice the logical flow: definition, conditional, consequence, conclusion.
2
Understand the definition of body
The author defines body as 'a dimension subsisting in matter'. This is crucial for the argument.
If space is a dimension subsisting in matter, it fits the definition of a body.
3
Trace the conditional argument
If space and void are dimensions in matter, then they are bodies. The statement 'the occupant penetrates through it' would then mean interpenetration of two bodies.
Interpenetration (تداخل الأجسام) is considered impossible in kalam.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Since interpenetration is impossible, the first division (that place is dimension) must be false.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما بطلان القسم الأول . فالدليل عليه : وهو أنه لا معنى للجسم إلا البعد الحال في المادة ، فإن كان الفضاء والخلاء كذلك ، كان جسماً . فالقول بأن المتمكن ينفد فيه قول بتداخل الجسمين . وهو محال
As for the invalidity of the first division: the proof for it is that there is no meaning of body except a dimension subsisting in matter. So if space and void were like that, they would be a body. Thus the statement that the occupant penetrates through it is a statement of the interpenetration of two bodies. And that is impossible.
Source pages p. 137

Commentary
This passage is a proof refuting the first division of the view that place (makan) is dimension (bu'd). The author defines body as a dimension subsisting in matter. If space (al-fada') and void (al-khala') are dimensions subsisting in matter, then they would be bodies. But the claim that an occupant (al-mutamakkin) penetrates through space implies interpenetration of two bodies, which is impossible (muhal). Thus the first division is false.

Refutation of the Second Division: Dimension Independent of Matter
Author refutes the second division and responds to an objection about the equality of dimensions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the contradiction that arises if dimension is considered independent of matter, and how an objector challenges the argument's reliance on the uniformity of all dimensions.

Core Idea
If dimension is independent of matter, then no dimension can ever be in matter—but bodies have dimensions, a clear contradiction.

This passage presents a key refutation of the notion that dimension can exist independently of matter. The author argues that such independence would prevent any dimension from subsisting in matter, yet bodies are defined by their dimensions. An objector then questions the argument's underlying assumptions, pointing out that it requires all dimensions to be equal in essence. The author refers to earlier discussions as the answer.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the translation to grasp the flow of the argument and the objection.
Notice the structure: a proof followed by an objection.
2
Identify the reductio argument
Trace the logic: if dimension is independent of matter, it cannot become dependent, so no dimension would be in matter. But bodies have dimensions—this contradicts the fact that bodies are extended.
This is a classical argument from contradiction (khalaf).
3
Understand the objection
An interlocutor argues that the proof only works if two premises are true: all dimensions share the same quiddity, and what holds for one holds for its like. Consider whether these premises are justified.
The author does not respond here but references prior discussion.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this objection matters: it shows that even a sound-looking reductio can depend on hidden assumptions. Think about how kalam scholars establish such premises.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما بطلان القسم الثاني . فهو أن على هذا التقدير تكون طبيعة البعد مع جميع ذاتياتها ولوازمها غنية عن المادة ، والغني عن المادة يمتنع أن يعرض له ما يحوجه إلى المادة ، فكان يجب أن لا يكون شيء من الأبعاد حالا في المادة فوجب أن لا يكون الجسم الذي هو المتمكن ذا بعد . هذا خلف . ولقائل أن يقول : هذه الحجة إنما تتم لو ثبت أن الأبعاد بأسرها متساوية في الماهية ، ثم ثبت أن كل ما صح على الشيء ، وجب أن يصح على مثله ، وقد تقدم الكلام على هاتين المقدمتين
And as for the invalidity of the second division: it is that according to this supposition, the nature of dimension with all its essentials and necessary concomitants is independent of matter. And that which is independent of matter is impossible to be subject to what makes it need matter. So it must be that no dimension subsists in matter, and then it must be that the body, which is the occupant, does not have a dimension. This is a contradiction. And someone might say: this argument is only complete if it has been established that all dimensions are equal in quiddity, and then established that whatever is true of one thing must be true of its like; and we have already discussed these two premises.
Source pages p. 137

Commentary
This passage is a proof refuting the second division, followed by an objection and a brief response. The proof assumes that if dimension is independent of matter, then it cannot become dependent, so no dimension would ever be in matter. But bodies have dimensions—contradiction. The objector says the argument requires two premises: (1) all dimensions are equal in quiddity, and (2) what holds for one holds for its like. The author refers to earlier discussion as the answer.

Correction: The objector points out that the argument relies on two premises: all dimensions are equal in quiddity, and what holds for one holds for its like. Without these, the argument fails.

?
The reductio argument forces a choice: either dimension depends on matter or it doesn't—one leads to absurdity. In matters of faith, are you willing to follow a line of reasoning to its end, even if it means abandoning a comfortable position? What would it cost you to submit to truth?

The Dilemma of Dimension's Movability
Author presents the third argument: dimension is either movable or not, both lead to absurdity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the third argument refutes the view that place is dimension by examining whether dimension is movable or not, and how both possibilities lead to absurdities.

Core Idea
The dilemma of dimension's movability shows the view of place as dimension is self-contradictory.

This lesson presents the third argument against the theory that place is simply dimension. The author constructs a dilemma: either dimension moves or it does not. By tracing each horn, we see that both lead to impossible consequences, thereby refuting the original claim. Follow the reasoning step by step to see why.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the overall structure of the dilemma.
Pay attention to the two main branches.
2
Analyze the first horn
Examine the argument that if dimension is movable, it leads to an infinite regress of places and a contradiction about the place of all dimensions.
The strongest reason is given to show the impossibility.
3
Analyze the second horn
Consider the alternative: if dimension is not movable, then bodies cannot move, which is false.
This horn is shorter but equally decisive.
4
Understand the conclusion
Recognize that both possibilities are absurd, so the original claim that place is dimension must be false.
The author concludes that the statement is invalid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثالثة في إبطال القول بأن المكان هو البعد : أن قالوا : البعد من حيث إنه بعد ، إما أن يقبل الحركة أو لا يقبل الحركة . فإن كان قابلاً للحركة ، فالبعد الذي هو الفضاء ، والمكان، يجب أيضاً أن يكون قابلا للحركة، وكل ما تحرك فإنما يتحرك من مكان إلى مكان ، فللمكان مكان آخر والكلام في الثاني كما في الأول ، فيلزم القول بإثبات أمكنة غير متناهية ، يكون كل واحد منها نافداً في الآخر . وذلك محال . لوجوه : أقواها [ وأقربها إلى الأفهام (۱) ] : أن مجموع تلك الأمكنة الغير المتناهية ، والأبعاد المتداخلة الغير المتناهية : أبعاد . وكل بعد فهو قابل للحركة ، فذلك المجموع قابل للحركة وكل ما كان قابلاً للحركة ، فحركته إنما تكون من مكان إلى مكان ، فلمجموع تلك الأبعاد الغير المتناهية مكان. فذلك المكان إما أن يكون بعداً ، وإما أن لا يكون . والأول باطل . لأن ذلك المكان لكونه مكاناً لكل الأبعاد يجب أن لا يكون من جنس الأبعاد . لأن كل ما كان ظرفاً [ ووعاء (٢) ] لمجموع أشياء كان خارجاً عنها ، والخارج عن الشيء لا يكون منه . ولكونه فرداً من أفراد الأبعاد ، يجب أن يكون داخلا في جملة الأبعاد ، فلو كان مكان جملة الأبعاد (۱) وأظهرها ( ت ) ، م ) . (۲) سقط ( م ) .  بعداً ، لزم كونه داخلاً في جملة تلك الأبعاد ، وكونه خارجاً عن جملتها . وذلك محال . وإما إن كان مكان جملة [ تلك (۱) ] الأبعاد ليس بعداً ، بل شيئاً مغايراً للبعد ، فحينئذ يكون المكان الحقيقي أمراً مغايراً للبعد ، وذلك هو المطلوب . وأما إن كان [ البعد (۲) ] من حيث إنه بعد ، غير قابل للحركة [ فنقول : الجسم له بعد ، وبعده غير قابل للحركة (۳) ] فوجب أن لا يكون [ الجسم (4) ] قابلا للحركة . وذلك باطل فاسد . فيثبت : أن القول بكون المكان بعداً ، يفضي إلى هذين القسمين الباطلين . فكان القول به باطلاً
The third argument for refuting the statement that place is dimension: they said: dimension, insofar as it is dimension, either accepts motion or does not accept motion. If it is movable, then dimension which is space and place must also be movable; and whatever moves moves from place to place, so place has another place, and the discussion about the second is like that about the first, necessitating the assertion of infinite places, each one penetrating into the other. That is impossible. For reasons, the strongest [and closest to understanding (1)] is: that the totality of those infinite places and infinite interpenetrating dimensions are dimensions. And every dimension is movable, so that totality is movable. And everything movable, its motion is only from place to place, so that totality of infinite dimensions has a place. So that place is either a dimension or not. The first is false, because that place, for being a place of all dimensions, must not be of the genus of dimensions; for whatever is a container [and vessel (2)] for a totality of things is outside them, and what is outside a thing is not from it. And for its being an individual of dimensions, it must be included in the totality of dimensions. So if it were a place of the totality of dimensions, it would necessitate its being included in that totality of dimensions and being outside them—impossible. And if the place of the totality of those dimensions is not a dimension, but something other than dimension, then the real place is something other than dimension, and that is what is sought. And if dimension, insofar as it is dimension, is not movable [then we say: the body has a dimension, and its dimension is not movable], so the body must not be movable. That is false and corrupt. So it is established that the statement that place is dimension leads to these two false divisions. So the statement is false.
Source pages p. 137

Commentary
This passage is a proof (al-hujjah al-thālithah) against the view that place is dimension. It presents a dilemma: dimension either accepts motion or does not. If movable, then place itself would be movable, leading to an infinite regress of places and a contradiction about the place of the infinite totality. The strongest reason is that the totality of infinite dimensions must have a place, but that place cannot be a dimension (since it must contain all dimensions) nor non-dimension (contradiction). If not movable, then body's dimension would not be movable, so body would not be movable—false. Thus the view is invalid.

Key Terms
بعد
حركة
مكان
غير متناهية
مجموع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Only material dimensions are movable; immaterial dimensions like place are not.
Correction: The argument assumes all dimensions are either movable or not, regardless of materiality; the dilemma applies to the concept of dimension itself.

Checkpoint
What does the third argument conclude if dimension is said to be non-movable?
A) All dimensions are immaterial
B) Bodies would not be movable
C) Place would be infinite
D) Dimensions would be multiple

Key Takeaway
The view that place is dimension fails because both possible natures of dimension (movable or not) lead to absurdities.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

When you think about the space you occupy right now, have you ever considered that your common-sense understanding of 'place' might actually be incoherent? This argument shows that even a simple concept can hide contradictions. What would it cost you to change that premise?
?
This argument relies on the principle that a container must be outside its contents. When it comes to your beliefs, what is containing them? Are you aware of the frameworks or worldviews that hold your thoughts together? Have you critically examined whether those frameworks themselves are consistent? If not, what is stopping you from doing so? Will you take a step today to reflect on the container of your own thinking?

Objection and Answer: Abstract vs. Material Dimension
Author responds to an objection distinguishing abstract and material dimension.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the objection that dimension in matter is movable while abstract dimension is not fails, through the author's two-part refutation.

Core Idea
Dimension cannot be an attribute of a subject without leading to absurdity.

The author anticipates an objection that might weaken his third argument. The objector tries to salvage the possibility of motion for dimension by distinguishing between abstract and material dimension. In response, the author offers two powerful refutations that dismantle the very idea of dimension inhering in a subject.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the objection carefully: the objector claims that abstract dimension (space) is not movable, but dimension in matter (body) is movable because it is in matter.
The objector assumes dimension can be an attribute of matter.
2
First refutation: dimension cannot be an attribute
The author states he will prove that dimension cannot subsist in a subject at all. This undercuts the objection's premise.
This is a promissory note; the proof comes later.
3
Second refutation: even if it could, the subject leads to absurdity
Assume dimension can be an attribute. The subject of dimension must either be extended (a dimension itself) or abstract. If extended, it leads to infinite regress. If abstract, it cannot move because motion requires spatial location.
Both options are impossible, so the objection fails.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : لم لا يجوز أن يقال : البعد المجرد وهو الفضاء غير قابل للحركة ، والبعد الذي يكون في المادة ، وهو بعد الجسم يكون قابلا للحركة لأجل أن الشرط في كون البعد قابلاً للحركة ، كونه في المادة ؟ نقول : هذا السؤال باطل من وجهين الأول: سنقيم الدلالة على أن البعد يمتنع أن يقال : إنه حال في محل ، وأنه صفة لشيء آخر . [ والثاني (٥) ] وهـو أنا نقول : هب أن البعد يعقل كونه حالاً في محل ، إلا أنا نقول : ذلك المحل من حيث إنه هو ، إما أن يكون [ شاغلاً للحيز والجهة (٦) ] وإما أن لا يكون كذلك ، بل يكون من الموجودات المجردة عن الوضع والإشارة . والأول باطل لأنه يقتضي أن يكون محل البعد بعداً ، فإن افتقر البعد إلى محل ، فليفتقر ذلك المحل لكونه بعداً إلى محل آخر ، ولزم التسلسل ، وهو محال . وأما الثاني فهو باطل أيضاً ، لأن كل موجود يكون مجرداً عن الإشارة والحيز والجهة ، فإنه يمتنع عليه الانتقال والحركة. لأن الانتقال والحركة عبارة عن الحصول في حيز ، بعد أن كان حاصلاً في حيز آخر ، وهذا إنما يعقل في حق الشيء الذي يصح عليه كونه حاصلاً في الحيز والجهة ، أما الشيء الذي يمتنع كونه حاصلاً في الحيز والجهة ،
If it is said: why is it not permissible to say that the abstract dimension—which is space—is not movable, while the dimension that is in matter, which is the dimension of the body, is movable because the condition for a dimension to be movable is its being in matter? We say: this question is invalid in two respects. The first: we will establish the proof that it is impossible for dimension to be said to subsist in a subject and to be an attribute of something else. [And the second (5)] is that we say: suppose that dimension is conceivable as subsisting in a subject, but we say: that subject, in itself, either occupies space and direction (6) or is not like that, but rather is among existents abstracted from position and indication. The first is false because it would require that the subject of dimension be a dimension; for if dimension needs a subject, then that subject, being a dimension, would need another subject, leading to an infinite regress, which is impossible. And the second is also false, because every existent that is abstracted from indication, space, and direction cannot undergo transition and motion. Because transition and motion are expressions for obtaining in a space after having obtained in another space, and this is only conceivable for something that can obtain in space and direction. As for that which cannot obtain in space and direction...
Source pages p. 138

Commentary
This passage is an objection (fa-in qīla) and answer (naqūlu) to the third argument. The objector distinguishes between abstract dimension (al-bu'd al-mujarrad) and dimension in matter, claiming only the latter is movable. The author rejects this with two points. First, he will prove that dimension cannot subsist in a subject at all. Second, even if it could, the subject of dimension would have to be either extended (occupying space) or abstract. If extended, it is itself a dimension, causing infinite regress. If abstract, it cannot move because motion requires spatial location, which abstract entities lack. The answer is cut off but the reasoning is clear.

Key Terms
بعد مجرد
بعد في مادة
محل
حركة
تسلسل
مجرد عن الإشارة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that dimension in matter is fundamentally different from abstract dimension, making it movable.
Correction: The author shows that even if dimension could be an attribute, the subject of dimension must itself either be a dimension (leading to infinite regress) or non-dimensional (which cannot move). Thus the distinction does not salvage the objection.

Checkpoint
According to the answer, what happens if the subject of dimension is itself a dimension?
A) It becomes immaterial
B) It leads to an infinite regress
C) It becomes movable
D) It becomes abstract

Key Takeaway
The author shows that the distinction between abstract and material dimension does not save the objection because dimension cannot be an attribute of anything without contradiction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever accepted a belief because it seemed convenient or plausible, only to later realize that it leads to an infinite regress or contradiction? What was that belief, and how did you resolve it?
?
The author shows that even a hypothetical concession leads to absurdity. Is there an area of your faith where you have allowed a 'what if' to linger without examining its logical consequences? Will you trace it to its end today?
?
When you encounter an objection to core Islamic beliefs, do you dismiss it quickly or do you engage with it as thoroughly as the author does here? What would it cost you to take up the burden of proof for your own convictions?

The Argument from Simple Parts and Its Implications
The author presents an argument about simple parts and its consequences for the nature of space.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how an argument about simple parts leads to the conclusion that space is a dimension and that a void exists outside the world.

Core Idea
Simple parts imply infinite regress of places, forcing a rethinking of space.

In this passage, the author presents a clever argument about the nature of simple parts and draws out its radical implications. He shows that if simple parts exist, they lead to an impossible regress, which then forces us to accept that celestial spheres can be torn, that there is a void outside the world, and that space is a dimension. This argument is crucial for understanding the classical debate about the nature of space and the cosmos.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the two-part structure: first the argument, then the implications.
2
Understand the initial argument about simple parts
Focus on the reasoning: every simple part has distinct right and left, so it is divisible into halves that are identical in essence. These halves can swap positions, which would require a new place for each half, leading to an infinite regression of places.
The author uses this to show that the assumption of simple parts leads to an impossibility.
3
See how impossibility follows
The transfer of halves demands another place, and that place would itself be subject to the same reasoning, creating an infinite chain. Since infinite regress is impossible, the initial assumption (simple parts) is problematic.
The impossibility here is a logical impossibility.
4
Follow the chain of implications
The author then says: if this argument is correct (for the opponents), it implies that the celestial spheres can be torn, separated, and ruptured. This further implies a place outside the convex sphere, which must be a void, and thus space is a dimension.
Each step follows from the previous: tearing → place → void → space as dimension.
5
Reflect on the author's conclusion
The author ends by stating that these premises need to be established. Consider what it means to 'establish' them: the argument is conditional, and the author may be pointing out what must be accepted if the argument is valid.
This is a meta-commentary on the status of the argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فهناك أجزاء بسيطة، وكل جزء بسيط فإن يمينه غير يساره، فيكون منقسماً، فيكون هذان النصفان [من ذلك الجزء البسيط] متساويين في تمام الماهية، يصح أن ينتقل كل واحد منهما إلى وضع الآخر وموضعه، فحينئذ يعود ما ذكرناه من افتقار المكان إلى مكان آخر، وحينئذ يلزم المحال.
واعلم أن هذه الحجة [قد وضعناها] للقوم، إلا أنا نقول: إنها إن صحت فهي توجب جواز الخرق والتفرق والتمزق على جملة الأفلاك، وذلك يوجب إثبات مكان خارج الفلك المحدب للجهات، وذلك يوجب إثبات الخلاء خارج العالم، وذلك يوجب كون المكان بعداً. فهذه مقدمات لا بد من تقريرها.
There are simple parts, and every simple part has its right not its left, so it is divisible, so these two halves [of that simple part] are equal in complete essence; it is valid for each of them to transfer to the other's position and place; then what we mentioned about the dependence of place on another place returns, and thus impossibility follows.
And know that this argument [we have set] for the people, except we say: if it is correct, it necessitates the possibility of tearing, separation, and rupture upon the whole of the celestial spheres, and that necessitates establishing a place outside the convex sphere for directions, and that necessitates establishing void outside the world, and that necessitates that space is a dimension. These are premises that must be established.
Source pages p. 140

Commentary
This passage presents an argument about simple parts and then draws out its implications. First, the author argues that if there are simple parts (presumably of the celestial sphere), each simple part has distinct right and left sides, so it is divisible into halves that are identical in essence. Then it is possible for each half to move to the other's position, which would require another place, leading to an infinite regress (which is impossible). The author then notes that this argument, which they have formulated for the opponents, if valid, implies that the celestial spheres can be torn apart, which necessitates the existence of a place outside the convex sphere (the outermost sphere), and that place must be a void, and thus space is a dimension. The author then concludes that these are premises that need to be established.

Key Terms
أجزاء بسيطة
الخرق
التفرق
التمزق
الفلك
الخلاء
البعد
المكان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Simple parts are indivisible atoms with no internal structure.
Correction: This argument shows that even simple parts have distinct sides (right/left), implying they are divisible. The argument uses this to critique the notion of simple parts.

Checkpoint
What does the author claim follows if the argument about simple parts is correct?
A) The celestial spheres are eternal.
B) Space is a dimension.
C) Simple parts are indivisible.
D) The void does not exist.

Key Takeaway
The argument from simple parts forces us to accept a void outside the cosmos if we reject an infinite regress of places.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a logical argument that leads to conclusions you find uncomfortable (like the possibility of void or tearing of spheres), do you tend to reject the argument or adjust your worldview? What does that reveal about your attachment to preconceived notions? Will you commit to following truth wherever it leads, even if it disturbs your current understanding?
?
The author presents a conditional argument: 'if it is correct, then...' How often do you trace out the implications of your own beliefs, even if they lead to conclusions you did not expect? Today, pick one of your deeply held beliefs and consider: If it were true, what must also be true? Are you willing to accept those consequences, or will you ignore them?
?
Consider your belief about the nature of space. Do you believe space is an infinite void or a finite container? This argument suggests a radical view. Are you prepared to rethink something as fundamental as space based on reasoned argument? What would it cost you to change your mind on such a foundational issue?

First Premise: The Celestial Sphere Must Be Capable of Tearing
First premise with case analysis of simple vs composite sphere.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first premise that argues the celestial sphere, whether simple or composite, must be capable of tearing and separation.

Core Idea
The celestial sphere's composition forces the possibility of tearing.

The first premise tackles whether the celestial sphere that defines directions can undergo tearing. By analyzing whether it is simple or composite, we see that in both cases, parts can exchange places, leading to the possibility of tearing. This sets the foundation for the argument that space is a dimension.

Guided Path
1
Read the first premise passage
Carefully read the Arabic passage for the first premise and its translation. Notice how the author sets up a conditional (if simple… if composite).
2
Understand the two cases
Identify the two cases: the sphere is either simple (all parts identical) or composite (parts of different natures). See how each case leads to the conclusion that tearing is possible.
The argument uses the idea that if parts are identical, they can swap positions; if composite, they resolve to simple parts which also can swap.
3
Reflect on the necessity
Ask yourself why this premise is needed. It establishes that the celestial sphere is not inherently indivisible, supporting the idea that space beyond may be dimensional.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما المقدمة الأولى: وهي قولنا: إن هذا الكلام يوجب صحة الخرق والتفرق والتمزق، على الأفلاك. فتقريرها أن نقول: هذا الفلك المحدد للجهات، إما أن يكون بسيطاً، وإما أن يكون مركباً. فإن كان بسيطاً، كانت كل قطعة تفرض فيه مساوية في تمام الماهية للقطعة الأخرى، وذلك يقتضى جواز أن ينتقل كل واحد من تلك [القطع إلى وضع القطعة الأخرى، وإلى موضعها]، وذلك يقتضي جواز التفرق والتمزق [على الفلك] وهو المطلوب. وأما إن كان هذا الفلك مركباً [من أجزاء مختلفة الطبائع فنقول: إنه لا بد وأن ينتهي تحليل تركيبها إلى أجزاء بسيطة، فنقول: وكل واحد من تلك البسائط فإن يمينه مغايرة ليساره، وطبيعة كل واحد من هذين القسمين واحدة، فحينئذ يلزم صحة أن ينقلب كل واحد من ذينك القسمين إلى موضع الآخر، وإلى وضعه. وذلك يوجب قبول الحرق والتفرق والتمزق، فيثبت: أن هذه الحجة توجب صح الخرق والتمزق على الفلك].
وأما المقدمة الثانية وهي أن صحة الانخراق [على الفلك] توجب كون أجزائه قابلة للحركة المستقيمة، فهذه أيضاً مقدمة جلية، لأن الانخراق لا يتم إلا بتباعد كل واحد من تلك الأجزاء المفترضة في الفلك [عن الآخر] على نعت الاستقامة.
وأما المقدمة الثالثة وهي في بيان أن ذلك يوجب كون المكان بعداً. فهو أنا نقول: تلك الأمكنة الحاصلة خارج العالم. إن كانت أجساماً فحينئذ يعود التقسيم فيها، وذلك يوجب وجود أجسام لا نهاية لها، وهو محال. وإن لم تكن أجساماً، وجب كون تلك الأمكنة أبعاداً خالية، وذلك هو المطلوب.
فيثبت: أن الحجة المذكورة إن صحت، دلت على أن المكان هو البعد.
As for the first premise, which is our statement: that this speech necessitates the validity of tearing, separation, and rupture upon the celestial spheres. Its formulation is that we say: This celestial sphere that determines directions is either simple or composite. If it is simple, then every imagined piece in it is equal in complete essence to the other piece, and that necessitates the validity that each of those [pieces] transfer to the position of the other piece and to its place, and that necessitates the validity of separation and rupture [upon the sphere], which is the desired. And if this sphere is composite [of parts of different natures, we say: then its composition must necessarily resolve into simple parts; we say: and each of those simple parts has its right different from its left, and the nature of each of these two divisions is one; then it follows the validity that each of those two divisions flips to the position of the other and to its place. And that necessitates the acceptance of tearing, separation, and rupture, so it is established: that this argument necessitates the validity of tearing and rupture upon the sphere].
As for the second premise, which is that the validity of tearing [upon the sphere] necessitates that its parts are capable of rectilinear motion; this too is a clear premise, because tearing is only completed by the separation of each of those assumed parts in the sphere [from the other] in a rectilinear manner.
As for the third premise, which is in explaining that that necessitates that space is a dimension. It is that we say: Those places occurring outside the world: if they are bodies, then the division returns in them, and that necessitates the existence of infinite bodies, which is impossible. And if they are not bodies, then those places must be empty dimensions, and that is the desired.
Thus it is established: that the mentioned argument, if correct, indicates that space is the dimension.
Source pages p. 140

Commentary
This passage explains three premises needed to prove that space is a dimension. The first premise argues that the celestial sphere, which determines directions, must be either simple or composite. If simple, any piece is identical to another, allowing transfer and thus tearing. If composite, it resolves into simple parts, which also allow transfer and tearing. Thus the argument necessitates the possibility of tearing the sphere. The second premise is straightforward: tearing implies that parts can move rectilinearly away from each other. The third premise argues that the places outside the world (the void) cannot be bodies because that would lead to infinite bodies; therefore they must be empty dimensions. From these premises, the conclusion is that if the argument holds, space is a dimension.

Key Terms
المقدمة الأولى
بسيط
مركب
الخرق
التفرق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does the first premise conclude about the celestial sphere?
A) It must be simple.
B) It must be composite.
C) It must be capable of tearing.
D) It determines directions.

Key Takeaway
The celestial sphere, whether simple or composite, necessarily admits the possibility of tearing and separation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The celestial sphere, whether simple or composite, cannot avoid the possibility of being torn. Your own heart—your habits, your attachments—might feel fixed and unchanging. But if even the heavens are subject to change, what false stability are you relying on that prevents you from letting Allah 'tear' away sin from your life? What one habit would you ask Him to tear out of you today?
?
The argument proves that change is possible, not that it will happen. It confronts you with the reality that you are contingent and that your current state is not permanent. Are you living as if your spiritual state is a given, or do you actively depend on the One who can tear and recreate? What specific prayer will you make tonight to ask Allah to change your heart?

Second Premise: Tearing Implies Rectilinear Motion
Second premise linking tearing to rectilinear motion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second premise which states that the possibility of tearing the celestial sphere implies its parts can undergo rectilinear motion.

Core Idea
Tearing requires rectilinear separation of parts.

The second premise is a clear and simple step: if the celestial sphere can be torn, then its parts must be able to move away from each other in a straight line. This premise connects the first premise to the need for a dimensional space.

Guided Path
1
Read the short second premise
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice it is presented as a clear (جلية) premise.
2
Understand the connection
Tearing means separation. Separation of parts in a sphere would occur along straight lines (the shortest path). So rectilinear motion is necessary.
This is almost self-evident to the author.
3
Consider why this matters
Rectilinear motion implies direction and extension, which points to space being dimensional.

Source Text

Third Premise: The Void Must Be Empty Dimensions
Third premise concluding that void is empty dimensions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third premise which argues that the places outside the world (the void) cannot be bodies and therefore must be empty dimensions, establishing space as a dimension.

Core Idea
The void is not body; it is empty dimension.

The third premise uses a dilemma to determine the nature of the space outside the world. If it were bodies, we would have an infinite regress of bodies, which is impossible. Therefore, it must be empty dimensions, i.e., space itself is dimensional.

Guided Path
1
Read the third premise and conclusion
Read the Arabic and translation. Note the two alternatives: either the empty places are bodies or not.
2
Understand the impossibility of infinite bodies
If the places are bodies, then those bodies themselves would occupy space, leading to an infinite chain of bodies. This is considered impossible in kalam.
3
Grasp the desired conclusion
The only remaining possibility is that these places are not bodies but empty dimensions. This directly proves that space is a dimension.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما المقدمة الأولى: وهي قولنا: إن هذا الكلام يوجب صحة الخرق والتفرق والتمزق، على الأفلاك. فتقريرها أن نقول: هذا الفلك المحدد للجهات، إما أن يكون بسيطاً، وإما أن يكون مركباً. فإن كان بسيطاً، كانت كل قطعة تفرض فيه مساوية في تمام الماهية للقطعة الأخرى، وذلك يقتضى جواز أن ينتقل كل واحد من تلك [القطع إلى وضع القطعة الأخرى، وإلى موضعها]، وذلك يقتضي جواز التفرق والتمزق [على الفلك] وهو المطلوب. وأما إن كان هذا الفلك مركباً [من أجزاء مختلفة الطبائع فنقول: إنه لا بد وأن ينتهي تحليل تركيبها إلى أجزاء بسيطة، فنقول: وكل واحد من تلك البسائط فإن يمينه مغايرة ليساره، وطبيعة كل واحد من هذين القسمين واحدة، فحينئذ يلزم صحة أن ينقلب كل واحد من ذينك القسمين إلى موضع الآخر، وإلى وضعه. وذلك يوجب قبول الحرق والتفرق والتمزق، فيثبت: أن هذه الحجة توجب صح الخرق والتمزق على الفلك].

فيثبت: أن الحجة المذكورة إن صحت، دلت على أن المكان هو البعد.
As for the first premise, which is our statement: that this speech necessitates the validity of tearing, separation, and rupture upon the celestial spheres. Its formulation is that we say: This celestial sphere that determines directions is either simple or composite. If it is simple, then every imagined piece in it is equal in complete essence to the other piece, and that necessitates the validity that each of those [pieces] transfer to the position of the other piece and to its place, and that necessitates the validity of separation and rupture [upon the sphere], which is the desired. And if this sphere is composite [of parts of different natures, we say: then its composition must necessarily resolve into simple parts; we say: and each of those simple parts has its right different from its left, and the nature of each of these two divisions is one; then it follows the validity that each of those two divisions flips to the position of the other and to its place. And that necessitates the acceptance of tearing, separation, and rupture, so it is established: that this argument necessitates the validity of tearing and rupture upon the sphere].
As for the second premise, which is that the validity of tearing [upon the sphere] necessitates that its parts are capable of rectilinear motion; this too is a clear premise, because tearing is only completed by the separation of each of those assumed parts in the sphere [from the other] in a rectilinear manner.
As for the third premise, which is in explaining that that necessitates that space is a dimension. It is that we say: Those places occurring outside the world: if they are bodies, then the division returns in them, and that necessitates the existence of infinite bodies, which is impossible. And if they are not bodies, then those places must be empty dimensions, and that is the desired.
Thus it is established: that the mentioned argument, if correct, indicates that space is the dimension.
Source pages p. 140

Commentary
This passage explains three premises needed to prove that space is a dimension. The first premise argues that the celestial sphere, which determines directions, must be either simple or composite. If simple, any piece is identical to another, allowing transfer and thus tearing. If composite, it resolves into simple parts, which also allow transfer and tearing. Thus the argument necessitates the possibility of tearing the sphere. The second premise is straightforward: tearing implies that parts can move rectilinearly away from each other. The third premise argues that the places outside the world (the void) cannot be bodies because that would lead to infinite bodies; therefore they must be empty dimensions. From these premises, the conclusion is that if the argument holds, space is a dimension.

Key Terms
الأبعاد الخالية
المكان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the void is filled with bodies or that it is nothingness.
Correction: The third premise shows the void cannot be bodies due to infinite regress, and it must be empty dimensions—i.e., space itself.

Checkpoint
According to the third premise, why can't the places outside the world be bodies?
A) Because they are too far away.
B) Because that would imply infinite bodies, which is impossible.
C) Because bodies cannot exist outside the world.
D) Because they are empty dimensions.

Key Takeaway
The void outside the world must be empty dimensions, establishing that space is a dimension.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you imagine the vastness beyond the observable universe, do you truly accept that it is not empty nothingness but real, created dimensions? How does this shape your awareness of Allah's boundless creative power? What will you do today to let this reality deepen your reverence for Him?
?
The argument shows that assuming the void is a body leads to logical impossibility. Are there beliefs you hold about the unseen reality that, upon reflection, lead to contradictions? How willing are you to let go of such assumptions and submit to what revelation teaches? What is one assumption you will consciously examine and surrender today?
?
If all of creation, including empty space, exists only because Allah wills it to be, does your daily life reflect the weight of that dependence? How can you, in the next hour, remind yourself that even the space you occupy is a sign of His existence and order? What specific action will you take to turn this truth into worship?

The Fifth Argument: Void Cannot Be Finite or Infinite
The author presents a dilemma argument dividing void into finite and infinite.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical structure of the fifth argument against space being a dimension, specifically how it rules out both an infinite and a finite void.

Core Idea
A void would have to be finite or infinite—but both lead to absurdity.

In this argument, the author presents a clean dilemma: if space were an independent void, it would be either infinite or finite. Both possibilities are shown to be impossible, thereby refuting the notion that space is a dimension. This lesson walks through the two prongs of the argument and their consequences.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic original and its literal translation. Notice the logical structure: a conditional with two branches.
The passage ends mid-sentence; we only have the first part of the finite branch analysis.
2
Identify the two possibilities
The author divides the hypothetical void into 'infinite' and 'finite'. Understand why these two categories cover all possibilities.
3
Examine the infinite branch
The author says proving the impossibility of an infinite void will be done later. For now, accept that it is impossible based on future proofs.
This is a deferred proof; the focus here is on the logic of the division.
4
Examine the finite branch
The finite void is argued to have a boundary and thus a shape. The author then starts questioning the cause of that shape.
The argument is incomplete here, but the direction is clear: a finite void would require a shape, leading to further problems.
5
Summarize the argument
Combine the two prongs: void must be finite or infinite; both are impossible; therefore void does not exist as a dimension.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الخامسة على أن المكان لا يجوز أن يكون هو البعد:
أن نقول: لو ثبت هذا الفضاء، لكان إما أن يكون غير متناهي، وإما أن يكون متناهياً، والقسمان باطلان، فكان القول بإثبات هذا الفضاء باطلاً. وإنما قلنا: إنه يمتنع كونه غير متناهي، وذلك لأنا سنقيم الدلائل [القاهرة] على أن القول بوجود بعد لا نهاية له: محال. وإنما قلنا: إنه يمتنع كونه متناهياً، وذلك أن كل متناه فإنه يحيط به حد [أو حدود] وكل ما كان كذلك فهو شكل. ينتج: أن كل بعد متناه، فله شكل. فنقول: المقتضى لذلك الشكل، إما أن يكون [هو نفس طبيعة البعد. أو أمراً حالاً فيها. أو ما يكون محلا لها. أو ما لا يكون حالاً فيها ولا يكون محلا لها. والأقسام]
The fifth argument that space is not permissible to be a dimension:
That we say: If this void were established, it would be either infinite or finite; and both divisions are false, so the statement of establishing this void is false.
And we said: It is impossible for it to be infinite, because we will establish conclusive proofs that the statement of the existence of infinite dimension is impossible.
And we said: It is impossible for it to be finite, because every finite is surrounded by a boundary [or boundaries], and everything that is so is a shape. So it results: that every finite dimension has a shape. Then we say: The cause of that shape is either [the very nature of the dimension, or an accident subsisting in it, or what is a locus for it, or what is neither an accident in it nor a locus for it. And the divisions]
Source pages p. 141

Commentary
This passage begins the fifth argument against the view that space is an independent dimension (void). The argument divides the void into two possibilities: infinite or finite. The author declares both are false. The impossibility of an infinite void is deferred to later proofs. For the finite case, the author argues that any finite thing is bounded, and anything bounded has a shape. Thus a finite void would have a shape. The author then starts to examine the cause of that shape, listing possibilities: the nature of the dimension itself, an accident in it, a locus for it, or something that is neither. The argument is incomplete due to the page ending, but the general approach is to show that a finite void would entail a shape, which leads to further absurdities.

غير متناهي

Checkpoint

A) It must be infinite.
B) It must have a boundary and thus a shape.
C) It must be a body.
D) It must be empty of all accidents.

Key Takeaway
The fifth argument uses a dilemma: the void, if it existed, would be either infinite or finite, but both are impossible; therefore the void does not exist.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This argument shows that holding both options (finite and infinite) for the void leads to contradiction. Where in your own beliefs do you accept two opposing ideas without resolving the conflict? Are you willing to examine them carefully, even if it means letting go of a cherished view?
?
The author defers the proof for why an infinite dimension is impossible. How often do you accept religious or philosophical claims based on trust in scholars without demanding evidence yourself? Will you commit today to verifying at least one foundational belief with the same rigor as this argument?
?
If every finite thing has a shape and a cause, what does that imply about your own existence—that you are bounded and contingent? How does the thought that you are not self-sufficient shape your attitude toward the Creator?

The First Proof: Place Persists When a Body Is Removed
Introduction to two groups and first proof for place as abstract dimension

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first proof for the view that place is an abstract dimension, using the example of a mountain's removal to demonstrate that space is distinct from the body that occupies it.

Core Idea
Space remains when a mountain is removed – proving place is not identical to body.

Al-Razi begins his chapter on proving that place is dimension by distinguishing two groups of proponents. He then presents the argument of the first group, who rely on necessary knowledge. Their key proof uses a simple observation: if a mountain is removed, the space it occupied remains, and we can point to that same space later. This lesson walks through that proof step by step.

Guided Path
1
Understand the two groups of proponents
Al-Razi divides those who believe place is dimension into two: those who claim necessary knowledge and those who use rational proof. Focus on the first group's argument.
The passage first states this division.
2
Grasp the core investigation
The argument asks: when we say a body is 'here' or 'there', is that referring to the body itself or to something else? The first group says it's something else.
This sets up the proof.
3
Examine the mountain example
Consider a mountain present in a certain direction. If that mountain is completely removed, does the direction cease to exist? No – the direction remains, as shown by building a house there later.
Sound intellect judges the house is in the same place as the mountain was.
4
Draw the conclusion
Since the direction persists after the body's removal, direction (place) must be distinct from the body. This proves that place is not identical to the body.
This is the first proof.
5
Reflect on the proof's basis
Notice that the proof does not assume void or imaginary spaces – it just uses the persistence of spatial reference as a fact. This makes it a strong intuitive argument.
It is a proof from necessary knowledge, according to Al-Razi.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الرابع في تقرير الوجوه التي عليها يعول من يعتقد أن المكان هو البعد. اعلم أن القائلين بهذا المذهب فريقان: منهم من يدعي العلم الضروري بأن المكان ليس إلا هذا الفضاء، وإلا هذه الأبعاد المجردة. ومنهم من يثبت هذه الدعوى بالحجة والبرهان. أما الفريق الأول: فتقرير كلامهم أن يقال: إنا نعلم بالضرورة أن الجسم يمكن أن يشار إليه بأنه حصل ههنا، ويمكن أن ينتقل من هناك إلى ههنا، وبالعكس. فوجب علينا أن نبحث أن المشار إليه بأنه ههنا وهناك هل هو نفس الجسم الحاصل ههنا وهناك، أو هو أمر مغاير لذات ذلك الجسم؟ أما القسم الثاني فهو المطلوب. وأما القسم الأول فهو باطل وتقريره من وجوه: الأول: إنا نشاهد أن هذا الجبل حاصل ههنا بعينه، فإذا فرضنا أن ذلك الجبل انقلع من تلك الجهة، ومن ذلك الحيز، فبعد انقلاع ذلك الجبل من هناك، بقيت تلك الجهة، وتلك المكانات، كما كانت، وما بطلت وما عدمت. حتى إنهم لو بنوا داراً في ذلك الحيز، صار يحكم كل عقل سليم، بأن هذه الدار حصلت في الجهة التي كان ذلك الجبل حاصلا فيها. وذلك يدل على أن الحيز والجهة، أمر مغاير للجسم، الذي كان حاصلا فيه.
Chapter Four: On Establishing the Considerations on which relies the one who believes that place is dimension. Know that those who hold this doctrine are two groups: some claim necessary knowledge that place is nothing but this space, and these abstract dimensions. And some prove this claim by argument and proof. As for the first group: the exposition of their statement is that we say: we know by necessity that a body can be pointed to as having come to be here, and can transfer from there to here, and vice versa. So it is necessary for us to investigate whether that which is pointed to as 'here' and 'there' is the same as the body that came to be here and there, or is something other than the essence of that body? The second division is what is sought; as for the first division, it is false, and its exposition is through several aspects: First: We observe that this mountain is present here itself. So if we suppose that mountain is removed from that direction and from that space, after its removal from there, that direction and those places remain as they were, and are not nullified and are not annihilated. So much so that if they built a house in that space, every sound intellect would judge that this house came to be in the direction in which that mountain was present. And that indicates that space and direction are something other than the body that was present in it.
Source pages p. 144

Commentary
This passage is an introduction and first proof within a chapter on establishing the arguments for those who believe that place is abstract dimension. The author, Fakhr al-Din al-Razi, first divides the proponents into two groups: those who claim necessary knowledge and those who use rational proof. He then expounds the argument of the first group. The argument begins with the observation that we know by necessity that a body can be pointed to as being 'here' and can move from 'there' to 'here'. This leads to an investigation: is the 'here' or 'there' the body itself or something else? They claim the second option is what they want, and the first is false. The first proof against the identity of place and body is given: we see a mountain present in a certain place. If we imagine that mountain is completely removed from that direction and space, the direction and place remain unchanged, not eliminated. If a house is then built in that same space, sound intellects judge that the house is in the same direction where the mountain was. This shows that space and direction are distinct from the body that occupied them. The proof relies on common observation and the persistence of spatial reference after body removal.

Key Terms
المكان (place)
الفضاء (space)
الحيز (space)
الجهة (direction)
الجبل (mountain)
العقل السليم (sound intellect)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This proof assumes the possibility of void (empty space).
Correction: The proof does not assume void; it only uses the observed fact that spatial reference remains after a body is removed, without committing to the nature of that space.

Checkpoint
What does the first proof conclude from the example of the mountain?
A) The mountain is identical to its place.
B) The place is distinct from the mountain.
C) The mountain's place is an accident.
D) The mountain's place is imaginary.

Key Takeaway
The persistence of space after a body's removal shows that place is not identical to the body but is an abstract dimension.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider the stability of your daily surroundings—the chair you sit on, the room you inhabit—do you ever reflect that the space itself is a distinct creation of Allah, independent of the objects that fill it? How might this awareness deepen your sense of Allah's power over all things, seen and unseen?
?
If a mountain can be removed and its place remains, what does that tell you about the impermanence of physical attachments? Is there a 'mountain' in your life—a status, possession, or relationship—that you are clinging to, while ignoring the enduring space Allah has set for your soul? What would it take for you to trust that after removal, the purpose remains?
?
Al-Razi's proof relies on 'every sound intellect' recognizing that the house occupies the same direction as the mountain. How does your own intellect judge the unseen dimensions of Allah's creation? Are you willing to let go of a physical certainty to affirm a spiritual reality, just as the proof affirms the persistence of space beyond the removal of bodies?

The Qibla Proof: Space is Distinct from Bodies
The author argues that the qibla remains the same space even after the Kaaba is removed.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that space (al-jiha/al-hayyiz) is distinct from bodies, based on the qibla example.

Core Idea
Space remains even when bodies are removed — the qibla proves it.

In this passage, the author presents a powerful argument that space is not merely a property of bodies. Using the example of the qibla — the direction of prayer — he shows that even if the Kaaba is completely destroyed and all its materials removed, the qibla remains the same empty space. This observation, he claims, is confirmed by sound intellect and innate disposition.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify key terms
Read the Arabic and translation once, noting terms like قبلة، جهة، حيز، خلاء، ملاء، فضاء. These are central to the argument.
The term 'milā' (ملاء) refers to plenum, the opposite of void.
2
Understand the two scenarios
The passage considers two beliefs: one allowing void (khalā'), and one denying void (requiring plenum). Notice how both lead to the conclusion that the qibla is a specific space, not the bodies or air that occupy it.
3
Grasp the conclusion
The passage concludes that sound intellects testify that direction and locus are distinct from moving bodies. Reflect on why this matters for proving the reality of space as an independent entity.
This argument supports the idea that space is not just a property of matter.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إنه لما ثبت أن القبلة هي الكعبة ، فعند هذا قالت الفقهاء : لو انهدمت الكعبة ، ونقل من هناك ما كان من الخشب والحجر والتراب بالكلية ، ولم يبق إلا الفضاء الصرف ، كانت القبلة ليست إلا ذلك الفضاء . ولو اعتقد معتقد بتجويز حصول الخلاء في داخل العالم ، لقال بأن القبلة هو ذلك القدر من الخلاء المعين ، ومن الفضاء المعين. ومن اعتقد أن الخلاء محال ، وأن الملاء واجب فإنه لا بد ، وأن يعتقد أن عند خراب ذلك البناء ، لا بد أن ينتقل إليه هواء ، حتى لا يحصل الخلاء . ثم إن القبلة ليست ذلك الهواء لأن ذلك الهواء قد ينتقل عن ذلك الحيز . ويتبدل بهواء آخر ، والقبلة ليست إلا تلك الجهة ، وإلا ذلك الحيز . وهذا يدل على أن العقول السليمة ، والفطرة الصحيحة ، شاهدة بأن الجهة والحيز ، أمر مغاير للأجسام المنتقلة المتحركة .
Indeed, when it was established that the qibla is the Kaaba, then the jurists said: if the Kaaba collapses and what was there of wood, stone, and dirt is completely removed, and nothing remains except pure space, then the qibla is nothing but that space. And if a person who holds the possibility of void within the world were to believe, he would say that the qibla is that specific amount of void and that specific space. And whoever believes that void is impossible and that plenum is necessary, then he must believe that at the destruction of that building, air must move into it so that void does not occur. Then the qibla is not that air, because that air may move away from that locus and be replaced by another air, and the qibla is nothing but that direction and that locus. And this indicates that sound intellects and sound innate disposition testify that direction and locus are something distinct from moving, changing bodies.
Source pages p. 145

Commentary
This passage presents a proof (dalīl) that space (al-jiha or al-hayyiz) is distinct from bodies. The argument uses the example of the qibla (the direction of prayer). When the Kaaba is destroyed and all its materials are removed, the jurists still consider the same empty space to be the qibla. If one believes in the possibility of void, that space is a specific void; if one believes void is impossible, air fills the space, but that air can move and be replaced. Yet the qibla remains the same direction/locus, not the air. Thus, sound intellects and innate disposition judge that direction and locus are something other than the bodies that occupy them.

Key Terms
القبلة
الجهة
الحيز
الخلاء
الملاء
الفضاء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Space is just a property of bodies and disappears when they are removed.
Correction: The qibla argument shows that even after the Kaaba's materials are removed, the qibla remains as empty space, proving space is an independent entity.

Checkpoint
In the qibla argument, what happens to the qibla when the Kaaba is destroyed and all materials are removed?
A) It becomes invalid.
B) It shifts to the nearest mosque.
C) It remains the same empty space.
D) It becomes the air that fills the space.

Key Takeaway
Direction and locus (space) are distinct from bodies, as shown by the permanence of the qibla even after the physical structure is gone.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the qibla points to the Kaaba and remains even after the building is gone, does your heart's direction toward Allah remain when the physical comforts of worship are removed? When you lose the outward structures of religion (a mosque, a teacher, a community), does your inner direction stay fixed, or does it drift? This lesson proves that space is constant — but is your spiritual qibla as constant?
?
The qibla is not the air that moves and changes. What 'air' have you been mistaking for your qibla — fleeting feelings, changing circumstances, worldly attachments? The passage forces you to distinguish between the permanent direction and the temporary fillers. What must you strip away so that you stand in prayer only toward the One, not toward the shifting winds of your desires?
?

Is your commitment to Allah's command tied to the external 'building' of your life — your health, wealth, status? If those were completely removed, would you still know the direction to face? Answer honestly: when your life feels demolished, do you still face Allah, or do you become lost in the rubble?

Fourfh Proof: Location vs Contact
Sanusi argues location is not reducible to contact.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fourth proof that spatial location is an attribute distinct from physical contact or enclosure.

Core Idea
Location is independent of other bodies.

This lesson presents a concise but powerful argument from the author. By examining what we can and cannot conceive about a body, we uncover that location ('here' and 'there') is not simply a relation to other bodies but a distinct attribute. This proof helps solidify the kalam concept of place (al-makan) as a real entity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic original and its translation carefully. Notice the two key claims: what we can conceive and what we cannot.
The passage is brief but packed with meaning.
2
Identify the two conceivability claims
The author states we can conceive a body without any other body touching or encompassing it. But we cannot conceive a body without it being 'here' or 'there'.
3
Understand the conclusion
The author concludes that the meaning of 'here'/'there' must be distinct from contact or enclosure. This establishes location as a real attribute.
The Arabic term 'mugāyir' (مغاير) means 'distinct' or 'different'.
4
Reflect on the significance
Think about why this matters: if location were just a relation, then an isolated body would have no location. But our conception shows otherwise. This supports the kalam view that place is a real entity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الرابع : وهو أنه يمكننا أن نتصور جسماً لا يماسه جسم ، ولا يحيط به جسم، ولا يمكننا أن نتصور جسماً لا يصدق عليه أنه ههنا أو هناك ، فوجب أن يكون المعنى المشار إليه بقولنا ههنا وهناك : أمر مغاير لكون الجسم ممسوس جسم آخر ، أو كونه بحيث يحيط به جسم آخر .
The fourth aspect: that we can conceive a body that no body touches and no body encompasses, but we cannot conceive a body of which it is not true that it is here or there. So it is necessary that the meaning referred to by our saying 'here' and 'there' is something distinct from the body being touched by another body or being such that another body encompasses it.
Source pages p. 146

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth proof. It states that we can imagine a body completely isolated, untouched and not surrounded by any other body. However, we cannot imagine a body without it being 'here' or 'there'. Therefore, the meaning of 'here' and 'there' is not the same as the body's contact with or enclosure by another body; rather, it is something distinct—namely, the spatial location itself.

Key Terms
يماسه
يحيط به
ههنا
هناك
مغاير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think spatial location is merely a relation between bodies (e.g., being touched or surrounded).
Correction: This proof shows location is independent of other bodies; we can conceive a body without any contact, yet it still has a location.

Checkpoint
What does the fourth proof claim we can conceive?
A) A body without a location.
B) A body without contact with any other body.
C) A body without any attribute.
D) A body without extension.

Key Takeaway
Location is a real attribute of a body, distinct from its relations to other bodies.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In this lesson, you learned that location is an essential attribute of a body, separable from contact with others. Reflect on your own life: what attributes of your faith are essential and cannot be removed? Are your religious practices like contact with others—meaningful but separable—or are they essential to your identity as a Muslim? Commit to one practice you will make so essential that you cannot conceive of yourself without it.
?
Sanusi argues that we can conceive a body without any external contact or enclosure, yet it still has a location. Are there areas in your life where you rely on the approval or presence of others to feel 'located' spiritually? If all human support vanished, would you still know where you stand with Allah? Write one concrete action you will take this week to ground your identity in Allah alone.

Fifth Proof: The Water in the Pitcher
The author argues that the space in a pitcher remains distinct from its contents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the example of water in a pitcher demonstrates that space (al-imtidād) is distinct from the bodies that occupy it.

Core Idea
Space remains the same while bodies change, showing space is distinct from bodies.

This lesson presents the fifth proof from Al-Matalib al-Aliyah, using the everyday example of water in a pitcher. The author clarifies a subtle point: 'in the pitcher' does not mean water soaks into the pitcher's material, but that it touches the inner surface. Reason then judges that the water occupies the space inside. The proof is that this space persists identically through changes—sometimes water, sometimes air, sometimes emptiness—indicating that the extension of space is different from the extension of bodies.

Guided Path
1
Understand the clarification
Notice that 'water in the pitcher' means water's surface touches the pitcher's inner surface, not that water permeates the pitcher's material.
This distinction is crucial to avoid misunderstanding.
2
Grasp the rational judgment
Plain reason judges that water occupies the space (al-faḍā') supposed between the two sides of the pitcher.
3
Analyze the evidence
The same space remains identical while three different states occur: water fills it, air fills it, or (for void-accepters) emptiness.
This shows the space is constant despite changing contents.
4
Draw the conclusion
Since the space persists through these changes, it must be distinct from the bodies that temporarily occupy it.
This proves that space (al-imtidād) is a separate entity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الخامس : هو أنا نقول : بأن الماء في الكوز ، لا بمعنى كون الماء سارياً في جسم الكوز ، بل بمعنى كون الماء مماس بسطحه سطح الكوز . وصريح العقل حاكم بأن الماء حاصل في الفضاء المفترض بين طرفي الكوز بدليل : أن ذلك الفضاء باقي بعينه واحد بشخصه ثم تارة يحصل فيه الماء ، وأخرى يخرج منه الماء ، وينتقل إليه الهواء ، وثالثاً يبقى الفضاء خالياً عن كل الأجسام عند من كانت الدلائل القطعية عنده على صحة القول بالخلاء ، وذلك يدل على أن ذلك الامتداد أمر مغاير لامتداد الأجسام الحاصلة في ذلك الفضاء
The fifth aspect: that we say: indeed, the water in the pitcher is not in the sense of water flowing in the body of the pitcher, but in the sense of water touching the surface of the pitcher with its surface. And plain reason judges that water occurs in the space supposed between the two sides of the pitcher, evidenced by the fact that that space remains the same identical one; then sometimes water occurs in it, another time water leaves it and air moves into it, and thirdly the space remains empty of all bodies for those who possess decisive proofs for the validity of void. And that indicates that that extension is distinct from the extension of the bodies occurring in that space.
Source pages p. 146

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth proof, using the example of water in a pitcher (kūz). The author clarifies that 'water in the pitcher' does not mean water flowing within the pitcher's material, but that the water's surface touches the pitcher's inner surface. Reason judges that the water occupies the space between the pitcher's walls. The proof is that this space remains the same 'in itself' (bi-aynihi) through changes: sometimes water fills it, then water leaves and air enters, and (for those who accept void) it can even be empty. Since the space persists while bodies change, the space (al-imtidād) is distinct from the bodies' extensions.

Correction: The author clarifies it means water's surface touches the pitcher's inner surface, so it occupies the interior space.

Checkpoint
What evidence does the fifth proof use to show space is distinct from bodies?
A) Water freezes and expands.
B) The pitcher changes shape.
C) The same space can hold water, air, or be empty.
D) The pitcher's material is porous.

Key Takeaway
The space (imtidād) inside the pitcher remains identical while bodies change, proving it is distinct from bodies.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You just read a rational proof that space itself is real and distinct from the bodies that fill it. Do you live as though only what is physical and tangible matters? What would change if you truly believed the unseen realities—like space—are also created by Allah and have meaning? Will you pause today to reflect on the createdness of the very space you occupy?
?
The argument hinges on the fact that the same space remains while water and air come and go. Consider your own life: your body, wealth, and relationships come and go, but your soul (ruh) and your deeds persist. Have you invested more in what changes or what remains? Commit to one action today that prioritizes what is eternal over what is temporary.
?
This proof uses plain reason (ṣarīḥ al-ʿaql) to deduce a truth about reality. Are you willing to let your intellect—guided by revelation—lead you to accept difficult truths about Allah's creation? Or do you dismiss rational arguments when they challenge your assumptions? Take a moment: what is one assumption you hold that you have never critically examined with reason and revelation?

Conclusion: Space as Distinct from Bodies
The author concludes that necessary knowledge confirms space is distinct from bodies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the six proofs yield necessary (self-evident) knowledge that spatial dimensions (loci, directions, alignments) are distinct from the dimensions of physical bodies.

Core Idea
Necessary knowledge confirms space is real and distinct from bodies.

This brief passage serves as the author's conclusion to a series of six proofs. He asserts that these proofs do not merely suggest or argue—they produce necessary knowledge (al-ʿilm al-ḍarūrī), meaning knowledge that is self-evident and undeniable. The conclusion is that spatial concepts like loci, directions, and alignments are extended dimensions that exist independently of the dimensions of moving bodies. This is a critical metaphysical claim: space is a real, non-physical framework.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic text and its translation once. Notice the concise, definitive tone of the conclusion.
The term 'تنبيهات' means 'indications' or 'warnings'—the author is pointing to truths.
2
Identify the key claim
Find the phrase 'العلم الضروري حاصل'. This asserts that the knowledge gained is necessary, not speculative. Understand why this matters: it elevates the proof beyond mere reasoning to self-evidence.
The word 'حاصل' means 'attained' or 'present'.
3
Understand the spatial terms
The passage lists three spatial concepts: الأحياز (loci), الجهات (directions), and المحاذيات (alignments). They are described as أبعاد ممتدة (extended dimensions). Recognize that these are the building blocks of the author's concept of absolute space.
These terms refer to the spatial framework in which bodies exist and move.
4
Grasp the distinction
The passage concludes that these spatial dimensions are مغايرة (distinct) from the dimensions of moving bodies. This means space is not just a property of bodies but an independent reality.
This distinction is crucial for arguments about motion and direction.
5
Reflect on the import
Ask yourself: Why does it matter that this knowledge is necessary? If it were merely probable, further proof might be needed. But as necessary, it forms a foundation for subsequent arguments in physics and theology.
Necessary knowledge is indubitable and serves as a starting point.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فهذه تنبيهات دالة على أن العلم الضروري حاصل بأن هذه الأحياز والجهات والمحاذيات أبعاد ممتدة ، وأنها مغايرة لأبعاد الأجسام المتحركة المتنقلة
These are indications showing that necessary knowledge is attained that these loci, directions, and alignments are extended dimensions, and that they are distinct from the dimensions of moving, translocating bodies.
Source pages p. 146

Commentary
This short concluding passage summarizes the previous six proofs (تنبيهات, 'warnings' or 'indications'). It states that they demonstrate that necessary knowledge (al-ʿilm al-ḍarūrī) confirms that loci (aḥyāz), directions (jihāt), and alignments (muḥādhayāt) are extended dimensions (abʿād mumtadda) and are distinct from the dimensions of bodies that move and change location.

Correction: The author explicitly states that the proofs produce necessary (self-evident) knowledge, not merely probable conclusions.

Checkpoint

C) It is speculative.
D) It is based on authority.

Key Takeaway
The six proofs yield necessary knowledge that space (loci, directions, alignments) is a set of extended dimensions distinct from the dimensions of physical bodies.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
If space is distinct from bodies, then no physical place can contain your connection to Allah. Are there places you have attached your spiritual state to, thinking that being there brings you closer to Him? How does this lesson free you from such spatial attachments? Reflect on a specific attachment and decide to detach your heart from it.
