Refuting the Definition of Rest as Remaining Contiguities (Part 1: Initial Objection)
Al-Razi critiques two definitions of rest and appeals to self-evident knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the definition of rest as permanence of contiguities is flawed and how a self-evident example disproves it.

Core Idea
Rest is not defined by contiguity; even moving objects can have unchanged contiguities.

Al-Razi begins by attacking a common definition of rest: that it is the permanence of a body's contiguities (musāmanāt) with others. He shows that this definition fails because contiguity can remain even when everything is in motion, as in the example of a falling box. He also warns against defining rest as remaining in a fixed space, which leads to circular reasoning. Finally, he appeals to self-evident knowledge: a bird stopping in air or a stone in water is clearly at rest, regardless of surrounding motion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice the structure: first an objection to the definition, then a clarification, then a direct appeal to necessity.
2
Identify the two flawed definitions
Find where al-Razi criticizes the definition of rest as permanence of contiguities, and also the alternative definition involving space (faḍāʾ).
He shows the first is not conditioned on essential rest, and the second leads to circularity.
3
Understand the self-evident example
Reflect on the bird and stone example. Why does al-Razi call denying their rest 'obstinacy against self-evident truths'?
This establishes that rest is known by direct intuition, not by a relational definition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إلا أن السكون بهذا التفسير غير مشروط بكونه في نفسه ساكناً . ألا ترى أن الصندوق إذا وضع فيه جسم، وفرضنا هوى ذلك الصندوق مع كل ما فيه ، فإن مسامتة تلك الأجسام الموضوعة في الصندوق مع جوانب ذلك الصندوق باقية غير متغيرة ، مع أن ذلك الصندوق، وكل ما فيه متحرك . فعلمنا : أن مسامتة ذلك الطير، وذلك الحجر مع سائر الأجسام ، لا يمنع من كونه في نفسه متحركاً . وإن عنيت بكونه ساكناً أنه بقي في ذلك الفضاء الذي هو حاصل فيه ومداخل له . فهذا الكلام إنما يتم بعد ثبوت القول بالفضاء ، فلو أثبتنا القول بالفضاء بهذه المقدمة لزم الدور وهو محال .

والجواب : أما أن الطير قد يقف في الهواء ، مع أن الهواء يمر عليه ، والحجر قد يقف في الماء مع أن الماء يجري عليه . فذلك معلوم بالضرورة ، والمنع من كونه ساكناً في هذه الحالة مكابرة في البديهيات .

وأما تفسير السكون ببقاء المسامنات فهو باطل . ويدل عليه وجوه :

الأول : إن الجسمين المتساويين في الثقل والحجم والشكل ، إذا ابتدأ بالنزول من رأس الجبل في هواء صاف لا تفاوت فيه البتة ، فههنا يبقى سمت كل واحد منهما مع الآخر ، من أول هذه الحركة إلى أخرها . فههنا المسامتة باقية مع كون كل واحد منها متحركاً ، فعلمنا : أنه لا يمكن تفسير كون الجسم ساكناً ببقاء مسامنته مع غيره .

الثاني : إن الفلك الحاوي، والفلك المحوي، إذا كانا يتممان الدورة في مدة واحدة ، فهناك لا يتغير شيء من المسامات الحاصلة بين الأجزاء المفترضة في كل واحد منهما ، مع كون كل واحد منهما متحركاً في نفسه .

الثالث : إن الجسمين من بقيا في ذاتيهما على السكون كان سكونها في ذاتيهما علة لبقاء المسامتة الحاصلة بينهما ، وذلك لأن سكونه في ذاته أمر حاصل له في ذاته ، وأما مسامته مع غيره فهي أمر يحصل له بالنسبة إلى غيره . وما ثبت للشيء بذاته قبل ما يثبت له بسبب غيره ، كان سكونه في ذاته أمراً مغايراً لبقاء مسامتاته مع غيره .

الرابع : إنه يمكننا أن نعقل كون الشيء ساكناً في ذاته ، مستقراً في حيزه الذي هو فيه ، حال كوننا غافلين عن كل ما سواه . وأما بقاء مسامنته مع غيره ، فهذا لا يمكن تعقله إلا مع تعقل غيره . فعلمنا : أن كونه ساكناً في ذاته أمر مغاير لبقاء مسامناته مع غيره .
However, rest (sukun) according to this interpretation is not conditioned upon its being in itself at rest. Do you not see that if a body is placed in a box, and we suppose the falling of that box with everything in it, then the contiguity (musāmatāt) of those bodies placed in the box with the sides of that box remains unchanged, even though the box and everything in it are in motion. So we know that the contiguity of that bird and that stone with other bodies does not prevent it from being in itself in motion. And if you mean by its being at rest that it remains in that space (faḍāʾ) in which it exists and enters it, then this statement is only complete after affirming the claim of space. If we affirm the claim of space by this premise, circularity (dawr) would occur, which is impossible.

The answer: As for the bird stopping in the air while the air passes over it, and the stone stopping in water while water flows over it, that is known by necessity (ḍarūra), and denying that it is at rest in this situation is obstinacy (mukābara) against self-evident truths.

As for the interpretation of rest as the permanence of contiguities (baqāʾ al-musāmanāt), it is false. Evidence for this:

First: If two bodies equal in weight, size, and shape begin descending from the top of a mountain in pure air with no variation whatsoever, then the alignment (samt) of each one with the other remains from the beginning of this motion to its end. Here the contiguity remains while each of them is in motion. So we know that it is impossible to interpret a body being at rest by the permanence of its contiguity with another.

Second: If the encompassing sphere (al-falak al-ḥāwī) and the encompassed sphere (al-falak al-maḥwī) complete a revolution in the same period, then none of the contiguities between the supposed parts in each of them changes, while each of them is in itself in motion.

Third: If two bodies remain in themselves at rest, their being at rest in themselves is the cause of the permanence of the contiguity between them, because its being at rest in itself is an attribute accruing to it in itself, while its contiguity with another is an attribute accruing to it in relation to another. And what is established for a thing by itself before what is established for it due to another — its being at rest in itself is distinct from the permanence of its contiguities with another.

Fourth: It is possible for us to conceive a thing being at rest in itself, settled in its own location (ḥayyiz), while we are oblivious to everything else. As for the permanence of its contiguity with another, this cannot be conceived except with the conception of that other. So we know that its being at rest in itself is distinct from the permanence of its contiguities with another.
Source pages p. 148

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the definition of rest (sukun) as 'the permanence of contiguities' (baqāʾ al-musāmanāt). The author begins by pointing out that this definition is not conditioned on the body being essentially at rest; an example of a box falling with its contents shows that contiguity can remain while everything moves. If one instead defines rest as remaining in a particular space, that assumes the existence of space (faḍāʾ) which leads to circular reasoning. The author then gives a direct answer: birds stopping in air and stones in water are known by necessity to be at rest, and denying that is obstinacy.

Next, the author declares the interpretation of rest as permanence of contiguities false and provides four proofs:
1. Two identical bodies falling together maintain contiguity while both move, so contiguity cannot define rest.
2. Concentric spheres completing a revolution together maintain contiguity while both move.
3. If two bodies are at rest in themselves, their intrinsic rest is the cause of contiguity; what is intrinsic is prior to relational, so rest is distinct from contiguity.
4. One can conceive a thing at rest without conceiving its contiguity with others, proving rest is an intrinsic state, not a relational one.
The argument establishes rest as an inherent state, not a relational property.

Key Terms
سكون
مسامتة
فضاء
دور
مكابرة
بديهيات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Rest is defined solely by unchanged contiguity with surrounding bodies.
Correction: Rest is an intrinsic state of a body, not a relational property; contiguity can remain even during motion.

Checkpoint
What does the falling box example demonstrate?
A) That rest is the same as contiguity.
B) That contiguity can remain while everything moves.
C) That space is necessary for rest.
D) That motion is impossible without rest.

Key Takeaway
Rest cannot be defined by contiguity or space; it is known directly through self-evident experience.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you try to define your faith, do you fall into circular reasoning, assuming what you want to prove? How can you ground your certainty in self-evident truths like the author does?
?
Are there areas of your life where you deny obvious truths because they conflict with your desires or worldview, committing 'mukābara' against your own conscience?
?
The author says that knowing a bird is at rest in moving air is self-evident. What is the most basic, self-evident truth about your soul that you have been ignoring, and what would it cost you to accept it?

Refuting the Definition of Rest as Remaining Contiguities (Part 2: Four Proofs)
Al-Razi gives four proofs against defining rest as permanence of contiguities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand four independent proofs that rest is an intrinsic state, not the permanence of contiguities.

Core Idea
Rest is intrinsic, not relational; it can be conceived without contiguity.

After the initial refutation, al-Razi presents four clear proofs that the definition of rest as permanence of contiguities is false. Each proof shows that contiguity can persist during motion, that rest is distinct from contiguity, and that rest can be conceived independently. These proofs reinforce the idea that rest is an inherent property of a body, not a relation to others.

Guided Path
1
Read the four proofs in Arabic and translation
Read each proof one by one. Notice that each proof is introduced with a number (الأول, الثاني, الثالث, الرابع).
2
Understand the first proof
The first proof uses two identical bodies falling together. Their alignment (samt) remains constant, yet both move. This shows contiguity is not rest.
3
Understand the second proof
The second proof uses concentric spheres rotating together. Their contiguities remain unchanged while both move.
4
Grasp the third and fourth proofs
The third proof establishes that intrinsic rest is the cause of contiguity; the fourth shows rest can be conceived without contiguity. Both prove rest is distinct from contiguity.
Pay attention to the logical priority arguments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

First: If two bodies equal in weight, size, and shape begin descending from the top of a mountain in pure air with no variation whatsoever, then the alignment (samt) of each one with the other remains from the beginning of this motion to its end. Here the contiguity remains while each of them is in motion. So we know that it is impossible to interpret a body being at rest by the permanence of its contiguity with another.

Second: If the encompassing sphere (al-falak al-ḥāwī) and the encompassed sphere (al-falak al-maḥwī) complete a revolution in the same period, then none of the contiguities between the supposed parts in each of them changes, while each of them is in itself in motion.

Third: If two bodies remain in themselves at rest, their being at rest in themselves is the cause of the permanence of the contiguity between them, because its being at rest in itself is an attribute accruing to it in itself, while its contiguity with another is an attribute accruing to it in relation to another. And what is established for a thing by itself before what is established for it due to another — its being at rest in itself is distinct from the permanence of its contiguities with another.

Fourth: It is possible for us to conceive a thing being at rest in itself, settled in its own location (ḥayyiz), while we are oblivious to everything else. As for the permanence of its contiguity with another, this cannot be conceived except with the conception of that other. So we know that its being at rest in itself is distinct from the permanence of its contiguities with another.
Source pages p. 148

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the definition of rest (sukun) as 'the permanence of contiguities' (baqāʾ al-musāmanāt). The author begins by pointing out that this definition is not conditioned on the body being essentially at rest; an example of a box falling with its contents shows that contiguity can remain while everything moves. If one instead defines rest as remaining in a particular space, that assumes the existence of space (faḍāʾ) which leads to circular reasoning. The author then gives a direct answer: birds stopping in air and stones in water are known by necessity to be at rest, and denying that is obstinacy.

Next, the author declares the interpretation of rest as permanence of contiguities false and provides four proofs:
1. Two identical bodies falling together maintain contiguity while both move, so contiguity cannot define rest.
2. Concentric spheres completing a revolution together maintain contiguity while both move.
3. If two bodies are at rest in themselves, their intrinsic rest is the cause of contiguity; what is intrinsic is prior to relational, so rest is distinct from contiguity.
4. One can conceive a thing at rest without conceiving its contiguity with others, proving rest is an intrinsic state, not a relational one.
The argument establishes rest as an inherent state, not a relational property.

Key Terms
سكون
مسامتة
سمت
فلك حاوي
فلك محوي
ذاتي
حيز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Rest is identical to permanence of contiguities.
Correction: Rest is an intrinsic state; contiguity is a relational byproduct that can exist even when bodies move.

Checkpoint
According to the fourth proof, why is rest distinct from permanence of contiguities?
A) Because contiguity cannot exist without rest.
B) Because rest can be conceived without conceiving contiguity.
C) Because contiguity is a cause of rest.
D) Because rest and contiguity are the same thing.

Key Takeaway
Rest is an intrinsic attribute of a body, not a relational property dependent on contiguity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you feel internally restless, do you tend to search for external causes—people, circumstances, or things—to explain it? How might the insight that rest is intrinsic change where you look for peace?
?
Al-Razi proves that rest can be conceived without any reference to another. Is there an area of your life where you are waiting for external confirmation before you allow yourself to be still? What would it take to declare that stillness is yours regardless of others?
?
If rest is an intrinsic attribute of a body, and you are more than a body, what does that say about the source of your spiritual calm? Are you trying to secure your peace through 'contiguities'—relationships, achievements, approval—or from within your own soul's orientation to Allah?

The Objection Against Place as Surface
Al-Razi addresses an objection about place as surface and motion by assumption.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical objection that arises when place is defined as the containing surface, and the initial attempt to resolve it by distinguishing essential motion from assumed motion.

Core Idea
Defining place as surface leads to paradox: moving objects would be judged at rest.

Al-Razi presents a powerful objection against those who define place (makān) as the inner surface of a container. If that were true, then when a bag of flour moves from Rayy to Khurasan, the flour's place—the inner surface—remains unchanged, so the flour should be considered at rest. Yet we know it moved. The opponent tries to escape by saying the flour moves 'by assumption' rather than essentially, but al-Razi begins to dissect this ambiguous phrase.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the concrete example of flour in a bag moving from Rayy to Khurasan.
This is a classic ad absurdum argument.
2
Identify the contradiction
The objection shows that if place is surface, then the flour's place (the inner bag surface) remains in contact, so the flour must be at rest. But we know it moved. This is a contradiction.
3
Understand the opponent's response
The opponent introduces 'motion by assumption' (mutaharrik bi-l-fard) to avoid saying the flour moves essentially. Al-Razi calls this 'ambiguous' and begins to analyze it.
The response is cut off, but note the beginning of al-Razi's rebuttal.
4
Reflect on the ambiguity
Consider why 'motion by assumption' might be insufficient. What does it mean to say something moves 'by assumption'? The passage hints that the flour actually ended up in Khurasan, so it did move.
Even though the passage ends, you can anticipate that al-Razi will argue that the flour moved essentially.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما قوله : إنه لو كان المكان عبارة عن السطح ، لزم أن نحكم على المتحرك حال كونه متحركاً بكونه ساكناً . فتقريره : أنا إذا وضعنا شيئاً من الدقيق في جراب . فعند القائلين بأن المكان هو السطح يكون مكان ذلك الدقيق هو السطح الباطن من ذلك الجراب ، المماس للسطح الظاهر من ذلك الدقيق . فإذا فرضنا أنه نقل ذلك الجراب من الري إلى خراسان فقد انتقل ذلك الدقيق من بلد إلى بلد ، مع أن المماسة الحاصلة بين دينك السطحين باقية ثابتة غير متغيرة. فلو لم يكن المكان إلا السطح ، لوجب الحكم على ذلك الدقيق بأنه بقي ساكناً ، مع أنا نعلم بالضرورة أنه انتقل من الري إلى خراسان ، وذلك باطل .

فإن قالوا : إن ذلك الدقيق الذي في ذلك الجراب ، لا نقول : إنه متحرك بالذات ، بل نقول : إنه متحرك بالفرض . فالجواب أن نقول : إن قول القائل هذا الجسم متحرك بالفرض : لفظة مبهمة لا بد من البحث عن معناها. فنقول : لا شك أنا حين جعلنا هذا الدقيق في الجراب ، حين كنا في الري ، فقد كان هذا الدقيق حاصلاً في الري ، فإذا جئنا بذلك الجراب إلى خراسان ، فلا شك أن ذلك الدقيق حصل في خراسان ، فذلك الدقيق الذي كان في الري ، إما أن يقال : إنه في نفسه لم ينتقل ولم يتحرك ، بل بقي
And as for his statement: 'If place (makān) were the surface, then it would be necessary to judge a moving body, while it is moving, as being at rest.' Its explanation: When we put some flour in a bag, according to those who say that place is the surface, the place of that flour is the inner surface of the bag which touches the outer surface of the flour. If we suppose that bag is transported from Rayy to Khurasan, then that flour has been transported from one country to another, while the contact (mumāssa) between those two surfaces remains unchanged. So if place were nothing but the surface, it would be necessary to judge that flour as having remained at rest, while we know by necessity that it moved from Rayy to Khurasan, and that is false.

Then if they say: 'Concerning that flour in the bag, we do not say it is in motion essentially (bi-dhāt), rather we say it is in motion by assumption (bi-l-farḍ).' The answer is to say: The statement of the speaker 'this body is in motion by assumption' is an ambiguous term (lafẓa mubhama) that must be investigated. We say: There is no doubt that when we placed this flour in the bag, when we were in Rayy, this flour existed in Rayy. Then when we brought that bag to Khurasan, there is no doubt that the flour came to exist in Khurasan. Concerning that flour which was in Rayy, either it is said that in itself it did not transfer and did not move, but remained...
Source pages p. 149

Commentary
This passage presents an objection against the definition of place as surface. The author (likely al-Razi) explains the objection: if place is defined as the inner surface of a container, then when flour is in a bag and the bag moves from Rayy to Khurasan, the flour's place (the inner surface) remains in contact, so the flour would be judged at rest, but we know it moved—a contradiction.

The opponent then responds by distinguishing between motion essentially (bi-dhāt) and motion by assumption (bi-l-farḍ). The author begins to rebut this by calling the term 'motion by assumption' ambiguous and starts to clarify: the flour was in Rayy and then in Khurasan, so either it didn't move in itself—the text cuts off. The incomplete answer suggests the author is about to argue that the flour did indeed move essentially, but the conclusion is missing from this page.

This passage is an objection-and-answer unit, though the answer is incomplete, exhibiting how a problem arises from defining place as surface and how the opponent tries to escape by invoking 'motion by assumption'.

Three absurd consequences of defining space as containing surface
Al-Razi enumerates three objections to the surface definition of space and states the correct definition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand three absurd consequences that arise from defining space as the containing surface, and why al-Razi argues for space as dimension instead.

Core Idea
Defining space as surface leads to contradictions; space must be a dimension in which bodies exist.

Al-Razi challenges a common definition of space by showing it leads to impossible consequences. Through three concrete cases—a cube, a waterskin, and a cube with hollows—he demonstrates that identifying space with the containing surface cannot be correct. This lesson walks through each objection and the alternative definition that avoids them.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation to grasp the overall argument. Notice the structure: three objections followed by the correct view.
The passage is dense; read slowly and refer back to the commentary if needed.
2
Examine each of the three objections
For each case (cube, waterskin, cube with hollows), identify how the surface definition leads to a contradiction between the located body and its space.
The three cases show: space increases without body change, space stays while body decreases, and space increases as body decreases.
3
Understand the alternative definition
Al-Razi concludes that space is the dimension in which the body's essence subsists, which avoids all these absurdities. Reflect on why this definition resolves the contradictions.
This is the positive argument for the correct definition of space.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بها عندما تجعل مكعبة فههنا المتمكن باقي بحاله، مع أن المكان ازداد عند التكعيب. وأما الإلزام الثاني: وهو أن يبقى المكان بحاله مع انتقاص المتمكن فنقول: الماء الذي في القربة مكانه هو السطح الداخل من القربة، فإذا عصرنا القربة حتى فاض بعض ما كان فيها من الماء، بقي سطح القربة محيطاً بما بقي فيها من الماء. فههنا المتمكن قد انتقص مع أن المكان باقي كما كان. وأما الإلزام الثالث: المتمكن قد ينتقص والمكان يزداد فنقول: تقريره: إنا إذا فرضنا مكعباً متساوي السطوح، فإن مكانه هو السطح المحيط به، فإذا أحدثنا في كل واحد من جوانبه نقراً عميقة واسعة، فههنا انتقض جرم المتمكن، ولزم من انتقاصه ازدياد السطوح المحيطة به. فلو كان المكان عبارة عن السطوح المحيطة بالشيء، لزم في هذه الصورة أن يقال: انتقاص ذات المتمكن أوجب ازدياد مكانه. ومعلوم أن كل هذه اللوازم باطلة فاسدة. أما إذا قلنا: المكان هو البعد الذي ينفد فيه ذات الجسم لم يلزم عليه شيئاً من هذه المنكرات والمحالات.
By it when it is made cubic, here the located (mutamakkin) remains in its state, while the space (al-makan) increased at the cubing. As for the second objection: that the space remains in its state while the located decreases, we say: the water in the waterskin – its space is the inner surface of the waterskin; so if we squeeze the waterskin until some of the water in it overflows, the surface of the waterskin remains surrounding what remains of the water. Here the located has decreased while the space remains as it was. As for the third objection: the located may decrease and the space increases, we say: its formulation: if we suppose a cube of equal surfaces, then its space is the surface surrounding it; so if we make in each of its sides deep wide hollows, here the body (jirm) of the located decreases, and from its decrease follows an increase of the surfaces surrounding it. So if space were the surfaces surrounding the thing, it would be necessary in this case to say: the decrease of the located's essence caused the increase of its space. And it is known that all these necessary consequences are false and corrupt. But if we say: space is the dimension (al-buʿd) in which the essence of the body penetrates, none of these objectionable and impossible things follow from it.
Source pages p. 152

Commentary
This passage presents three objections (ilzāmāt) against the view that space (al-makan) is the containing surface (al-sath al-hawi). The author is refuting that definition by showing it leads to absurdities. First case: a body is made cubic (transformed into a cube) while remaining the same body; the space increases because the surface area increases, but the body itself hasn't changed – so space is not identical to the surface. Second case: water in a waterskin; when squeezed, the water decreases but the inner surface remains the same – space stays constant while the located decreases. Third case: a cube with deep hollows carved into it; the body's volume decreases but the surrounding surface area increases – so space increases as the located decreases. The author concludes that these consequences are false (bāṭila fāsida), meaning the surface definition must be false. He then states the correct view: space is the dimension (al-buʿd) in which the body's essence subsists, and this view avoids all these absurdities.

Key Terms
مكان (makan)
متمكن (mutamakkin)
سطح (sath)
بعد (buʿd)
إلزام (ilzam)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Space is merely the inner surface that contains a body.
Correction: Al-Razi shows this leads to logical contradictions, as demonstrated by three cases where the relationship between body and space becomes inconsistent.

Checkpoint
Which of the three cases involves a decrease in the located body causing an increase in the space?
A) The case of making a cube
B) The case of the waterskin
C) The case of the cube with hollows
D) All three cases

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi's three objections show that defining space as the containing surface leads to logical absurdities, supporting the view that space is the dimension in which a body exists.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Al-Razi shows that an intuitive definition leads to contradictions under scrutiny. What is one belief you hold about yourself or your faith that seems obvious, yet you have never examined its logical consequences? Will you test it by asking what would happen if it were false?
?
The waterskin analogy: the water decreases but the surface remains. In your own spiritual journey, is there a 'surface'—a routine, a label, an outward practice—that remains unchanged while your inner devotion has shrunk? What would it take to let your definition of piety be shaped by the reality of your heart, not by the container of habit?

Fourth Argument: Solitary Body Proves Space ≠ Surface
Al-Razi presents a syllogistic proof that the place (hayyiz) of a body is not its containing surface.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how imagining a solitary body demonstrates that the concept of location (hayyiz) is distinct from the concept of being surrounded by a surface.

Core Idea
A body alone still has location, proving space is not just the surface around it.

Al-Razi's fourth argument uses a simple thought experiment: imagine a single body with nothing else. Even then, the mind must assign it a 'here' or 'there'. This shows that location is an intrinsic property separable from being contained by surfaces.

Guided Path
1
Set up the thought experiment
Imagine a single body with nothing else existing alongside it. Ask yourself: does the mind still conceive of it as being 'here' or 'there'?
This forces us to separate location from external relations.
2
Distinguish necessary from accidental attributes
Notice that the judgment of location is necessary—you cannot conceive the body without it. But being touched or surrounded is accidental; you can ignore it.
This contrast forms the core of the argument.
3
Grasp the syllogism's conclusion
Since location is always present even without surroundings, the meaning of 'being here' is different from 'being surrounded'. Therefore, the hayyiz is not the containing surface.
The conclusion is that space is an independent reality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الرابعة: إنا إذا تخيلنا جسماً واحداً، وتخيلنا أنه وجد وليس معه غيره، فإنه لا بد وأن يحكم العقل عليه بأنه ههنا أو هناك فيثبت: أن المعنى المعقول من قولنا: الجسم ههنا أو هناك: أمر لا يقدر الذهن على تصور وجود الجسم إلا مع تصور حصوله. وأما كون الجسم ممسوس جسم أو محاط جسم آخر فأمر يتمكن العقل من تصور الجسم جسماً مع الذهول عنه. وهذا القياس ينتج إنتاجاً ضرورياً: أن المعقول من كون الجسم ههنا أو هناك مغاير للمعقول من كونه بحيث يحيط به غيره. فيثبت: أن الحيز الذي حكم العقل بكون الجسم حاصلاً فيه مغاير للسطح الحاوي والله أعلم.
The fourth proof: If we imagine a single body, and we imagine that it exists and nothing else is with it, then the mind must judge that it is here or there. So it is established that the intelligible meaning (al-maʿnā al-maʿqūl) of our saying 'the body is here or there' is something that the mind cannot conceive the existence of the body except with the conception of its occurrence. As for the body being touched by a body or surrounded by another body, that is something that the mind can conceive the body as a body while being heedless of it. This syllogism yields a necessary conclusion: that the intelligible meaning of the body being here or there is different from the intelligible meaning of it being such that something else surrounds it. So it is established that the locus (al-hayyiz) in which the mind judges the body to be present is different from the containing surface. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 152

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth argument (al-ḥujja al-rābiʿa) to refute the definition of space as the containing surface. The author asks us to imagine a single body with nothing else coexisting. Even in that solitary state, the mind necessarily judges that the body is 'here' or 'there' – i.e., it has a location (hayyiz). This judgment is inseparable from the concept of the body's existence; we cannot think of the body without also thinking of it as being in some place. On the other hand, we can conceive of a body without thinking about whether it is touched or surrounded by other bodies – that is accidental. Therefore, the meaning of 'being here' is not the same as 'being surrounded by a surface'. The conclusion is that the hayyiz (space/locus) that the mind affirms for the body is distinct from the containing surface (al-sāṭḥ al-ḥāwī).

Key Terms
حيز (hayyiz)
سطح حاو (sath hawi)
معنى معقول (ma'na ma'qul)
قياس (qiyas)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Location is the same as being in contact with surroundings.
Correction: This argument shows they are conceptually distinct because a solitary body still has location even without any contact.

Checkpoint
According to the fourth argument, what does the mind necessarily judge about a solitary body?
A) That it is touched by something
B) That it is here or there
C) That it is surrounded by surface
D) That it has no location

Key Takeaway
The necessity of assigning location to a solitary body proves that hayyiz is a distinct concept from being surrounded by a surface.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Does this awareness of your own location make you realize that even in solitude, you are placed by a Creator? How does this shape your gratitude for the very space you occupy right now?
?
If the mind cannot conceive a body without location, what does that say about the necessity of space for your own existence? Does this drive you to ponder the One who created space and placed you within it? Will you pause today to thank Allah for the simple fact that you exist somewhere, not nowhere?
?
Al-Razi argues that being surrounded by other bodies is not essential to your existence—you are first a body with a location. How much of your sense of identity is tied to the people and things around you? If all of them were suddenly removed, would you still know who you are as a being located by Allah? What change in your heart would that realization bring?

Motion and Rest Prove Space is Not Surface
Al-Razi proves that motion and rest require a space distinct from surfaces.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the necessary attributes of motion and rest demonstrate that space (al-hayyiz) is not identical to the containing surface (al-sath al-hawi).

Core Idea
Motion and rest are defined by change of locus, not by surface contact.

This passage builds on earlier arguments by presenting a new proof from reason: every body must be either moving or at rest, but it need not be touching any surface. By analyzing these two necessary attributes, al-Razi shows that the concept of motion and rest are fundamentally tied to a locus (hayyiz) that is separate from surfaces. This strengthens the conclusion that space is not merely the containing surface.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation to get an overview of the argument.
Notice the logical structure: premise, distinction, definition, conclusion.
2
Identify the key premise
Al-Razi states that reason requires every body to be either moving or at rest, but not necessarily in contact with a surface. Understand why this distinction is important.
3
Follow the definitions
Learn the definitions of motion as transition from one locus to the next, and rest as settling in one locus. See how these definitions imply that the locus is distinct from surfaces.
The definition uses the term 'hayyiz' which is the object of this proof.
4
Connect to the conclusion
Recognize that the argument proves that the locus where the body settles or moves is not the same as the containing surface. This is the desired conclusion.
This is the maṭlab (the sought conclusion).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومما يؤكد ذلك: أن صريح العقل يحكم بأن الجسم حال وجوده لا بد وأن يكون إما متحركاً وإما ساكناً. وصريح العقل لا يحكم بأنه يجب أن يكون إما مماساً لسطح واحد أو منتقلاً من مماسة سطح آخر. لأن العقل يمكنه تصور الجسم بحيث لا يماسه شيء من السطوح أصلاً. فيثبت: أن المعقول من كونه متحركاً وساكناً مغاير للمعقول من كونه مماساً لسطح واحد أو منتقلاً من مماسة سطح إلى مماسة سطح آخر. وإذا ثبت هذا فنقول: المعقول من الحركة هو الانتقال من الحيز إلى الحيز الذي يليه، والمعقول من السكون هو الاستقرار في الحيز الواحد، وهذا يقتضي أن سكون هذا الحيز الذي يستقر الجسم فيه تارة وينتقل منه إلى غيره أخرى مغايراً للسطح الحاوي. وذلك هو المطلوب.
And what confirms that is: that clear reason (ṣarīḥ al-ʿaql) judges that the body, at the moment of its existence, must be either moving or at rest. And clear reason does not judge that it must be either touching one surface or transferring from touching one surface to another. Because the mind can conceive of the body such that nothing of surfaces touches it at all. So it is established that the intelligible meaning of it being moving and at rest is different from the intelligible meaning of it being touching one surface or transferring from touching a surface to touching another surface. And when this is established, we say: the intelligible meaning of motion (al-ḥaraka) is the transition from one locus (al-ḥayyiz) to the next locus, and the intelligible meaning of rest (al-sukūn) is the settling in one locus. And this requires that the locus in which the body settles sometimes and from which it transfers to another at other times is different from the containing surface. And that is what is sought.
Source pages p. 153

Commentary
This passage provides a supporting argument (wa mimmā yu'akkidu dhālik) for the conclusion that space is not the containing surface. The author notes that reason requires every existing body to be either moving (mutaharrik) or at rest (sākin). These are necessary attributes. But reason does not require the body to be in contact with a surface; we can conceive of a body that touches no surface at all. Therefore, the concepts of motion and rest are not the same as the concepts of being in contact with surfaces. Then he defines motion as transition from one locus (hayyiz) to another, and rest as remaining in one locus. This implies that the locus (hayyiz) itself is something distinct from the containing surface, because the body can be in that locus without touching any surface, and it can move from one locus to another independently of surface contacts. Hence the locus (space) is not the containing surface.

Key Terms
حركة (haraka)
سكون (sukun)
حيز (hayyiz)
مماسة (mumassa)
عقل (aql)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Many think that motion and rest are defined by a body's contact with surfaces (e.g., moving means changing which surfaces it touches).
Correction: Al-Razi shows that motion and rest are defined by change or stability of locus (hayyiz), not by surface contact. A body could move without touching any surface.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the intelligible meaning of motion?
A) Transferring from one surface to another
B) Transition from one locus to the next
C) Change in shape
D) Increase in size

Key Takeaway
Motion and rest are defined by the locus (hayyiz), proving that locus is a distinct reality from surfaces.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You move and rest every day without thinking. Does the fact that motion and rest require a distinct locus—something beyond surfaces—change how you see the world as created with wisdom? What would it mean for your trust in Allah if you ignored such rational signs?
?
This argument uses clear reason to prove an unseen reality. When was the last time you actively used your intellect to strengthen your faith rather than just following tradition? What is stopping you from doing that today?
?
Al-Razi says the mind can conceive a body touching no surfaces. Have you ever considered that your own existence depends on realities your senses cannot directly perceive? How should this affect your daily awareness of Allah's existence and attributes?

Fifth Argument: The Totality of Finite Bodies Proves Space is Not a Surface
Al-Razi presents a syllogism, an objection, and a rebuttal to prove that locus/direction is not a surface.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Razi's fifth argument that the totality of finite bodies must have a locus and direction, and why this cannot be a containing surface, as well as the refutation of the objection based on estimation and imagination.

Core Idea
The universe as a whole must occupy a real space, not just a relative position.

Al-Razi presents a concise yet powerful argument that builds on the finitude of bodies. He argues that if every individual body is finite, then the entire collection must have a locus and direction, but no surface can contain the whole. The objection that the totality has no actual locus is dismissed as a product of imagination, not reason.

Guided Path
1
Read and follow the syllogism
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Identify the main premise: bodies are finite, so the totality must have a locus and direction. Notice how Al-Razi concludes that the locus cannot be a containing surface.
The argument is structured as a syllogism: Premise (bodies finite) → Conclusion (totality has locus/direction, not a surface).
2
Analyze the objection and response
The objector claims the totality has no locus or direction, only relative position. Al-Razi responds that this view comes from estimation and imagination (wahm and khayal), not from reason or innate disposition (fitra). Understand why this distinction matters.
Al-Razi asserts that reason and innate disposition affirm that every body must be in a locus and direction.
3
Reflect on the role of wahm and fitra
Consider why Al-Razi dismisses the judgment of wahm and khayal as false. He appeals to 'clear reason' and 'innate disposition' as reliable sources. This highlights a key epistemological principle in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الخامسة: إن الأجسام متناهية. فمجموع الأجسام يجب أن يكون حيز وجهة، ومجموع الأجسام يمتنع أن يحيط به سطح آخر. ينتج: أن الحيز والجهة أمر مغاير للسطح الحاوي. لا يقال: مجموع الأجسام ليس لها حيز ولا جهة البتة، وإنما حصل له وضع بالقياس إلى الأجسام الموجودة في داخله. نعم لا شك أنه يتعذر علينا إثبات جسم لا يكون له حيز ولا جهة. إلا أن الحاكم بهذا الحكم هو الوهم والخيال، لا العقل. وقد ثبت أن حكم الوهم والخيال باطل كاذب. لانا نقول: إنا نجد صريح العقل والفطرة شاهدان بأن كل جسم فإنه لا بد وأن يكون حاصلاً في حيز وفي جهة. والذي يؤكد هذا وجوه:
The fifth proof: Bodies are finite. So the totality of bodies must have a locus (ḥayyiz) and direction (jiha), and it is impossible for the totality of bodies to be surrounded by another surface. The conclusion is that the locus and direction are different from the containing surface. It cannot be said: the totality of bodies has no locus or direction at all; rather it has a position relative to the bodies existing inside it. Yes, there is no doubt that it is impossible for us to prove a body that has no locus and no direction. But the one judging this judgment is estimation (al-wahm) and imagination (al-khayāl), not reason (al-ʿaql). And it has been established that the judgment of estimation and imagination is false and deceptive. Because we say: we find that clear reason and innate disposition (al-fiṭra) testify that every body must be present in a locus and direction. And what confirms this are ways:
Source pages p. 153

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth argument (al-ḥujja al-khāmisa). The author starts with a syllogism: bodies are finite (mutanāhiya), so the entire collection of bodies (majmūʿ al-ajsām) must have a locus (ḥayyiz) and a direction (jiha). But this totality cannot be surrounded by another surface (since it is all bodies). Therefore, the locus/direction is not the containing surface. Then an objection is raised: one might say that the totality of bodies has no locus or direction at all; it only has a relative position with respect to bodies inside it. The author responds that this objection comes from estimation (wahm) and imagination (khayāl), not from reason. Reason and innate disposition (fiṭra) clearly affirm that every body must be in a locus and direction. Thus the argument stands. The passage ends with a lead-in to further confirmations that are on subsequent pages.

Key Terms
جسم (jism)
حيز (hayyiz)
جهة (jiha)
وهم (wahm)
خيال (khayal)
فطرة (fitra)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The totality of bodies has no locus or direction; it only has a relative position.
Correction: Al-Razi argues that reason and innate disposition (fitra) testify that every body must have a locus and direction, so the totality must too. The contrary view arises from estimation (wahm) and imagination, which are unreliable.

Checkpoint

B) Estimation and imagination (al-wahm wa al-khayāl)

D) Sense perception

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi's fifth argument uses the finitude of bodies to show that space (locus/direction) is not a containing surface, and dismisses the objection as relying on unreliable estimation and imagination.

What will you do today to train your 'aql to dominate your wahm?

The Argument from Motion and Rest for Distinction between Space and Surface
Proof that space is distinct from surface via motion and rest.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why space (ḥayyiz) is a distinct reality from the encompassing surface (saṭḥ) of a body, based on the rational analysis of motion and rest.

Core Idea
Motion and rest are intelligible without surface contact, proving space is not just the inner boundary.

This lesson examines a key argument from classical Kalām that distinguishes between the space a body occupies and the surface that bounds it. The author invites us to use our pure intellect to see that motion and rest can be conceived independently of any contact with surfaces, thus revealing that space is a distinct concept. By following the logical steps, we will see how this argument strengthens the understanding of bodies and their locations.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then read the full English translation. Let the author's reasoning flow naturally before analyzing.
2
Identify the initial claim
The passage begins by asserting that there is an intelligible concept other than the surface. Notice how it establishes that space is other than surface.
Look for the phrase 'مغاير للمعقول'.
3
Follow the confirmation argument
The author uses the intellect's judgments about motion and rest. Contrast the two judgments: one about motion/rest is necessary, the other about surface contact is not.
Understand why motion/rest do not require surface contact.
4
Understand the definitions of motion and rest
Motion is transfer from space to space, rest is stability in one space. These definitions directly involve space, not surfaces.
This connects the argument to the conclusion.
5
Reach the conclusion
Since motion and rest are defined via space, and they are distinct from surface contact, space must be distinct from surface. Confirm this final step.
The conclusion is 'وذلك هو المطلوب'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هناك : مغاير للمعقول من كونه بحيث يحيط به غيره. فيثبت : أن الحيز الذي حكم العقل بكون الجسم حاصلاً فيه : مغاير للسطح الحاوي [ والله أعلم (١) ] . ومما يؤكد ذلك : أن صريح العقل يحكم بأن الجسم حال وجوده لا بد وأن يكون إما متحركاً وإما ساكناً . وصريح العقل لا يحكم بأنه يجب أن يكون [ إما () ] مماساً لسطح واحد ، أو منتقلاً من مماسة سطح آخر . لأن العقل يمكنه تصور الجسم بحيث لا يماسه شيء من السطوح أصلاً . فيثبت : أن المعقول من كونه متحركاً وساكناً ، مغاير للمعقول من كونه مماساً لسطح واحد ، أو منتقلاً من مماسة سطح إلى مماسة سطح آخر . وإذا ثبت هذا فنقول : المعقول من الحركة هو الانتقال من [ الحيز إلى الحيز الذي يليه (۳) ] والمعقول من السكون هو الاستقرار في الحيز الواحد ، وهذا يقتضي أن سكون هذا الحيز الذي يستقر الجسم فيه تارة ، وينتقل منه إلى غيره أخرى : مغايراً للسطح الحاوي . وذلك هو المطلوب .
There is something other than the intelligible (al-maʿqūl) in that it is such that something else encompasses it. So it is established that the space (al-ḥayyiz) which the intellect judges the body to be in is other than the encompassing surface (al-saṭḥ al-ḥāwī). And what confirms this is that the plain intellect judges that a body, at the moment of its existence, must necessarily be either moving or at rest. But the plain intellect does not judge that it must be either touching a single surface or moving from contact with another surface, because the intellect can conceive of the body without any surfaces touching it at all. So it is established that the intelligible of its being moving and at rest is other than the intelligible of its being touching a single surface or moving from contact with a surface to contact with another surface. And when this is established, we say: the intelligible of motion is the transfer from one space to the next, and the intelligible of rest is stability in one space. And this entails that the space in which the body sometimes settles and from which it moves to another is other than the encompassing surface. And that is what was sought.
Source pages p. 153

Commentary
This passage is a proof establishing that space (ḥayyiz) is a distinct reality from the encompassing surface (saṭḥ) of a body. The author begins by stating that there is an intelligible concept other than the surface—namely, that the body is in a place such that something else (the body itself or another body) can encompass it. The intellect judges that a body, when it exists, must be either moving or at rest. However, the intellect does not judge that the body must be touching a surface or moving from one surface contact to another; one can conceive a body floating in a vacuum with no surface contact. Therefore, the concepts of motion and rest are different from the concepts of touching surfaces. Since motion is defined as transfer from one space to another, and rest as stability in one space, it follows that the space in which the body settles or moves is not the same as the surface that bounds it. This proves that space (ḥayyiz) is other than the surface (saṭḥ).

Key Terms
حيز (ḥayyiz)
سطح (saṭḥ)
حركة (ḥaraka)
سكون (sukūn)
معقول (maʿqūl)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some people think that the space a body occupies is just the inner boundary of its container (i.e., the surface).
Correction: The argument shows that space is a distinct reality because motion and rest can be conceived without any surface contact, and the definitions of motion and rest refer to space, not surfaces.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does the intellect not judge that a body must be touching a surface?
A) Because surfaces are not real.
B) Because the intellect can conceive of a body without any surface contact.
C) Because motion and rest require surfaces.
D) Because the body is always in a vacuum.

Key Takeaway
Space (ḥayyiz) is a distinct reality from the encompassing surface (saṭḥ) because the concepts of motion and rest, which are necessary for a body, do not require surface contact and are defined in terms of space itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the intellect can conceive of a body without any surface touching it, what does that imply about how much you rely on physical sensations to affirm your existence? Are you willing to accept a reality that the intellect affirms even when your senses cannot confirm it? Name one such reality you struggle to accept.
?
The argument distinguishes between the surface (external appearance) and the space (the inner reality where motion and rest happen). In your spiritual life, do you sometimes focus on external shows of religiosity while ignoring the inner movement of your heart? Commit to one change that redirects your attention from surface to substance.
?

The Fifth Proof: Space and Direction Are Not Surfaces
Presents the proof and objection, with a brief response.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fifth proof that space and direction are distinct from surfaces, and be able to answer the objection that space and direction are mere imaginary constructs.

Core Idea
Space and direction are real, not imaginary—proven by reason, not illusion.

This lesson presents the core of the fifth proof: because the totality of finite bodies must occupy a space and direction, and cannot be encompassed by another surface, space and direction are something other than a surface. We then address a powerful objection that appeals to illusion and imagination, and respond by affirming the authority of intellect and natural disposition.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof statement
Read the first few lines carefully: bodies are finite, so their totality must be a space and direction, and cannot be enclosed by another surface. This yields the conclusion that space and direction are not surfaces.
Notice the logical structure: premise → conclusion.
2
Examine the objection
The objection claims that the totality of bodies has no space or direction at all, only relative position. It argues that our inability to conceive a body without space is due to imagination, not reason.
The objector concedes we cannot prove a body without space, but questions the basis.
3
Understand the response
The author responds by citing the plain intellect and natural disposition (fiṭra) as witnesses that every body must be in a space and direction. The judgment of illusion is declared false.
Fiṭra here refers to an innate, untaught human cognition.
4
Reflect on the key terms
Note the terms: ḥayyiz (space), jiha (direction), saṭḥ (surface), wahm (illusion), khayāl (imagination), fiṭra (natural disposition). These are central to the argument.
Wahm and khayāl are lower cognitive faculties that can err.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الخامسة : إن الأجسام متناهية . فمجموع الأجسام يجب أن يكون حيز وجهة ، ومجموع الأجسام يمتنع أن يحيط به سطح آخر . ينتج : أن الحيز والجهة أمر مغاير للسطح الحاوي . لا يقال : مجموع الأجسام ليس لها حيز ولا جهة البنة ، وإنما حصل له وضع بالقياس إلى الأجسام الموجودة في داخله . نعم لا شك أنه يتعذر علينا إثبات جسم لا يكون له حيز ولا جهة . إلا أن الحاكم بهذا الحكم هو الوهم والخيال، لا العقل. وقد ثبت أن حكم الوهم والخيال باطل كاذب . لانا نقول : إنا نجد صريح العقل والفطرة شاهدان بأن كل جسم فإنه لا بد وأن يكون حاصلاً في حيز وفي جهة . والذي يؤكد هذا وجوه : الأول : إنا ببداهة عقولنا نعلم أن الخط الذي هو محور العالم ، لو خرج من جانبيه إلى ما لا نهاية له ، فإن أحد جانبي ذلك الخط ، مغاير للجانب الآخر منه . ومن أنكر هذا التغاير ، فقد نازع في أقوى البديهيات الثاني : إنا لو فرضنا إنساناً واقفاً على طرف القطب الشمالي ، فإنه لا بـد وان يميز قدامه عن خلفه . والعلم بهذا الامتياز ضروري . الثالث : إن النقطة المفروضة في السطح الأعلى من الفلك ، لا شك أنها تكون متحركة [ حال استدارة (۱) ] الفلك ، ولا معنى لتلك الحركة ، إلا أن تلك النقطة انتقلت من حيز إلى حيز [ آخر (٢) ] وذلك هو المطلوب
The fifth proof: Bodies are finite. So the totality of bodies must be a space (ḥayyiz) and a direction (jiha), and it is impossible for the totality of bodies to be encompassed by another surface. This yields that space and direction are something other than the encompassing surface. It cannot be said: The totality of bodies has no space nor direction at all, but rather it has a position relative to the bodies inside it. Yes, there is no doubt that it is impossible for us to prove a body that has no space and no direction. But the judge of this judgment is illusion (al-wahm) and imagination (al-khayāl), not the intellect. And it has been established that the judgment of illusion and imagination is false and lying. For we say: We find the plain intellect and natural disposition (al-fiṭra) witnessing that every body must necessarily be located in a space and in a direction. And what confirms this are aspects: First: By the self-evidence of our intellects, we know that the line which is the axis of the world, if it extended from its two sides to infinity, then one side of that line is other than the other side. And whoever denies this difference has contested the strongest self-evident truths. Second: If we suppose a man standing at the North Pole, he must necessarily distinguish his front from his back. And knowledge of this distinction is necessary. Third: The point assumed in the upper surface of the sphere, there is no doubt that it moves with the rotation of the sphere, and there is no meaning to that motion except that that point transferred from one space to another space. And that is what was sought.
Source pages p. 153

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth proof that space (ḥayyiz) and direction (jiha) are not identical to the encompassing surface (saṭḥ). The proof begins by noting that bodies are finite (mutanāhiya). Therefore, the aggregate of all bodies must occupy a space and have a direction, and it is impossible for that aggregate to be encircled by another surface (since it is the totality). Thus, space and direction must be something other than a surface. An objection is then raised: one might claim that the totality of bodies has no space or direction at all, but only a relative position with respect to the bodies inside it. The objector concedes that we cannot prove a body without space or direction, but argues that such judgments come from illusion (wahm) and imagination (khayāl), not reason, and are therefore false. The author answers by appealing to the plain intellect and natural disposition (fiṭra), which testify that every body must be in a space and direction. Three supporting arguments (wujūh) are given: (1) The axis of the world, if extended infinitely, has two distinct sides—this is self-evident. (2) A person at the North Pole can distinguish front from back, a necessary judgment. (3) A point on the celestial sphere moves with the sphere's rotation, and motion is nothing but transfer from one space to another. These confirm that space and direction are real and distinct from surfaces.

Key Terms
حيز (ḥayyiz)
جهة (jiha)
سطح (saṭḥ)
وهم (wahm)
خيال (khayāl)
فطرة (fiṭra)
بديهة (badīha)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Space and direction are mere imaginary constructs with no real existence.
Correction: The proof shows they are real, as the totality of bodies cannot be conceived otherwise, and the objection based on illusion is false.

Checkpoint
According to the fifth proof, why must the totality of bodies have a space and direction?
A) Because each body has a surface that defines its space.
B) Because bodies are finite, so their totality cannot be encompassed by another surface.
C) Because illusion proves it.
D) Because the North Pole example shows it.

Key Takeaway
Space and direction are real entities distinct from surfaces, proven by the finitude of bodies and the impossibility of an encompassing surface.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about the space you occupy and the direction you face, do you consider them as real or just mental constructs? How does your answer affect your understanding of the universe being finite or infinite?
?
The author says that our deep-seated belief that everything must have a space and direction is not an illusion but a rational truth. Have you ever denied a self-evident truth because you were overly impressed by a philosophical doubt? How can you guard against that?
?
If space and direction are real and not just relative, then the totality of bodies points to something beyond itself. What does that imply about the Creator's relationship to space? Are you comfortable with that implication, or does it challenge your assumptions?

Three Supporting Arguments for the Reality of Space and Direction
Presents the response and three self-evident supporting arguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three self-evident arguments that confirm space and direction are real and not mere illusions.

Core Idea
Intellect and natural instinct testify that every body occupies a real space and direction.

After stating the objection, the author provides a robust response: the plain intellect and natural disposition (fiṭra) witness that every body must be in a space and direction. To reinforce this, he offers three self-evident arguments—the axis line, the North Pole example, and the rotating point. These are designed to show that the denial of real space and direction contradicts the strongest self-evident truths.

Guided Path
1
Read the response statement
Start with 'lā naqūlu' and understand the appeal to the plain intellect and fiṭra. The author asserts that these faculties testify directly that every body has a space and direction.
Fiṭra here means an innate, untaught human disposition that recognizes truth.
2
Analyze the first argument
The first argument uses the axis of the world: if extended infinitely, its two sides are distinct. This distinction is self-evident. Denying it is to reject the strongest self-evident truths.
The axis line represents a direction; its sides imply real spatial differentiation.
3
Analyze the second argument
A man at the North Pole must distinguish front from back. This distinction is necessary (ḍarūrī), proving direction is real.
Even if there is no external reference, the person still has a built-in sense of direction.
4
Analyze the third argument
A point on the celestial sphere moves with the sphere's rotation. Motion is defined as transfer from one space to another, so space must be real.
The point's movement presupposes changing locations in real space.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الخامسة : إن الأجسام متناهية . فمجموع الأجسام يجب أن يكون حيز وجهة ، ومجموع الأجسام يمتنع أن يحيط به سطح آخر . ينتج : أن الحيز والجهة أمر مغاير للسطح الحاوي . لا يقال : مجموع الأجسام ليس لها حيز ولا جهة البنة ، وإنما حصل له وضع بالقياس إلى الأجسام الموجودة في داخله . نعم لا شك أنه يتعذر علينا إثبات جسم لا يكون له حيز ولا جهة . إلا أن الحاكم بهذا الحكم هو الوهم والخيال، لا العقل. وقد ثبت أن حكم الوهم والخيال باطل كاذب . لانا نقول : إنا نجد صريح العقل والفطرة شاهدان بأن كل جسم فإنه لا بد وأن يكون حاصلاً في حيز وفي جهة . والذي يؤكد هذا وجوه : الأول : إنا ببداهة عقولنا نعلم أن الخط الذي هو محور العالم ، لو خرج من جانبيه إلى ما لا نهاية له ، فإن أحد جانبي ذلك الخط ، مغاير للجانب الآخر منه . ومن أنكر هذا التغاير ، فقد نازع في أقوى البديهيات الثاني : إنا لو فرضنا إنساناً واقفاً على طرف القطب الشمالي ، فإنه لا بـد وان يميز قدامه عن خلفه . والعلم بهذا الامتياز ضروري . الثالث : إن النقطة المفروضة في السطح الأعلى من الفلك ، لا شك أنها تكون متحركة [ حال استدارة (۱) ] الفلك ، ولا معنى لتلك الحركة ، إلا أن تلك النقطة انتقلت من حيز إلى حيز [ آخر (٢) ] وذلك هو المطلوب
The fifth proof: Bodies are finite. So the totality of bodies must be a space (ḥayyiz) and a direction (jiha), and it is impossible for the totality of bodies to be encompassed by another surface. This yields that space and direction are something other than the encompassing surface. It cannot be said: The totality of bodies has no space nor direction at all, but rather it has a position relative to the bodies inside it. Yes, there is no doubt that it is impossible for us to prove a body that has no space and no direction. But the judge of this judgment is illusion (al-wahm) and imagination (al-khayāl), not the intellect. And it has been established that the judgment of illusion and imagination is false and lying. For we say: We find the plain intellect and natural disposition (al-fiṭra) witnessing that every body must necessarily be located in a space and in a direction. And what confirms this are aspects: First: By the self-evidence of our intellects, we know that the line which is the axis of the world, if it extended from its two sides to infinity, then one side of that line is other than the other side. And whoever denies this difference has contested the strongest self-evident truths. Second: If we suppose a man standing at the North Pole, he must necessarily distinguish his front from his back. And knowledge of this distinction is necessary. Third: The point assumed in the upper surface of the sphere, there is no doubt that it moves with the rotation of the sphere, and there is no meaning to that motion except that that point transferred from one space to another space. And that is what was sought.
Source pages p. 153

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth proof that space (ḥayyiz) and direction (jiha) are not identical to the encompassing surface (saṭḥ). The proof begins by noting that bodies are finite (mutanāhiya). Therefore, the aggregate of all bodies must occupy a space and have a direction, and it is impossible for that aggregate to be encircled by another surface (since it is the totality). Thus, space and direction must be something other than a surface. An objection is then raised: one might claim that the totality of bodies has no space or direction at all, but only a relative position with respect to the bodies inside it. The objector concedes that we cannot prove a body without space or direction, but argues that such judgments come from illusion (wahm) and imagination (khayāl), not reason, and are therefore false. The author answers by appealing to the plain intellect and natural disposition (fiṭra), which testify that every body must be in a space and direction. Three supporting arguments (wujūh) are given: (1) The axis of the world, if extended infinitely, has two distinct sides—this is self-evident. (2) A person at the North Pole can distinguish front from back, a necessary judgment. (3) A point on the celestial sphere moves with the sphere's rotation, and motion is nothing but transfer from one space to another. These confirm that space and direction are real and distinct from surfaces.

جهة (jiha)
فطرة (fiṭra)
بديهة (badīha)
وهم (wahm)
خيال (khayāl)

Key Takeaway
Three self-evident arguments—the axis line, the North Pole, and the moving point—confirm that space and direction are real, not illusions.

Why Space Judgments Cannot Be Mere Illusion
Refutes the claim that space judgments are from illusion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the claim that space judgments are merely products of illusion leads to circular reasoning and is arbitrary.

Core Idea
If self-evident truths can be false, all knowledge collapses into circularity.

This passage tackles a fundamental objection: that our judgments about space and direction are products of illusion, not reason. The author refutes this by two arguments: first, showing the disastrous consequence of doubting such a strong self-evident proposition; second, pointing out the arbitrariness of distinguishing intellect from illusion without a criterion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the English translation. Notice the confident tone and logical structure.
The passage is part of a larger work; focus on the two arguments presented.
2
Analyze the first argument (circularity)
The author argues that if a self-evident proposition as strong as this could be false, then no self-evident truth is secure, leading to circularity in proof. Understand why this is devastating for the opponent.
The key term is 'dawr' (circularity).
3
Analyze the second argument (arbitrary distinction)
The author points out that the opponent's partitioning of judgments into intellect (true) and illusion (false) is arbitrary without a clear criterion. Identify why this undermines their claim.
The passage ends mid-sentence, but the argument is clear: the opponent must first establish a distinction.
4
Reflect on the overall impact
Consider why this refutation is important for the broader theological discussion about knowledge of space and direction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما قوله : ( إن هذا الحكم عمل الوهم والخيال . وذلك كاذب ) . فنقول : هذا الكلام باطل من وجوه : الأول : وهو أنا نجد الجزم بصحة هذه القضية جزماً قاطعاً غير قابل للشك والشبهة ، ولا نجد في القلب تفاوتاً بين قوة هذه القضية وبين سائر البديهيات . وإذا كان كذلك فنقول : لو جوزنا أن تكون القضية مع هذا القدر من القوة كاذبة فحينئذ يرتفع الأمان عن البديهيات ، ويحتاج في تصحيح ما يصح منها إلى النظر والاستدلال ، لكن الاستدلال موقوف على صحة البديهيات ، فيلزم الدور . وهو فاسد . والثاني : وهو إنا نجد في فطرة النفس السليمة عن الآفات ، وغريزة القلب المبرأة عن المشوشات أحكاماً يقينية قاطعة، ثم أنتم تقولون : الحاكم ببعضها هو العقل ، [ وهو صادق (۳) ] والحاكم [بالبعض الآخر (1) ] هو الوهم وهو كاذب . فعلى هذا التقرير ما لم يُعرف الفرق بين العقل وبين الوهم ، وما لم يُعرف ثانياً أن هذا الحكم هو حكم العقل لا حكم الوهم ، وما لم يُعرف ثالثاً
And as for his statement: 'This judgment is the work of illusion and imagination, and that is false.' We say: This statement is invalid from several aspects: The first: We find certainty in the truth of this proposition with a decisive certainty not open to doubt or suspicion, and we find no difference in the heart between the strength of this proposition and other self-evident truths. And if it is so, we say: If we allowed that the proposition, with this degree of strength, could be false, then the security of self-evident truths would be lifted, and one would need, in order to verify what is sound among them, to employ examination and inference (al-naẓar wa-l-istidlāl). But inference depends on the truth of self-evident truths, so circularity (al-dawr) would follow, and it is invalid. The second: We find in the sound nature (fiṭra) of the soul free from defects, and the innate disposition (gharīza) of the heart purified from distractions, decisive certain judgments. Then you say: The judge of some of them is the intellect, and it is truthful, and the judge of others is illusion, and it is false. Thus, on this account, as long as the distinction between the intellect and illusion is not known, and secondly that this judgment is the judgment of the intellect not of illusion is not known, and thirdly ...
Source pages p. 154

Commentary
This passage refutes the objection that judgments about space and direction are merely products of illusion (wahm) and imagination (khayāl) and therefore false. The author gives two arguments (wujūh). The first argument: We find ourselves certain of the truth of such propositions (e.g., every body must be in a space) with a decisive certainty no different from that of other self-evident truths (badīhiyyāt). If we allowed that such a strong proposition could be false, then no self-evident truth would be trustworthy. Any verification of self-evident truths would require inference (naẓar), but inference itself depends on self-evident truths, leading to circularity (dawr)—which is invalid. Therefore, these propositions must be true. The second argument: We observe that in a sound soul and pure heart there are many decisive certain judgments. The opponent arbitrarily claims that some are from intellect (true) and others from illusion (false). But without a criterion to distinguish intellect from illusion, and without knowing that a particular judgment is from intellect, their claim is baseless. The passage ends mid-sentence, but the point is clear: the opponent's division is arbitrary and unsupported.

خيال (khayāl)
بديهية (badīhiyya)
دور (dawr)
فطرة (fiṭra)
غريزة (gharīza)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Rejecting a single self-evident truth does not affect the reliability of other self-evident truths.
Correction: The author shows that if one equally certain self-evident proposition can be false, then confidence in all self-evident truths is undermined, leading to circularity in proof.

Checkpoint

A) All self-evident truths remain secure.
B) Inference becomes unnecessary.
C) The security of all self-evident truths is removed, leading to circularity.
D) Illusion becomes the sole source of knowledge.

Key Takeaway
To reject a self-evident judgment of equal certainty to basic axioms would destroy the foundation of all knowledge.

The opponent's claim shows how easily we can doubt what is obvious to suit our arguments. Where in your life are you currently doubting something undeniably true because it is inconvenient?
?
If you were to act as if no self-evident truth is secure, how would your daily decision-making change? What foundation would you rely on?

Place as a Self-Subsistent Dimension: The Epistemological Proof
Epistemological proof that place is a self-subsistent dimension.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Kalam argument uses the concepts of self-evident and inferential knowledge to prove that place is a self-subsistent dimension, not a containing surface.

Core Idea
Some truths are undeniable; place is one such self-evident dimension.

The passage presents a foundational epistemological argument in Kalam: the intellect is truthful, and certain propositions are self-evident (like the distinctness of the North and South Poles). By analyzing what happens if knowledge were only inferential—leading to circularity and sophistry—the argument establishes that place (space) must be an independent, self-subsistent dimension. Let's examine this logical chain.

Guided Path
1
Understand the two options for knowledge
The author divides knowledge into two categories: self-evident (بديهي) and inferential (استدلالي). Self-evident knowledge cannot be doubted, while inferential depends on proofs.
Notice that self-evident truths are the foundation of all reasoning.
2
Analyze the consequence of inferential knowledge
If self-evident truths were inferential, they would depend on proofs, which themselves depend on self-evident truths—causing circularity (دور). This would invalidate all sciences and lead to sophistry (سفسطة).
This is a reductio ad absurdum: assuming the opposite leads to absurdity.
3
Apply the conclusion to the nature of place
Having established that some truths are self-evident, the author argues that the distinctness of places (like North vs South Pole) is self-evident. Therefore, place cannot be a mere surface but must be a self-subsistent dimension.
The example bridges epistemology to physics.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أن العقل صادق ، وأن الوهم كاذب . فإنه لا يمكننا أن نحكم بصحة تلك القضية التي جزمنا بصحتها .
ونقول : العلم بهذه الأمور الثلاثة إن كان بديهياً ، امتنع حصول الجزم في القضايا الكاذبة ، مع حصول العلم البديهي بكونه كاذباً . وإن كان استدلالياً لزم توقف البديهيات على الدلائل لكن الدلائل موقوفة على البديهيات . فيلزم الدور وتبطل كل العلوم وتلزم السفسطة
الثالث : وهو أنه لا معنى للعلوم البديهية الأولية إلا القضايا التي أراد العاقل تشكيل نفسه فيها لما قدر عليه لكن العلم بأنه لا بد وأن يمتاز جانب القطب الشمالي عن جانب القطب الجنوبي، علم لو أراد العاقل تشكيك نفسه فيه لما قدر عليه فوجب أن يكون ذلك من العلوم البديهية .
فهذا مجموع الدلائل الدالة على أن المكان عبارة عن البعد القائم بذاته ،
لا عن السطح الحاوي . والله أعلم.
That the intellect is truthful, and that the imagination is false. For we cannot judge the correctness of that proposition which we have affirmed as correct.
We say: knowledge of these three matters – if it is self-evident, then the obtaining of certainty in false propositions is impossible, while self-evident knowledge of its falsehood exists. And if it is inferential, then self-evident truths would depend on proofs, but proofs depend on self-evident truths, leading to circularity and the invalidation of all sciences, and sophistry results.
Third: That there is no meaning to primary self-evident sciences except the propositions which the rational person cannot doubt when he reflects. [Example]: knowledge that the North Pole side must be distinct from the South Pole side – knowledge which, if the rational person tried to doubt, he would not be able to. So that must be among self-evident sciences.
Thus this is the totality of proofs indicating that place is a self-subsistent dimension, not a containing surface. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 155

Commentary
This passage presents an epistemological proof that place is a self-subsistent dimension. It begins by asserting the reliability of reason (العقل) and the falsity of imagination (الوهم). The argument then examines knowledge of three matters: if such knowledge is self-evident (بديهي), one cannot simultaneously hold certainty in a false proposition while having self-evident knowledge of its falsehood; if it is inferential (استدلالي), self-evident truths would depend on proofs, but proofs themselves depend on self-evident truths, creating a circularity (دور) that invalidates all knowledge and leads to sophism (سفسطة). A concrete example of a self-evident truth—the distinctness of the North and South Poles—is given to illustrate a proposition that cannot be doubted. The conclusion synthesizes these proofs to affirm that place (المكان) is a self-subsistent dimension (البعد القائم بذاته), not a containing surface (السطح الحاوي). The passage ends with a pious phrase.

البديهي
الاستدلالي
الدور
السفسطة
المكان
البعد القائم بذاته
السطح الحاوي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Self-evident truths are not really certain because they depend on proofs.
Correction: The argument shows that if self-evident truths were inferential, circularity would result; thus they must be certain and not dependent on proofs.

Checkpoint
According to the argument, what would happen if self-evident truths were inferential?
A) They would become even more certain.
B) They would depend on proofs, which depend on self-evident truths, causing circularity.
C) They would be impossible to know.
D) The intellect could doubt them.

Key Takeaway
The argument demonstrates that some truths are self-evident and undeniable, and this fact is used to prove that place is a self-subsistent dimension, not a containing surface.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
