Second Premise: Parts of the Void May Differ in Essence
The author presents the Second Premise: critiquing necessary similarity of void parts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that sharing properties like dimensionality does not guarantee a common essence, so the world's location in one part of the void is not necessitated by similarity.

Core Idea
Shared properties don't imply shared essence.

The Second Premise challenges the assumption that all parts of the void are identical in essence. The author argues that even if parts share the capacity for dimensions, they may differ in their true nature. This undermines any claim that the world's placement in a particular part of the void is necessary due to uniformity.

Guided Path
1
Read the opponent's claim
The premise begins by stating the opponent's view: that parts of the void are alike. Then it questions this assumption.
Notice the rhetorical question format.
2
Identify the rebuttal
The author proposes that some parts may differ in essence, making the world's occurrence in one part more appropriate. This is the core objection.
3
Understand the reasoning
The explanation (taqrir) clarifies that sharing the capacity for dimensions does not entail sharing in complete essence, using the principle that shared properties do not prove shared essences.
Think about examples: two things can both be extended but still be different in kind.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقدمة الثانية : إن الأجزاء المفترضة في هذا الخلاء : متشابهة . فتقول : ولم قلتم : إن الأمر كذلك ؟ ولم لا يجوز أن يقال : بعض أجزاء هذا الخلاء مخالف بالماهية للبعض فلا جرم كان حصول هذا العالم في بعض تلك الأجزاء ، أولى من حصوله في الباقي ؟ وتقريره : أن الخلاء موجود يمكن فرض الأبعاد الثلاثة فيه ، ولا يلزم من اشراك أجزاء الخلاء في قابلية الأبعاد ، اشتراكها في تمام الماهية. لما ثبت أن الاشتراك في الأحكام واللوازم لا يدل على الاشتراك في ماهيات الملزومات

المقدمة الثالثة : هب أن أجزاء الخلاء متشابهة في تمام الماهية. لكن لم لا يجوز أن يقال : الفاعل المختار خصص العالم ببعض أجزاء الخلاء دون البعض ، بمجرد الإرادة ؟ وحينئذ يرجع هذا الكلام إلى البحث عن المقدمة المشهورة المذكورة في مسألة الفرق بين القادر وبين الموجب. وقد تقدم ذلك بالاستقصاء .

المقدمة الرابعة : هب أن القول بالفاعل المختار باطل عندكم إلا أنا نشاهد أن كل واحد من الأجسام الحيوانية والنباتية والمعدنية مختص بصورة معينة وحيز معين ومقدار معين مع أنه لا يمتنع في العقل حصول خلاف ما وقع. فاختصاص كل واحد من هذه الأجسام بصفاته المعينة إما أن يكون لا لمرجح أو المرجح هو الفاعل المختار . أو إن كان ذلك المرجح موجباً بالذات إلا أن كل حادث ، فإنه مسبوق بحادث آخر ، وكان الحادث المتقدم ، أعد المادة لقبول الحادث المتأخر. وعلى هذا الترتيب يكون قبل كل حادث ، حادث آخر لا إلى أول . وبالجملة فإذا عقلنا هذه الاختصاصات في هذه الأجسام ، لأحد هذه الأقسام ، فلم لا يجوز مثله في اختصاص كل العالم الجسماني لموضع معين من الخلاء، الذي لا نهاية له ؟ وذلك أن يقال : إنه حصل في ذلك الحيز المعين، لا لمرجح أو المرجح هو الفاعل المختار أو لأجل أن هذا العالم كان قبل أن حصل في هذا الحيز ، حاصلا في حيز آخر من الخلاء ، وكان حصوله في ذلك الحيز من الخلاء ، أعد له لأن يتنقل منه إلى هذا الحيز الثاني من الخلاء وهكذا لا إلى أول : أن كل ما يقولونه في الأعراض المعينة ، الحاصلة في الأجسام السفلية ، فنحن نقول مثله في اختصاص كلية العالم بالجزء المعين من الخلاء .
The Second Premise: The assumed parts of this void are similar. So we say: Why do you claim that this is the case? And why may it not be said that some parts of this void differ in essence from others, so that necessarily this world's occurrence in some of those parts would be more appropriate than its occurrence in the rest? And its explanation: The void is existent, in which the three dimensions can be supposed, and the sharing of the parts of the void in the capacity for dimensions does not entail their sharing in complete essence. Because it has been established that sharing in properties and concomitants does not indicate sharing in the essences of what bears them.

The Third Premise: Suppose the parts of the void are similar in complete essence. But why may it not be said that the agent by choice (al-fa'il al-mukhtar) particularized the world to some parts of the void rather than others, simply by will (al-irada)? In that case, this discussion reverts to the investigation of the well-known premise mentioned in the issue of the difference between the capable (al-qadir) and the necessitating cause (al-mujib). And that has already been discussed thoroughly.

The Fourth Premise: Suppose the statement of the agent by choice is false according to you, except that we observe that every one of animal, plant, and mineral bodies is particularized with a specific form, a specific location, and a specific quantity, while it is not intellectually impossible for the opposite of what occurred to occur. So the particularization of each of these bodies with its specific attributes is either without a determining factor, or the determining factor is the agent by choice. Or if that determining factor is a necessitating cause by essence, except that every event is preceded by another event, and the preceding event prepared the matter to receive the subsequent event. And according to this arrangement, before every event there is another event without a first. In summary, if we understand these particularizations in these bodies as one of these divisions, then why is the like not permissible in the particularization of the entire corporeal world to a specific place in the infinite void? That is, it is said: It occurred in that specific location either without a determining factor, or the determining factor is the agent by choice, or because this world, before it occurred in this location, was occurring in another location of the void, and its occurrence in that location of the void prepared it to move from it to this second location of the void, and so on without a first: that everything they say about specific accidents occurring in sublunary bodies, we say the same regarding the particularization of the entire world to the specific part of the void.
Source pages p. 173

Commentary
This passage presents three sequential premises (muqaddimat) that critique the claim that the world's location in the void is necessary. The Second Premise argues against the assumption that all parts of the void are similar in essence. It notes that even if parts share the capacity for dimensions, they might differ in essence, so the world's placement in one part rather than another could be due to essential variance. Thus, necessity is not proven. The Third Premise grants similarity in essence but raises the possibility of a free agent (al-fa'il al-mukhtar) choosing a location by sheer will. This turns the question into the broader debate between a capable agent and a necessitating cause, which has been addressed earlier. The Fourth Premise grants that the idea of a free agent is rejected by opponents, but then points to observable particularization in natural bodies (animals, plants, minerals) that have specific forms, places, and sizes. Since these particularizations are not logically necessary, they must be explained either by no determining factor, or by a free agent, or by an infinite regress of events where each prepares the matter for the next. The same three options are then applied to the entire corporeal world's location in the void. Thus, the premises collectively undermine any necessitarian explanation of the world's spatial location.

Key Terms
خلاء
أجزاء
ماهية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Shared properties (like dimensionality) guarantee a common essence.
Correction: Sharing properties does not prove sharing in complete essence; essence is deeper than attributes.

Checkpoint
What is the main argument of the Second Premise?
A) All parts of the void are exactly the same in every way.
B) Sharing the capacity for dimensions does not prove sharing in complete essence.
C) The void does not exist.
D) The world must be in the center of the void.

Key Takeaway
The Second Premise weakens necessitarianism by showing that similarity in appearance does not guarantee similarity in essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The author dares to ask 'Why do you claim this is the case?' about a deeply held assumption. Is there a belief in your heart about Allah, the world, or yourself that you have accepted without ever questioning its foundation? Will you today take that belief and ask it the same piercing 'why'?
?
If sharing external properties does not prove two things are the same in essence, then what does that say about how you judge others? Are you willing to see people as unique essences rather than categories, starting with someone you have dismissed as 'just like the rest'?
?
The world's occurrence in a particular part of void is not forced by uniformity—it points to a purposeful choice. Your existence here, now, is not arbitrary. How does that change what you will do with the next hour of your life?

Third Premise: Free Agent Can Choose Location by Will
The author presents the Third Premise: free choice as an alternative to necessity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that even if void parts are identical, a free agent could select a location by will, shifting the debate to the nature of divine agency.

Core Idea
Free will can explain particularization without necessity.

The Third Premise grants the opponent's assumption that void parts are identical, but then introduces the possibility of a free agent who chooses by sheer will. This moves the discussion to a deeper question: the distinction between a capable agent (qadir) and a necessitating cause (mujib). The author references an earlier treatment of this topic.

Guided Path
1
Grant the assumption
The premise begins by conceding that parts of the void might be similar in complete essence.
This is a hypothetical concession.
2
Introduce the alternative
Even so, a free agent (al-fa'il al-mukhtar) could particularize the world to a specific part by mere will (al-irada).
Note the emphasis on 'mere will'—no external cause.
3
Link to broader issue
This reverts to the known premise about the difference between a capable agent and a necessitating cause, which the author says has been discussed thoroughly.
This connects to earlier material in the book.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقدمة الثانية : إن الأجزاء المفترضة في هذا الخلاء : متشابهة . فتقول : ولم قلتم : إن الأمر كذلك ؟ ولم لا يجوز أن يقال : بعض أجزاء هذا الخلاء مخالف بالماهية للبعض فلا جرم كان حصول هذا العالم في بعض تلك الأجزاء ، أولى من حصوله في الباقي ؟ وتقريره : أن الخلاء موجود يمكن فرض الأبعاد الثلاثة فيه ، ولا يلزم من اشراك أجزاء الخلاء في قابلية الأبعاد ، اشتراكها في تمام الماهية. لما ثبت أن الاشتراك في الأحكام واللوازم لا يدل على الاشتراك في ماهيات الملزومات

المقدمة الثالثة : هب أن أجزاء الخلاء متشابهة في تمام الماهية. لكن لم لا يجوز أن يقال : الفاعل المختار خصص العالم ببعض أجزاء الخلاء دون البعض ، بمجرد الإرادة ؟ وحينئذ يرجع هذا الكلام إلى البحث عن المقدمة المشهورة المذكورة في مسألة الفرق بين القادر وبين الموجب. وقد تقدم ذلك بالاستقصاء .

المقدمة الرابعة : هب أن القول بالفاعل المختار باطل عندكم إلا أنا نشاهد أن كل واحد من الأجسام الحيوانية والنباتية والمعدنية مختص بصورة معينة وحيز معين ومقدار معين مع أنه لا يمتنع في العقل حصول خلاف ما وقع. فاختصاص كل واحد من هذه الأجسام بصفاته المعينة إما أن يكون لا لمرجح أو المرجح هو الفاعل المختار . أو إن كان ذلك المرجح موجباً بالذات إلا أن كل حادث ، فإنه مسبوق بحادث آخر ، وكان الحادث المتقدم ، أعد المادة لقبول الحادث المتأخر. وعلى هذا الترتيب يكون قبل كل حادث ، حادث آخر لا إلى أول . وبالجملة فإذا عقلنا هذه الاختصاصات في هذه الأجسام ، لأحد هذه الأقسام ، فلم لا يجوز مثله في اختصاص كل العالم الجسماني لموضع معين من الخلاء، الذي لا نهاية له ؟ وذلك أن يقال : إنه حصل في ذلك الحيز المعين، لا لمرجح أو المرجح هو الفاعل المختار أو لأجل أن هذا العالم كان قبل أن حصل في هذا الحيز ، حاصلا في حيز آخر من الخلاء ، وكان حصوله في ذلك الحيز من الخلاء ، أعد له لأن يتنقل منه إلى هذا الحيز الثاني من الخلاء وهكذا لا إلى أول : أن كل ما يقولونه في الأعراض المعينة ، الحاصلة في الأجسام السفلية ، فنحن نقول مثله في اختصاص كلية العالم بالجزء المعين من الخلاء .
The Second Premise: The assumed parts of this void are similar. So we say: Why do you claim that this is the case? And why may it not be said that some parts of this void differ in essence from others, so that necessarily this world's occurrence in some of those parts would be more appropriate than its occurrence in the rest? And its explanation: The void is existent, in which the three dimensions can be supposed, and the sharing of the parts of the void in the capacity for dimensions does not entail their sharing in complete essence. Because it has been established that sharing in properties and concomitants does not indicate sharing in the essences of what bears them.

The Third Premise: Suppose the parts of the void are similar in complete essence. But why may it not be said that the agent by choice (al-fa'il al-mukhtar) particularized the world to some parts of the void rather than others, simply by will (al-irada)? In that case, this discussion reverts to the investigation of the well-known premise mentioned in the issue of the difference between the capable (al-qadir) and the necessitating cause (al-mujib). And that has already been discussed thoroughly.

The Fourth Premise: Suppose the statement of the agent by choice is false according to you, except that we observe that every one of animal, plant, and mineral bodies is particularized with a specific form, a specific location, and a specific quantity, while it is not intellectually impossible for the opposite of what occurred to occur. So the particularization of each of these bodies with its specific attributes is either without a determining factor, or the determining factor is the agent by choice. Or if that determining factor is a necessitating cause by essence, except that every event is preceded by another event, and the preceding event prepared the matter to receive the subsequent event. And according to this arrangement, before every event there is another event without a first. In summary, if we understand these particularizations in these bodies as one of these divisions, then why is the like not permissible in the particularization of the entire corporeal world to a specific place in the infinite void? How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a situation in your life where you cannot find a logical reason for God's decree, do you accept that His will alone is sufficient? Or do you secretly demand an explanation, as if He must be a necessitating cause? This lesson teaches that God's will is enough. Examine one such situation right now and commit to saying, 'I am satisfied with His choice, even if I do not understand it.'
?
The author argues that a free agent's will is a valid explanation without further reason. How often do you, in prayer, try to convince God of what you think is best, instead of trusting that His unchallenged will is already best? Write a short prayer that begins, 'O Fa'il Mukhtar, I surrender my need to understand and accept Your will alone.'
?
God particularized the world to a specific location without any external cause. Are there specific blessings in your life that you attribute to your own effort or chance, rather than to His pure will? Name one blessing today and say, 'This came only because You willed it,' and change how you thank Him.

Fourth Premise: Observable Particularization Applies to the World
The author presents the Fourth Premise: analogy from bodies to the cosmos.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the particularization observed in physical bodies (form, place, size) can be explained by the same options—no cause, free agent, or infinite regress—and this reasoning applies to the entire world's location in the void.

Core Idea
Worldly particularization mirrors cosmic placement.

The Fourth Premise builds on observable facts: every natural body (animal, plant, mineral) has a specific form, place, and size, even though alternatives are logically possible. Three explanatory options are offered—no determining factor, free agent, or infinite regress. The same options are then applied to the entire corporeal world's position in the infinite void.

Guided Path
1
Observe the given
The premise starts with an observation: all natural bodies have specific attributes (form, location, size) that are not logically necessary.
Think of a specific tree or stone.
2
List the three explanations
Three possibilities are given: (1) no determining factor, (2) a free agent, or (3) a necessitating cause with infinite regress of events.
Note the structure: these are exhaustive alternatives.
3
Apply to the world
The same three options are applied to the entire corporeal world's location in the void. The author summarizes by saying: whatever they say about specific accidents, we say the same about the world as a whole.
This is an argument by analogy.

?

?

Answer to Remaining Four Proofs: Plenum, Not Void Impossibility
Author states remaining four proofs only indicate occurrence of plenum, not impossibility of void.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the remaining four proofs for void's impossibility actually only establish the occurrence of plenum, not its necessity or void's impossibility.

Core Idea
Proving plenum exists does not prove void impossible.

The author now addresses the final four proofs that opponents use to argue for the impossibility of void. His answer is concise but crucial: even if these proofs are valid, they only show that plenum (ملاء) occurs in the universe, not that void (خلاء) cannot exist. This distinction prevents an unwarranted leap from observation to necessity.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the short Arabic text once. Notice the phrase 'إنما تفيد هذا' (only benefit this). The 'this' refers to the occurrence of plenum, as the context clarifies.
The passage is very condensed; understand that 'this' points to the existence of plenum.
2
Understand the key distinction
The author argues that the proofs demonstrate the occurrence (حصول) of plenum, but not that plenum is necessary or that void is impossible. This is a logical distinction: showing something is present does not prove its opposite is absent.
This is a common fallacy – confusing 'is' with 'must be'.
3
Reflect on the implication
Ask yourself: if the proofs only show plenum, not void's impossibility, then the opponents have not proven their case. The possibility of void remains open.
This answer defends the possibility of void against these arguments.
4
Test your understanding with the checkpoint
Take the multiple-choice question to confirm you grasped the author's claim.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب عن الوجوه الأربعة الباقية : إن تلك الوجوه ، إنما تفيد هذا .
And the answer to the remaining four proofs: that those proofs only benefit this.
Source pages p. 177

Commentary
This very short passage gives the author's answer to the remaining four proofs. The full answer, inferred from the context and footnotes, is that these proofs, even if valid, only demonstrate the existence of plenum (ملاء) in the universe, not that plenum is necessarily existent or that void (خلاء) is impossible. Thus they do not establish the impossibility of void.

Key Terms
ملاء (plenum)
خلاء (void)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Thinking that proving the existence of something (plenum) automatically proves the impossibility of its absence (void).
Correction: The author points out that the proofs only indicate the occurrence of plenum, not the necessity of plenum or the impossibility of void. Existence of one thing does not negate the possibility of its opposite.

Checkpoint
What does the author say the remaining four proofs indicate?
A) The impossibility of void.
B) The necessity of plenum.
C) The occurrence of plenum.
D) The non-existence of void.

Key Takeaway
The remaining four proofs only show that plenum occurs, not that void is impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever held a belief simply because you had evidence it is true, without checking whether the opposite is impossible? Where in your life might you be confusing existence with necessity?
?
When you hear a convincing argument, do you ask yourself 'what exactly does this prove?' or do you accept it as sufficient? How can you train yourself to distinguish between proving that something is and proving that its opposite cannot be?
?
Consider one of your deepest convictions about faith. Is it based on arguments that show the alternative is impossible, or only that your position is plausible? Are you willing to seek the stronger kind of proof?
